Daily Tops for last 60 days","link":"http://r-posts.com/?p=1881","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://r-posts.com/most-popular-on-netflix-daily-tops-for-last-60-days/\"> R-posts.com</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>Everyday, around 9 pm, I get fresh portion of the Netflix Top movies / TV shows. I’ve been doing this for more than two months and decided to show the first results answering following questions:</p>\n<p>How many movies / TV shows make the Top?<br />\nWhat movie was the longest #1 on Netflix?<br />\nWhat movie was the longest in Tops?<br />\nFor how many days movies / TV shows stay in Tops and as #1?<br />\nTo have a try to plot all this up and down zigzags…</p>\n<p>I took 60 days span (almost all harvest so far) and Top5 overall, not Top 10, in each category to talk about really the most popular and trendy.</p>\n<p>So, let`s count how many movies made the Top5, I mean it is definitely less than 5 *60…</p>\n<pre class='brush: r'>library(tidyverse)\nlibrary (stats)\nlibrary (gt)\nfjune <- read_csv(\"R/movies/fjune.csv\")\n#wrangle raw data - reverse (fresh date first), take top 5, take last 60 days\nfjune_dt % rev () %>% slice (1:5) %>% select (1:60)\n\n#gather it together and count the number of unique titles \nfjune_dt_gathered <- gather (fjune_dt)\ncolnames (fjune_dt_gathered) <- c(\"date\", \"title\")\nunique_fjune_gathered <- unique (fjune_dt_gathered$title)\n\nstr (unique_fjune_gathered)\n#chr [1:123] \"Unknown Origins\" \"The Debt Collector 2\" \"Lucifer\" \"Casino Royale\"</pre>\n<p>OK, it is 123 out of 300. <br />\nNow, it is good to have distribution in each #1 #2 #3 etc.</p>\n<pre class='brush: r'>t_fjune_dt <- as.data.frame (t(fjune_dt),stringsAsFactors = FALSE)\n# list of unique titles in each #1 #2 #3etc. for each day\nunique_fjune <- sapply (t_fjune_dt, unique)\n\n# number of unique titles in each #1 #2 #3 etc.\nn_unique_fjune <- sapply (unique_fjune, length)\nn_unique_fjune <- setNames (n_unique_fjune, c(\"Top1\", \"Top2\", \"Top3\", \"Top4\", \"Top5\"))\n\nn_unique_fjune\nTop1 Top2 Top3 Top4 Top5\n32   45   45   52   49</pre>\n<p>What movie was the longest in Tops / #1?</p>\n<pre class='brush: r'># Top5\ntable_fjune_dt5 <- sort (table (fjune_dt_gathered$title), decreasing = T)\n# Top1\ntable_fjune_dt1 <- sort (table (t_fjune_dt$V1), decreasing = T)\n\n# plotting the results\nbb5 <- barplot (table_fjune_dt5 [1:5], ylim=c(0,22), main = \"Days in Top5\", las = 1, col = 'light blue')\ntext(bb5,table_fjune_dt5 [1:5] +1.2,labels=as.character(table_fjune_dt5 [1:5]))\nbb1 <- barplot (table_fjune_dt1 [1:5], main = \"Days as Top1\", las = 1, ylim=c(0,6), col = 'light green')\ntext(bb1,table_fjune_dt1 [1:5] +0.5, labels=as.character(table_fjune_dt1 [1:5]))</pre>\n<p><img data-attachment-id=\"1883\" 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Numerical Partial Derivative Estimation – the {NNS} package","link":"http://r-posts.com/?p=1890","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://r-posts.com/numerical-partial-derivative-estimation/\"> R-posts.com</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p><span><code><strong>NNS (v0.5.5)</strong></code> now on <a href=\"https://cran.r-project.org/package=NNS\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">CRAN</a> has an updated partial derivative routine <span style=\"color: #0000ff;\"><strong><code>dy.d_()</code></strong></span> . This function estimates true average partial derivatives, as well as ceteris paribus conditions for points of interest.</p>\n<p>Example below on the syntax for estimating first derivatives of the function <code><strong>y = x_1^2 * x_2^2</strong></code> , for the points <code><strong>x_1 = 0.5</strong></code> and <strong><code>x_2 = 0.5</code></strong>, and for both regressors <strong><code>x_1</code></strong> and <code><strong>x_2</strong></code>.</p>\n<p></span><code><span style=\"color: #ff0000;\"><strong><span style=\"font-size: 12pt;\">set.seed(123)</span></strong></span></code><br />\n<strong><span style=\"color: #ff0000; font-size: 12pt;\"><code>x_1 = runif(1000)</code></span></strong><br />\n<strong><span style=\"color: #ff0000; font-size: 12pt;\"><code>x_2 = runif(1000)</code></span></strong><br />\n<strong><span style=\"color: #ff0000; font-size: 12pt;\"><code>y = x_1 ^ 2 * x_2 ^ 2<br />\ndy.d_(cbind(x_1, x_2), y, wrt = 1:2, eval.points = t(c(.5,.5)))[\"First\",]<br />\n<span style=\"color: #0000ff;\">[[1]]</span><br />\n<span style=\"color: #0000ff;\">[1] 0.2454744</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"color: #0000ff;\">[[2]]</span><br />\n<span style=\"color: #0000ff;\">[1] 0.2439307</span></code></span></strong></p>\n<p>The analytical solution for both regressors at <strong><code>x_1 = x_2 = 0.5</code></strong> is 0.25.</p>\n<p>The referenced paper gives many more examples, comparing <span style=\"color: #0000ff;\"><strong><code>dy.d_()</code></strong></span> to kernel regression gradients and OLS coefficients.</p>\n<p>For even more <code><strong>NNS</strong></code> capabilities, check out the examples at GitHub:</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://github.com/OVVO-Financial/NNS/blob/NNS-Beta-Version/examples/index.md\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://github.com/OVVO-Financial/NNS/blob/NNS-Beta-Version/examples/index.md</a></p>\n<p>\n<span style=\"font-size: 14pt;\"><strong>Reference Paper:</strong></span><br />\n<span>Vinod, Hrishikesh D. and Viole, Fred, <em>Comparing Old and New Partial Derivative Estimates from Nonlinear Nonparametric Regressions </em><br />\n<a href=\"https://ssrn.com/abstract=3681104\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://ssrn.com/abstract=3681104</a> </p>\n<p><strong><span style=\"font-size: 14pt;\">Supplemental Materials:</span></strong><br />\n<a href=\"https://ssrn.com/abstract=3681436\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://ssrn.com/abstract=3681436</a></p>\n<p>\n</span></p>\n<hr style=\"border-top:black solid 1px\" /><a href=\"https://r-posts.com/numerical-partial-derivative-estimation/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Numerical Partial Derivative Estimation – the {NNS} package</a> was first posted on September 3, 2020 at 5:20 pm.<br />©2020 \"<a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R-posts.com</a>\". package</a> first appeared on <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" target=\"_blank\">R-bloggers</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/L-knLldO9Gw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 03 Sep 2020 11:20:52 +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"b7ebc2d7c4e4b1fffc3087127258344e","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Fred Viole","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/L-knLldO9Gw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn21@2020_6/2020/09/05/06-27-12-062_5fbfac8b02fdea52.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287180039},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Partial dependence plots for tidymodels-based xgboost","link":"http://aliarsalankazmi.github.io/blog_DA/posts/r/2020/09/03/pdp-xgboost-tidymodels.html","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"http://aliarsalankazmi.github.io/blog_DA/posts/r/2020/09/03/pdp-xgboost-tidymodels.html\"> A Blog on Data Analytics</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<h3 id=\"problem\">Problem!</h3>\n<p>ML algorithms, although their <a href=\"https://projecteuclid.org/euclid.ss/1149600839\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">proclaimed effectiveness</a> on predictive accuracy might be suspect, are often the first tool that a data scientist resorts to when tackling a prediction problem. Such an attitude is not the best in my opinion, and I generally favour a statistical/probabilistic model over a black box<sup id=\"fnref:1\" role=\"doc-noteref\"><a href=\"http://aliarsalankazmi.github.io/blog_DA/posts/r/2020/09/03/pdp-xgboost-tidymodels.html#fn:1\" class=\"footnote\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">1</a></sup>. Nevertheless, there are situations when one is asked (read: told) to use the latter.</p>\n<h3 id=\"solution\">Solution?</h3>\n<p>Then, typically <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">xgboost</code> is an ML model resorted to quite often, due to its good performance straight out-of-the-box. Once such a model has been trained and tested, the analyst is faced with the challenging task of explaining what the model is doing under the hood.</p>\n<h3 id=\"problem-with-our-solution\">Problem with our solution!</h3>\n<p>To be able to explain our model — which is often more complex than to be easily explained — we tend to use techniques such as feature importance via permutation, partial dependence, and <a href=\"https://christophm.github.io/interpretable-ml-book/ale.html#ale\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">others</a>. Notwithstanding that the 2 aforesaid techniques make an assumption of feature independence that may be unrealistic (at which point, they are not different than a vanilla regression model), such visualisations are helpful as a first attempt at model interpretation.</p>\n<p>While working on a project, I found that some tweaks were required to be able to use the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">pdp</code> package for partial dependence plots with an <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">xgboost</code> model built from <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">tidymodels</code>. Let’s try this with code that Julia Silge used in her modelling, just to quickly show the procedure.</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code># Read in the data from #tidytuesday repo\n\nvb_matches <- readr::read_csv('https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday/master/data/2020/2020-05-19/vb_matches.csv', guess_max = 76000)\n\n\n# Generate a derived data set for training\n\nvb_parsed <- vb_matches %>%\n  transmute(\n    circuit,\n    gender,\n    year,\n    w_attacks = w_p1_tot_attacks + w_p2_tot_attacks,\n    w_kills = w_p1_tot_kills + w_p2_tot_kills,\n    w_errors = w_p1_tot_errors + w_p2_tot_errors,\n    w_aces = w_p1_tot_aces + w_p2_tot_aces,\n    w_serve_errors = w_p1_tot_serve_errors + w_p2_tot_serve_errors,\n    w_blocks = w_p1_tot_blocks + w_p2_tot_blocks,\n    w_digs = w_p1_tot_digs + w_p2_tot_digs,\n    l_attacks = l_p1_tot_attacks + l_p2_tot_attacks,\n    l_kills = l_p1_tot_kills + l_p2_tot_kills,\n    l_errors = l_p1_tot_errors + l_p2_tot_errors,\n    l_aces = l_p1_tot_aces + l_p2_tot_aces,\n    l_serve_errors = l_p1_tot_serve_errors + l_p2_tot_serve_errors,\n    l_blocks = l_p1_tot_blocks + l_p2_tot_blocks,\n    l_digs = l_p1_tot_digs + l_p2_tot_digs\n  ) %>%\n  na.omit()\n\nwinners <- vb_parsed %>%\n  select(circuit, gender, year,\n         w_attacks:w_digs) %>%\n  rename_with(~ str_remove_all(., \"w_\"), w_attacks:w_digs) %>%\n  mutate(win = \"win\")\n\nlosers <- vb_parsed %>%\n  select(circuit, gender, year,\n         l_attacks:l_digs) %>%\n  rename_with(~ str_remove_all(., \"l_\"), l_attacks:l_digs) %>%\n  mutate(win = \"lose\")\n\nvb_df <- bind_rows(winners, losers) %>%\n  mutate_if(is.character, factor)\n  \n\n# Split data for training, testing (even though we only really need the training for our purpose)\n\nlibrary(tidymodels)\n\nset.seed(123)\nvb_split <- initial_split(vb_df, strata = win)\nvb_train <- training(vb_split)\nvb_test <- testing(vb_split)\n\n\n# Fitting our xgboost model\n\nxgb_spec <- boost_tree(\n  trees = 1000,\n  tree_depth = 4,\n  min_n = 25,\n  loss_reduction = 0.0011,\n  sample_size = 0.977,\n  mtry = 5,\n  learn_rate = 0.0195,\n) %>%\n  set_engine(\"xgboost\") %>%\n  set_mode(\"classification\") %>% \n  fit(win ~ ., data = vb_train)\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>So far, so good. Now, we want to obtain partial dependence plots. The <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">partial</code> function from <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">pdp</code> expects an <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">xgb.Booster</code> object, along with the training data used in modelling.</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>pdp::partial(\n  xgb_spec$fit,\n  pred.var = \"kills\",\n  ice = T,\n  center = T,\n  plot = T,\n  alpha = .1,\n  plot.engine = \"ggplot2\",\n  train = vb_train %>% select(-win)\n)\n#> Error in predict.xgb.Booster(object, newdata = newdata, reshape = TRUE,  : Feature names stored in `object` and `newdata` are different!\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Hmm…How are the names different?</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>xgb_spec$fit$feature_names\n#> [1] \"circuitAVP\"   \"circuitFIVB\"  \"genderM\"      \"genderW\"      \"year\"         \"attacks\"     \n#> [7] \"kills\"        \"errors\"       \"aces\"         \"serve_errors\" \"blocks\"       \"digs\"\n\nnames(vb_train %>% select(-win))\n#> [1] \"circuit\"      \"gender\"       \"year\"         \"attacks\"      \"kills\"        \"errors\"      \n#> [7] \"aces\"         \"serve_errors\" \"blocks\"       \"digs\"  \n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Well, this is understandable, because:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>xgboost expects all categorical variables to be one-hot encoded</li>\n<li>tidymodels rather helpfully does this automatically for us during fitting</li>\n<li>since vb_train is left as is/incompatible with how xgboost sees it, the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">partial</code> function exits with an error</li>\n</ol>\n<p>We can try adding a dummy coding step in our recipe…</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>vb_recipe <- recipe(win ~., data = vb_train) %>% \n  step_dummy(all_nominal(),-all_outcomes(), one_hot = T) %>%\n  prep()\nvb_train_2 <- juice(vb_recipe)\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Are the names identical now?</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>vb_train_2 %>% select(-win) %>% names()\n#> [1] \"year\"         \"attacks\"      \"kills\"        \"errors\"       \"aces\"         \"serve_errors\"\n#> [7] \"blocks\"       \"digs\"         \"circuit_AVP\"  \"circuit_FIVB\" \"gender_M\"     \"gender_W\" \n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Oops. Seems like the only difference now is in the underscore that separates a categorical variable from its value in our train dataset, while the model does not have any underscore separation.</p>\n<p>Luckily, without much changes, we can get this sorted: the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">step_dummy</code> function has an argument <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">naming</code>, which uses a function <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">dummy_names</code>. This function can be used to indicate whether an underscore or any other separator is to be added for one-hot encoded variables.</p>\n<p>Below, I will create another version of this function, without any separator, and pass it to the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">step_dummy</code> function.</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>\n# Creating another version of the dummy names function\n\ndummy_names_2 <- function (var, lvl, ordinal = FALSE, sep = \"\") \n{\n    if (!ordinal) \n        nms <- paste(var, make.names(lvl), sep = sep)\n    else nms <- paste0(var, names0(length(lvl), sep))\n    nms\n}\n\n\n# Testing it now\n\nvb_recipe <- recipe(win ~., data = vb_train) %>% \n  step_dummy(all_nominal(),-all_outcomes(), one_hot = T, naming = dummy_names_2) %>%\n  prep()\nvb_train_2 <- juice(vb_recipe)\n\nall(vb_train_2 %>% select(-win) %>% names() %in% xgb_spec$fit$feature_names)\n#>[1] TRUE\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Perfect! Can we generate a partial dependence plot now?</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>pdp::partial(\n  xgb_spec$fit,\n  pred.var = \"kills\",\n  ice = T,\n  center = T,\n  plot = T,\n  alpha = .1,\n  plot.engine = \"ggplot2\",\n  train = vb_train_2 %>% select(-win)\n)\n\n#> Error in predict.xgb.Booster(object, newdata = newdata, reshape = TRUE,: Feature names stored in `object` and `newdata` are different!\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>This was strange, so I had to do a bit of debugging with <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">traceback()</code> to find out why this was happening. The problem seemed to be occurring at the following function within <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">partial</code>.</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>\nobject <- xgb.Booster.complete(object, saveraw = FALSE)\nif (!inherits(newdata, \"xgb.DMatrix\")) \n  newdata <- xgb.DMatrix(newdata, missing = missing)\nif (!is.null(object[[\"feature_names\"]]) && !is.null(colnames(newdata)) && \n    !identical(object[[\"feature_names\"]], colnames(newdata)))            # Herein lies the problem!\n  stop(\"Feature names stored in `object` and `newdata` are different!\")\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>It seems like <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">identical()</code> expects character vectors to follow the same order too, in addition to just being the same vectors:</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>a1 <- letters[1:5]\na1\n#> [1] \"a\" \"b\" \"c\" \"d\" \"e\"\n\na2 <- letters[sample(x = 1:5, size = 5)]\na2\n#> [1] \"c\" \"a\" \"d\" \"b\" \"e\"\n\nidentical(a1, a2)\n#> FALSE\n\na3 <- a2[order(match(a2, a1))]\na3\n#> [1] \"a\" \"b\" \"c\" \"d\" \"e\"\n\nidentical(a1, a3)\n#> TRUE\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>So if we just rearrange our columns in training data in accordance with feature names of xgboost model, it should work…</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>\ndata.table::setcolorder\n\npdp::partial(\n  xgb_spec$fit,\n  pred.var = \"kills\",\n  ice = T,\n  center = T,\n  plot = T,\n  alpha = .1,\n  plot.engine = \"ggplot2\",\n  train = vb_train_2 %>% \n  select(-win) %>% \n  select(match(xgb_spec$fit$feature_names, vb_train_2 %>% select(-win) %>% names()))\n)\n\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><a href=\"https://i1.wp.com/aliarsalankazmi.github.io/blog_DA/assets/img/pdp_xgboost_tidymodels_1.jpeg\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/aliarsalankazmi.github.io/blog_DA/assets/img/pdp_xgboost_tidymodels_1.jpeg?w=578\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>Finally!</p>\n<div class=\"footnotes\" role=\"doc-endnotes\">\n<ol>\n<li id=\"fn:1\" role=\"doc-endnote\">\n<p>If you are interested in weighing which option to choose — ML or Statistical model, here is a <a href=\"https://www.fharrell.com/post/stat-ml/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">great resource</a> that presents a roadmap view to select the type of modelling adequate for your problem. <a href=\"http://aliarsalankazmi.github.io/blog_DA/posts/r/2020/09/03/pdp-xgboost-tidymodels.html#fnref:1\" class=\"reversefootnote\" role=\"doc-backlink\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/21a9.png\" alt=\"↩\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></a></p>\n</li>\n</ol>\n</div>\n\n<script 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Why do I have a data science blog? 7 benefits of sharing your code","link":"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/\"> R on Stats and R</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<div id=\"TOC\">\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#learn-by-writing\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#1 Learn by writing</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#get-feedback\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#2 Get feedback</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#personal-note-to-remind-my-future-self\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#3 Personal note to remind my future self</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#contribute-to-the-open-source-community\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#4 Contribute to the open source community</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#stay-humble-stay-curious\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#5 Stay humble, stay curious</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#learn-to-be-less-perfectionist-and-to-prioritize\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#6 Learn to be less perfectionist and to prioritize</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#build-connections-and-professional-relationships\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#7 Build connections and professional relationships</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#how-to-start-your-own-blog\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">How to start your own blog?</a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.statsandr.com/blog/2020-09-02-why-do-i-blog-5-benefits-of-sharing-your-code_files/why-do-i-blog-5-benefits-of-sharing-your-code.jpeg?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" style=\"width:100.0%\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>My blog <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">statsandr.com</a> was launched in December 2019. Although 9 months of writing is a very short period compared to others, I can already say that it’s been an incredible and very enriching adventure!</p>\n<p>With <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">45 articles</a> published and topics ranging from <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/descriptive-statistics-in-r/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">descriptive statistics</a>, <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/the-9-concepts-and-formulas-in-probability-that-every-data-scientist-should-know/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">probability</a>, <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/student-s-t-test-in-r-and-by-hand-how-to-compare-two-groups-under-different-scenarios/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">inferential statistics</a> to <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/tags/r-markdown/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R Markdown</a> and <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/graphics-in-r-with-ggplot2/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">data visualization</a>, I have seen many benefits of sharing my code through a technical blog. In this article, I highlight 7 of them (in no particular order) with the hope that it will give ideas and incentives to some of you.</p>\n<p>In the end of this article, I also mention a couple of modern solutions needed for starting your own blog.</p>\n<p>Note that in these 9 months, I did not make a living from my blog and this is not my goal as I would need to make it a priority. However, I have received enough positive feedback from readers, and more importantly, I have learned enough to continue writing.</p>\n<div id=\"learn-by-writing\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#1 Learn by writing</h1>\n<p>I really enjoy learning new stuff in many different domains. I learn (and I am still learning) a lot by teaching statistics to students from diverse backgrounds as part of my <a href=\"https://www.antoinesoetewey.com/teaching/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">teaching assistant</a> position at university.</p>\n<p>Before launching this blog, I believed that I understood a statistical concept as soon as I was able to <em>teach</em> it to my students. If I was not able to explain it in a clear and understandable way, it meant that I needed to study it more thoroughly because I actually did not fully understand it.</p>\n<p>This is often referred as the Feynman technique. This method of learning is based on the fact that in order to fully master a topic you need to be able to explain it back to someone in simple terms.</p>\n<p>Throughout this blog, I actually realized that in order to learn and <strong>fully understand something new</strong>, one must:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>be able to clearly communicate it and teach it in simple terms,</li>\n<li>but <em>also</em> be able to <strong>write it down in a precise and concise manner</strong></li>\n</ul>\n<p>So although this blog was first launched to share statistical concepts I am most familiar with (hoping that it would be useful to some people), I now also use it to <strong>learn by writing</strong>. I think that this additional way of learning is actually <strong>as powerful as teaching</strong> because writing allows me to <strong>consolidate my understanding</strong>.</p>\n<p>Obviously, I am learning mainly about <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/tags/statistics/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">statistics</a> and its applications in <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/tags/r/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R</a> as they are the main topics of the blog. However, I never thought that I would also learn so much about:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>web development and SEO/analytics (which are increasingly important skills nowadays)</li>\n<li>project management (as you build something from scratch and wish to develop it)</li>\n<li>writing (a skill I still need to improve as a non-native English speaker)<a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#fn1\" class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref1\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><sup>1</sup></a></li>\n<li>communicating results (as any data scientist will tell you, results without proper communication are useless and writing a blog is a great practice)</li>\n<li>marketing/public relation/brand management (think about social networking and how to deal with all sorts of questions from readers)</li>\n<li>etc.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Maintaining a blog teaches me essential skills that are usually taught in full-time jobs. With a blog, you are responsible of everything from the content to readers’ inquiries, similar to an employee in charge of a project and who has to deal and communicate with end users.</p>\n<p>Don’t get me wrong, apart from exceptions, I do not think that a blog can completely replace a job in terms of personal development, but it definitely helps to learn a broad range of important skills. Reading books and completing data science online courses are other examples to gain skills in addition to a job, but a blog tends to be more diverse and applied, making it more beneficial (in my opinion).</p>\n<p>Another way I am learning is by <strong>doing research about the topic I am writing about</strong>.</p>\n<p>An example of it is my post on <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/outliers-detection-in-r/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">outliers detection in R</a>. As part of any <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/descriptive-statistics-in-r/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">descriptive analysis</a>, I am used to check for potential outliers. Since I was familiar with this topic, I decided to write about it. However, I found the post not complete enough so I did some further research. It turned out that there were in fact several statistical tests that I did not know about. I wrote about these tests and I now include these new techniques whenever I check for potential outliers.</p>\n<p>Last but not least, I often receive emails from readers asking to write about a topic of their choice. It sometimes happens that I never heard about their suggested topic, so I do some research out of curiosity. Even if I still do not write about it because I am not familiar enough with it, at least I am aware that it exists and I know more or less what this is about.</p>\n<p>If the sole benefit of learning did not convince you to start your blog, see other benefits I have experienced in the following sections.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"get-feedback\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#2 Get feedback</h1>\n<p><strong>Get feedback</strong>, suggestions and constructive critics <strong>from more experienced users</strong>. Remarks greatly help in correcting typos and bugs present in my code, and they also help in improving my R skills.</p>\n<p>You will be amazed to see that, although you spent countless hours to check your code, there will always be someone who will spot a typo that you missed. Valuable feedback from people all over the world has, for instance, definitely improved the quality and completeness of my <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/tags/shiny/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R Shiny applications</a> that I use on a daily basis.</p>\n<p>A blog can therefore be seen as a <strong>powerful peer-review method</strong> of your understanding of a concept, code or R practices. It is also better to make mistakes when working on a toy example and correct them, than to make mistakes on a real project at your workplace.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"personal-note-to-remind-my-future-self\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#3 Personal note to remind my future self</h1>\n<p>How many times you searched for a piece of code in the folders of your computer, to finally look for the solution on Google because you could not remember for which project you wrote that piece of code? It happened to me every day.</p>\n<p>With blog posts organized by <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/tags/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">topics</a>, it now takes me much less time (and less frustration!) to <strong>find code snippets that I wrote several months ago</strong>. This also allows me to keep my <strong>code and R practices up-to-date</strong>, as I only have to edit them in one place.</p>\n<p>An example of this is my article about <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/graphics-in-r-with-ggplot2/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">graphics in R with <code>{ggplot2}</code></a>. I prefer plots with the <code>{ggplot2}</code> package over plots available by default in R base, but I cannot remember all layers and their arguments. Now every time I struggle with a plot using this package, I simply revisit the corresponding article to find the solution. Same goes for many of my articles, every time I have forgotten the code or a nuance around how it works.</p>\n<p>You could store your code in different files (<a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/getting-started-in-r-markdown/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R Markdown</a> documents or R scripts for example, as I used to do in the past), but blog posts have the advantages that:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>code snippets are highlighted, and</li>\n<li>by making it available to the world, you are forced to keep it tidy, complete and up-to-date.</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<div id=\"contribute-to-the-open-source-community\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#4 Contribute to the open source community</h1>\n<p>I have learned so much and I keep learning everyday about R thanks to great resources made available for free by developers and scientists who believe in <strong>open source and free materials</strong>.</p>\n<p>Making all my code and articles freely available through a blog is in some sense, my way of:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>“paying back” people who helped me to learn and thanks to whom I stand where I am now, and</li>\n<li>pay in advance the many more from who I will learn in the future.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>If you also believe in sharing your knowledge or expertise, having a blog is definitely a great way to <strong>contribute to the community</strong>. Your contribution does not necessarily have to be huge, as long as it adds something, someone will use it. Remember that everyone started as a beginner, and even world experts are beginners in other domains. And you will see that as you keep sharing your knowledge with others, some people will appreciate it because they can learn from it.</p>\n<p>As far as I am concerned, if my small contribution is useful for some people in having a better understanding of statistics or in learning R, my goal will be reached.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"stay-humble-stay-curious\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#5 Stay humble, stay curious</h1>\n<p>Since launching my blog, I discovered plenty of other data science blogs of high quality. The more I see new things about different topics and the more I see people doing incredible stuff, the more I realize that I actually do not know much. This reminds me to <strong>stay humble</strong>.</p>\n<p>In addition to that, by sharing my code with scientists from diverse backgrounds and coming from all over the world, it allows me to <strong>consider other perspectives</strong> and practice open-mindedness, which in turn helps me to <strong>stay curious</strong>.</p>\n<p>Being humble and curious is, in my opinion, a good starting point to keep learning. I would not want to lose my curiosity, otherwise I may lose my appetite for learning.</p>\n<p>For your information, <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R-bloggers</a> and <a href=\"https://rweekly.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R Weekly</a> are two great blog aggregators focusing on R, and the <a href=\"https://towardsdatascience.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Towards Data Science</a> publication on Medium is full of high quality blog posts covering many topics related to data science. Subscribe to these if you are interested in discovering more technical blogs.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"learn-to-be-less-perfectionist-and-to-prioritize\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#6 Learn to be less perfectionist and to prioritize</h1>\n<p>No matter how much time you spend on your blog, remember that <strong>everything cannot always be perfect</strong>. As a perfectionist, I cannot deny that I would love everything to run perfectly in all aspects of my life.</p>\n<p>At the time of writing my first articles, my tendency for perfectionism was sometimes so prevalent that it was a real weakness: I could spend several minutes thinking whether a comma between two words was needed or not (which would of course make no difference at all). However, at some point, <strong>improving something takes so much time and energy that the added value is not as large as the added value you could create by devoting your time in producing something new</strong>.</p>\n<p>This gets even worse as you keep learning every day. As a consequence, an article which seemed to be perfect in the past may no longer be viewed as perfect today, so you want to change or add a small detail in all your previous articles. I am not saying you should not edit a blog post, it is even recommended! Nonetheless, I advise you to edit it only if it really adds a significant value to it. Otherwise, I believe it is best to spend that time and effort in creating something else.</p>\n<p>With a blog you will thus gradually learn to put your effort and time (which are limited resources and seem to be scarcer with the years) where it is most productive. In other words, you will learn to <strong>prioritize</strong>.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"build-connections-and-professional-relationships\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>#7 Build connections and professional relationships</h1>\n<p>By sharing your practices in a specific field, you sometimes come across people who actually work on the same topic than you or have similar research interests. This was the case, among others, with:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>My <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/top-r-resources-on-covid-19-coronavirus/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">collection of top R resources on Coronavirus</a>. It started with only 5 resources, then as I was gradually adding more and more resources, Rees Morrison who was collecting blog posts on the same topic contacted me and I added his blog posts to the original collection.</li>\n<li>The presentation of a R package developed by Renan Xavier Cortes which could be used to <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/a-package-to-download-free-springer-books-during-covid-19-quarantine/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">download free Springer books during Covid-19 quarantine</a>.</li>\n<li>My article on the analysis of the spread of <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/covid-19-in-belgium/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">COVID-19 in Belgium</a>, which led, in collaboration with three other researchers, to a <a href=\"https://www.antoinesoetewey.com/files/slides-how-can-we-predict-the-evolution-of-covid-19-in-Belgium.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">seminar</a> and the creation of graphs representing <a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/covid-19-in-belgium-is-it-over-yet/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">COVID-19 hospital admissions in Belgium</a> (which were later used by a Belgian news television channel).</li>\n</ul>\n<p>These <strong>collaborations extended my professional network</strong> and allowed me to build connections with new researchers from different universities and from different countries. I would never have known these people without first sharing my analyses on a specific topic.</p>\n<p>I am sure that a blog can lead to numerous collaborations, making the whole journey even more interesting and enriching. And who knows, the next chapter of your professional life may be shaped by one person with whom you have worked in the past.</p>\n<p>Even if your blog does not lead to any collaboration, it is still a <strong>great tool for self-promotion</strong>. Doing some research, practicing, and writing about a topic help to become (and to be seen as) an <strong>expert</strong> in the field. Moreover, your blog will become your <strong>portfolio</strong> when applying for jobs or projects. Recruiters will definitely appreciate to see what you are capable of.</p>\n<p>Whether your blog is at the origin of some fruitful collaborations or a showcase of your work, it is a <strong>valuable asset</strong> when applying for your dream job.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"how-to-start-your-own-blog\" class=\"section level1\">\n<h1>How to start your own blog?</h1>\n<p>I have seen an acceleration in my learning curve, a deeper understanding of statistics and R, and a large improvement in my communication skills since starting my blog. I never realized how useful maintaining a blog could be as a learning technique. This comes with the added benefit of being able to store information and code for myself and for others to also use, which is essential for getting feedback and connecting with other researchers.</p>\n<p>If these 7 benefits have convinced you, the good news is that nowadays it is cheaper and easier than ever to start your own blog.</p>\n<p>For non-technical blog I recommend Medium or WordPress. For <strong>technical blogs</strong>, there are plenty of <strong>static site generators</strong> to choose from but I highly recommend using <a href=\"https://gohugo.io/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Hugo</a> and the <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>{blogdown}</code> package</a> (I created my blog after reading this book). You can then host it on <a href=\"https://github.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">GitHub</a> then publish it using <a href=\"https://www.netlify.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Netlify</a>.<a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#fn2\" class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref2\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><sup>2</sup></a></p>\n<p>Thanks for reading. I hope this article answered questions such as “Why do you have a blog?” or “What is the purpose of it?”, and who knows, gave you the motivation to start your own blog. If you are still undecided, I truly recommend to do it and remember that the hardest part is to start. If you are patient and passionate about a topic, you simply have to start and the rest will follow.</p>\n<p>As always, if you have a question or a suggestion related to the topic covered in this article, please add it as a comment so other readers can benefit from the discussion.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"footnotes\">\n<hr />\n<ol>\n<li id=\"fn1\">\n<p>Even native English speakers can improve their writing skills with a blog.<a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#fnref1\" class=\"footnote-back\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/21a9.png\" alt=\"↩\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" />︎</a></p>\n</li>\n<li id=\"fn2\">\n<p>Note that I am not a programmer nor a computer scientist so I do not have extensive knowledge in creating websites. There must be many more options available but I find these options the most optimal choices given my computer skills and my goals.<a href=\"https://www.statsandr.com/blog/7-benefits-of-sharing-your-code-in-a-data-science-blog/#fnref2\" class=\"footnote-back\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n<p>And if you enjoy the post be sure to share it</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/share?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" class=\"twitter-share-button\" data-show-count=\"false\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Tweet</a><br />\n<script async src=\"https://platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script></p>\n<div id=\"new-series-exploring-r-packages\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>New Series: Exploring R {packages}</h3>\n<p>When I discover new and helpful <code>functions</code> I light up <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f4a1.png\" alt=\"💡\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> with excitement.</p>\n<p>Interestingly, I have a pattern of finding one or two useful <code>functions</code> in a <code>{package}</code>, but rarely explore further to discover other useful <code>functionality</code>.</p>\n<p>That’s what this New Series is all about – <code>Exploration</code>.</p>\n<p>In each post, I will share a bit about how I was using a <code>{package}</code> and then use a case-study to highlight other <code>functionality</code> I discovered to be useful.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"leave-a-comment\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Leave a Comment</h3>\n<p>Leave a comment at the end to let me know if you like this style of post. The feedback will be considered in determining the direction of this series.</p>\n<div id=\"examples-of-feedback\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Examples of feedback:</h4>\n<ol style=\"list-style-type: decimal\">\n<li>Was the post to long?</li>\n<li>Do you like the case-study approach?</li>\n<li>Could I have just shared the functions without the case-study?</li>\n<li>Was sharing about the pre-processing pro-tip helpful or distracting?</li>\n</ol>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div id=\"janitor-package\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>janitor {package}</h3>\n<p>This a wonderful <code>{package}</code> built by <a href=\"https://github.com/sfirke\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Sam Firke</a>.</p>\n<p>Take the time to explore the <a href=\"https://github.com/sfirke/janitor\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Github Page</a> for the <code>{janitor:package}</code>, where Sam describes it as follow:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>janitor has simple functions for examining and cleaning dirty data. It was built with beginning and intermediate R users in mind and is optimized for user-friendliness. Advanced R users can already do everything covered here, but with janitor they can do it faster and save their thinking for the fun stuff.</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>There are many more functions (20+) in the package that we will not cover – head over to the following page to learn more about them: <a href=\"http://sfirke.github.io/janitor/articles/janitor.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Overview of janitor functions.</a></p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"case-study\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Case-Study</h3>\n<p>The case-study will provide and illustrate the following:</p>\n<ol style=\"list-style-type: decimal\">\n<li>A pro-tip for setting up a pre-processing data pipepline.</li>\n<li>The <code>function</code> I use often: <code>clean_names()</code>.</li>\n<li>Newly discovered <code>functions</code> from <code>{janitor}</code>.</li>\n</ol>\n</div>\n<div id=\"lets-dive-in\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Let’s dive in…</h3>\n<p>Imagine being tasked with doing an analysis on Starbucks coffee locations. Your manager has provided you with <code>raw-data</code> from coffee chains and requested that you:</p>\n<ol style=\"list-style-type: decimal\">\n<li>QA the data for duplicates (by store and by location).</li>\n<li>Tabulate the various types of Starbucks Ownership:\n<ul>\n<li>Worldwide &</li>\n<li>US (lower 48)</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li>Deliver a US map that identifies patterns in ownership types.</li>\n</ol>\n<p>To streamline your efforts and get swiftly to making that map, you decide to leverage the <code>{janitor:package}</code>.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"load-our-libraries\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Load our Libraries</h3>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>library(tidyverse)     # Work-Horse Package\nlibrary(janitor)       # Data cleaning (+tabulating data)\nlibrary(janitor)       # Business Ready Plots\nlibrary(ggthemes)      # Clean ggplot theme for Maps\nlibrary(USAboundaries) # Get state name/code mapping</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"lets-get-some-data\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Let’s Get Some Data</h3>\n<p>For our case-study we are using data from the <a href=\"https://github.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Tidy Tuesday Project</a> archive.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Import Data ----\n# tuesdata <- tidytuesdayR::tt_load(\"2018-05-07\") </code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"pro-tip-pre-processing-pipeline\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Pro-Tip: Pre-Processing Pipeline</h3>\n<p>When working with new data, I’ll typically setup up a pre-processing step at the beginning of the script. It typically starts out with no steps and then they get added as I move through my analysis.</p>\n<p>The idea is that as you conduct your Exploratory Data Analysis <code>(EDA)</code>, you will discover pre-processing steps that need to be added to your pipeline.</p>\n<p>In this post, I’ll illustrate this technique by adding to our pipeline as we go; however, this data pipeline would live near the top of the script and would not move.</p>\n<div id=\"step-1\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Step 1</h4>\n<p>Save <code>raw</code> data to a variable.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># coffee_chains_raw <- tuesdata$week6_coffee_chains</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"step-2\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Step 2</h4>\n<p>Immediately save the <code>raw</code> data to new variable labeled with the suffix, <code>processed</code>.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Beginning of Pre-Processing Pipeline\ncoffee_chains_processed <- coffee_chains_raw</code></pre>\n<p>This obviously has ZERO pre-processing done to the data at this point. The point though is that as you discover areas of your data that require attention, you then can circle back to this pipeline and add those steps.</p>\n<p>This may seem odd, but the beauty comes in not having to get further along in your analysis before realizing that you need to do data cleaning steps; if you approach it that way, then you have to go back and rename your variables created along the way – this method allows you to keep working with your <code>processed</code> data as you move swiftly through your analysis.</p>\n<p>I picked up this <code>pro-tip</code> while watching <code>David Robinson</code> in his <code>Tidy Tuesday Screencasts</code> – check those out here: <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list=PL19ev-r1GBwkuyiwnxoHTRC8TTqP8OEi8\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Tidy Tuesday R Screencasts</a></p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Hat-Tip to D-Rob</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"step-3\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Step 3</h4>\n<p>Begin <code>Exploring</code> your <code>Data</code> and conducting your analysis.</p>\n<p>At this point, I’ll do a bit of <code>EDA</code> to familiarize myself with the data I’m working with; this process is always to get a high-level understanding of the data so that I can pick up on nuances along with data integrity issues that need attention (dealt with in the pre-processing pipeline).</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div id=\"initial-exploration\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Initial Exploration</h3>\n<p>Let’s look at these <code>raw</code> data using the <code>tibble::glimpse()</code> function.</p>\n<p>The <code>glimpse()</code> function allows us to quickly assess column names, data-types, and also view a sample of the values contained in each column – you can read more about the <code>glimpse()</code> function in my archived post, <a href=\"https://www.exploringdata.org/post/examining-data-with-glimpse/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Examining Data with glimpse()</a>.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>coffee_chains_processed %>% \n  tibble::glimpse()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## Rows: 25,600\n## Columns: 13\n## $ Brand            <chr> \"Starbucks\", \"Starbucks\", \"Starbucks\", \"Starbucks\", …\n## $ `Store Number`   <chr> \"47370-257954\", \"22331-212325\", \"47089-256771\", \"221…\n## $ `Store Name`     <chr> \"Meritxell, 96\", \"Ajman Drive Thru\", \"Dana Mall\", \"T…\n## $ `Ownership Type` <chr> \"Licensed\", \"Licensed\", \"Licensed\", \"Licensed\", \"Lic…\n## $ `Street Address` <chr> \"Av. Meritxell, 96\", \"1 Street 69, Al Jarf\", \"Sheikh…\n## $ City             <chr> \"Andorra la Vella\", \"Ajman\", \"Ajman\", \"Abu Dhabi\", \"…\n## $ `State/Province` <chr> \"7\", \"AJ\", \"AJ\", \"AZ\", \"AZ\", \"AZ\", \"AZ\", \"AZ\", \"AZ\",…\n## $ Country          <chr> \"AD\", \"AE\", \"AE\", \"AE\", \"AE\", \"AE\", \"AE\", \"AE\", \"AE\"…\n## $ Postcode         <chr> \"AD500\", NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, \"31…\n## $ `Phone Number`   <chr> \"376818720\", NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, NA, \"26670052\",…\n## $ Timezone         <chr> \"GMT+1:00 Europe/Andorra\", \"GMT+04:00 Asia/Dubai\", \"…\n## $ Longitude        <dbl> 1.53, 55.47, 55.47, 54.38, 54.54, 54.49, 54.49, 54.6…\n## $ Latitude         <dbl> 42.51, 25.42, 25.39, 24.48, 24.51, 24.40, 24.40, 24.…</code></pre>\n<p>Immediately, we can see that our column names are not optimal for analysis. Personally, I’m VERY biased towards <code>snake_case</code> and therefore always like to get column names into that format.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"janitorclean_names\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>janitor::clean_names()</h3>\n<p>In comes <code>{janitor::clean_names}</code> to the rescue <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/26d1.png\" alt=\"⛑\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n<p>By default, <code>clean_names()</code> outputs column naming with the <code>snake_case</code> format – maybe this is one of the reasons that it’s in my top 10 for favorite functions in <code>R</code>.</p>\n<p>Let’s test it out on our coffee data.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># clean_names() with default naming\ncoffee_chains_processed %>% \n  janitor::clean_names() %>% \n  base::names()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>##  [1] \"brand\"          \"store_number\"   \"store_name\"     \"ownership_type\"\n##  [5] \"street_address\" \"city\"           \"state_province\" \"country\"       \n##  [9] \"postcode\"       \"phone_number\"   \"timezone\"       \"longitude\"     \n## [13] \"latitude\"</code></pre>\n<div id=\"awesome\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Awesome!</h4>\n<p>You’ll notice the <code>function</code> took care of the <code>/</code> in <code>State/Province</code> and replaced it with an underscore – simply amazing <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f60e.png\" alt=\"😎\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"naming-convention-options\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Naming Convention Options</h4>\n<p>If you prefer a different naming convention – I’m not sure why you would <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f644.png\" alt=\"🙄\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> – then you can use the <code>case</code> argument.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># clean_names() with diff. naming convention\ncoffee_chains_processed %>% \n  clean_names(case = \"small_camel\") %>% \n  names()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>##  [1] \"brand\"         \"storeNumber\"   \"storeName\"     \"ownershipType\"\n##  [5] \"streetAddress\" \"city\"          \"stateProvince\" \"country\"      \n##  [9] \"postcode\"      \"phoneNumber\"   \"timezone\"      \"longitude\"    \n## [13] \"latitude\"</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div id=\"pre-processing-addition\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Pre-Processing Addition</h3>\n<p>Now let’s add this step to our pre-processing pipeline.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Adding to our Pre-Processing Pipeline\ncoffee_chains_processed <- coffee_chains_raw %>% \n  \n  # clean up column names\n  janitor::clean_names()</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"janitorget_dupes\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>janitor::get_dupes()</h3>\n<p><code>get_dupes()</code> is at the top of the list for newly discovered <code>functionality</code> within the <code>{janitor}</code> package.</p>\n<p>This is one of those things you need to do often (check for duplicates) and <code>{janitor}</code> makes it simple.</p>\n<p>Going back to our <code>case-study</code>, our manager asked us to check for duplicated records (a common <code>data-cleaning</code> and <code>EDA</code> step).</p>\n<p>Let’s subset our data and investigate.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>coffee_chains_processed %>% \n  \n  # subset data by store and by location\n  dplyr::select(brand, store_number, \n                city, state_province, country) %>% \n  \n  # identify duplicated records\n  janitor::get_dupes()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 2 x 6\n##   brand     store_number city  state_province country dupe_count\n##   <chr>     <chr>        <chr> <chr>          <chr>        <int>\n## 1 Starbucks 19773-160973 Seoul 11             KR               2\n## 2 Starbucks 19773-160973 Seoul 11             KR               2</code></pre>\n<p>Using <code>janitor::get_dupes()</code> we’ve quickly identified a potential issue: store number <code>19773-160973</code> has duplicated records.</p>\n<p>Let’s investigate further.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># filter to store with dupes\ncoffee_chains_processed %>% \n  \n  # filter to store and glimpse data\n  dplyr::filter(store_number == \"19773-160973\") %>% \n  glimpse()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## Rows: 2\n## Columns: 13\n## $ brand          <chr> \"Starbucks\", \"Starbucks\"\n## $ store_number   <chr> \"19773-160973\", \"19773-160973\"\n## $ store_name     <chr> \"Yoido IFC Mall - 1F\", \"Yoido IFC Mall - 1F\"\n## $ ownership_type <chr> \"Joint Venture\", \"Joint Venture\"\n## $ street_address <chr> \"23 & 23-1, Yoido-Dong, Yongdongpo-Gu, 1F, #101\", \"23 …\n## $ city           <chr> \"Seoul\", \"Seoul\"\n## $ state_province <chr> \"11\", \"11\"\n## $ country        <chr> \"KR\", \"KR\"\n## $ postcode       <chr> \"153-023\", \"153-023\"\n## $ phone_number   <chr> NA, NA\n## $ timezone       <chr> \"GMT+09:00 Asia/Seoul\", \"GMT+09:00 Asia/Seoul\"\n## $ longitude      <dbl> NA, 126.92\n## $ latitude       <dbl> NA, 37.53</code></pre>\n<p>Look carefully and you’ll notice that the latitude/longitude are missing for one of these records.</p>\n<p>We need lat/long for mapping and so we will want to prioritize the records with those data. Also, we don’t want duplicated records to interfere with our tabulations later on in this analysis.</p>\n<p>Let’s quickly look and see how much data is missing from the lat/long columns.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># plot missing data (using raw data)\nDataExplorer::plot_missing(\n  title   = \"% of Missing Data (filtered to cols w/missing data)\",\n  data    = coffee_chains_raw,\n  ggtheme = tidyquant::theme_tq(), \n  missing_only = TRUE) </code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.exploringdata.org/post/2020-09-02-how-to-clean-data-janitor-package_files/figure-html/unnamed-chunk-13-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>The plot shows that <code>0%</code> of data are missing for lat/long leading me to believe that the store identified earlier is the only record with missing data (insignificant amount when plotted).</p>\n<p>We will filter that record out in our <code>data-cleaning</code> step.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"pre-processing-addition-1\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Pre-Processing Addition</h3>\n<p>Now let’s add this step to our pre-processing pipeline</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Adding to our Pre-Processing Pipeline\ncoffee_chains_processed <- coffee_chains_raw %>% \n  \n  # clean up column names\n  janitor::clean_names() %>% \n  \n  # filter out records missing lat/long values\n  dplyr::filter(!is.na(latitude), !is.na(longitude))</code></pre>\n<p>Let’s use <code>get_dupes()</code> to confirm the problem is solved</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>coffee_chains_processed %>% \n  \n  # subset data\n  dplyr::select(brand, store_number, city, state_province, country) %>% \n  \n  # identify duplicated records\n  janitor::get_dupes()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 0 x 6\n## # … with 6 variables: brand <chr>, store_number <chr>, city <chr>,\n## #   state_province <chr>, country <chr>, dupe_count <int></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"starbucks-analysis\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Starbucks Analysis</h3>\n<p>Now that we’ve done our due diligence in being sure we’ve dealt with data issues, let’s knock out this analysis by<br />\ntabulating these data and compiling a map, or two <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f913.png\" alt=\"🤓\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n<p>Before doing so, let’s add one final step to our pre-processing data pipeline.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"pre-processing-addition-2\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Pre-Processing Addition</h3>\n<p>The final step is to subset the columns needed to complete the analysis.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Adding to our Pre-Processing Pipeline\ncoffee_chains_processed <- coffee_chains_raw %>% \n  \n  # clean up column names\n  janitor::clean_names() %>% \n  \n  # filter out records missing lat/long values\n  dplyr::filter(!is.na(latitude), !is.na(longitude)) %>% \n  \n  # subset columns for analysis\n  dplyr::select(brand, ownership_type, country, \n                state_province, latitude, longitude)</code></pre>\n<div id=\"view-data\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>View Data</h4>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># view first 5 rows\ncoffee_chains_processed %>% head(5)</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 5 x 6\n##   brand     ownership_type country state_province latitude longitude\n##   <chr>     <chr>          <chr>   <chr>             <dbl>     <dbl>\n## 1 Starbucks Licensed       AD      7                  42.5      1.53\n## 2 Starbucks Licensed       AE      AJ                 25.4     55.5 \n## 3 Starbucks Licensed       AE      AJ                 25.4     55.5 \n## 4 Starbucks Licensed       AE      AZ                 24.5     54.4 \n## 5 Starbucks Licensed       AE      AZ                 24.5     54.5</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div id=\"tabulate-data-worldwide\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Tabulate Data (worldwide)</h3>\n<p>Let’s start with looking at Ownership Types worldwide.</p>\n<p><code>janitor::tabyl</code> stuck out to me because the ease with which to generate frequency tables.</p>\n<p>Check it out.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># generate frequency table\ncoffee_chains_processed %>% \n  \n  # filter data\n  dplyr::filter(brand == \"Starbucks\") %>% \n  \n  # tabulate and arrange data\n  janitor::tabyl(ownership_type) %>% \n  arrange(desc(percent)) %>% \n  \n  # formatting\n  janitor::adorn_totals() %>% \n  janitor::adorn_pct_formatting() %>% \n  rmarkdown::paged_table()</code></pre>\n<div data-pagedtable=\"false\">\n<script data-pagedtable-source type=\"application/json\">\n{\"columns\":[{\"label\":[\"\"],\"name\":[\"_rn_\"],\"type\":[\"\"],\"align\":[\"left\"]},{\"label\":[\"ownership_type\"],\"name\":[1],\"type\":[\"chr\"],\"align\":[\"left\"]},{\"label\":[\"n\"],\"name\":[2],\"type\":[\"dbl\"],\"align\":[\"right\"]},{\"label\":[\"percent\"],\"name\":[3],\"type\":[\"chr\"],\"align\":[\"left\"]}],\"data\":[{\"1\":\"Company Owned\",\"2\":\"11581\",\"3\":\"45.9%\",\"_rn_\":\"1\"},{\"1\":\"Licensed\",\"2\":\"9375\",\"3\":\"37.1%\",\"_rn_\":\"2\"},{\"1\":\"Joint Venture\",\"2\":\"3975\",\"3\":\"15.7%\",\"_rn_\":\"3\"},{\"1\":\"Franchise\",\"2\":\"317\",\"3\":\"1.3%\",\"_rn_\":\"4\"},{\"1\":\"Total\",\"2\":\"25248\",\"3\":\"100.0%\",\"_rn_\":\"5\"}],\"options\":{\"columns\":{\"min\":{},\"max\":[10]},\"rows\":{\"min\":[10],\"max\":[10]},\"pages\":{}}}\n  </script>\n</div>\n<p>Using just the <code>tabyl</code> function we were able to generate frequencies along with the percent of total.</p>\n<p>However, <code>{janitor}</code> is packed full of other goodies – the creator(s) have crafted a number of <code>adorn</code> options for formatting our outputs. I used the <code>adorn_totals</code> and <code>adorn_pct_formatting</code> to tidy up and make our table ready for presentation.</p>\n<p>Simply Amazing <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f60e.png\" alt=\"😎\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"tabulate-data-us-lower-48\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Tabulate Data (US, lower 48)</h3>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># generate frequency table\ncoffee_chains_processed %>% \n  \n  # filter data\n  dplyr::filter(brand   == \"Starbucks\", \n                country == \"US\", \n                state_province != \"AK\", \n                state_province != \"HI\") %>% \n  \n  # tabulate and arrange data\n  janitor::tabyl(ownership_type) %>% \n  arrange(desc(percent)) %>% \n  \n  # formatting\n  janitor::adorn_totals() %>% \n  janitor::adorn_pct_formatting() %>% \n  rmarkdown::paged_table()</code></pre>\n<div data-pagedtable=\"false\">\n<script data-pagedtable-source type=\"application/json\">\n{\"columns\":[{\"label\":[\"\"],\"name\":[\"_rn_\"],\"type\":[\"\"],\"align\":[\"left\"]},{\"label\":[\"ownership_type\"],\"name\":[1],\"type\":[\"chr\"],\"align\":[\"left\"]},{\"label\":[\"n\"],\"name\":[2],\"type\":[\"dbl\"],\"align\":[\"right\"]},{\"label\":[\"percent\"],\"name\":[3],\"type\":[\"chr\"],\"align\":[\"left\"]}],\"data\":[{\"1\":\"Company Owned\",\"2\":\"7859\",\"3\":\"59.7%\",\"_rn_\":\"1\"},{\"1\":\"Licensed\",\"2\":\"5306\",\"3\":\"40.3%\",\"_rn_\":\"2\"},{\"1\":\"Total\",\"2\":\"13165\",\"3\":\"100.0%\",\"_rn_\":\"3\"}],\"options\":{\"columns\":{\"min\":{},\"max\":[10]},\"rows\":{\"min\":[10],\"max\":[10]},\"pages\":{}}}\n  </script>\n</div>\n<blockquote>\n<p>All Starbucks are either company owned, which is almost all of them, or else they’re “licensed” locations, which are the Starbucks in airports, supermarkets, etc. – Charles Partrick</p>\n</blockquote>\n</div>\n<div id=\"map-starbucks-locations\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Map Starbucks Locations</h3>\n<p>Now lets make those maps and get this analysis wrapped up.</p>\n<p>Lets start by getting a general sense of where in the US these Starbucks are located.</p>\n<div id=\"data-manipulation\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Data Manipulation</h4>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Data Manipulation\nstarbucks_lower_48 <- coffee_chains_processed %>% \n  \n  # filter data\n  dplyr::filter(brand   == \"Starbucks\", \n                country == \"US\", \n                state_province != \"AK\", \n                state_province != \"HI\") </code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"data-visualization\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Data Visualization</h4>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Data Visualization\nstarbucks_lower_48 %>% \n  \n  # setup ggplot canvas + US borders\n  ggplot(aes(longitude, latitude, color = ownership_type)) +\n  \n  # add geometries\n  borders(\"state\") +\n  geom_point(size = .75, alpha = 0.5) +\n  \n  # formatting\n  ggthemes::theme_map() +   # remove x/y for tidy map\n  coord_map() +             # scales map (simple approach)\n  scale_color_manual(values = c(\"#2c3e50\", \"#18BC9C\")) +\n  labs(title = \"Starbucks Locations by Ownership Type (Lower 48)\",\n       color = \"Ownership Type\")</code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.exploringdata.org/post/2020-09-02-how-to-clean-data-janitor-package_files/figure-html/unnamed-chunk-21-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>That’s a solid map but I think we can do better to identify patterns in ownership types.</p>\n<p>Let’s calculate the ratio of Corporate (Company Owned) vs. Licensed ownership and map that at the state level.</p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"data-acquisition-state-boundaries\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Data Acquisition (state boundaries)</h4>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Get state level lat/long table\nstates <- ggplot2::map_data(\"state\") %>% \n  tibble() %>% \n  mutate(region = str_to_title(region))</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"data-manipulation-1\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>Data Manipulation</h4>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code># Data Manipulation\nownership_ratios_by_state <- starbucks_lower_48 %>% \n  \n  # count ownership types by state\n  group_by(state_province, ownership_type) %>% \n  summarize(n = n()) %>% \n  ungroup() %>% \n  \n  # pivot data and calculate ratios\n  pivot_wider(names_from = ownership_type, \n              values_from = n) %>% \n  clean_names() %>% \n  mutate(corp_vs_lic = company_owned/licensed) %>% \n  \n  # join to get state names from codes\n  left_join(USAboundaries::state_codes %>% \n          select(state_name, state_abbr),\n          by = c(\"state_province\" = \"state_abbr\")) %>% \n  \n  # reorder columns\n  select(state_name, everything())</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div id=\"view-data-1\" class=\"section level4\">\n<h4>View Data</h4>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>ownership_ratios_by_state %>% head()</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 6 x 5\n##   state_name  state_province company_owned licensed corp_vs_lic\n##   <chr>       <chr>                  <int>    <int>       <dbl>\n## 1 Alabama     AL                        48       36       1.33 \n## 2 Arkansas    AR                        35       19       1.84 \n## 3 Arizona     AZ                       196      283       0.693\n## 4 California  CA                      1943      839       2.32 \n## 5 Colorado    CO                       227      250       0.908\n## 6 Connecticut CT                        83       35       2.37</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div id=\"data-visualization-1\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Data Visualization</h3>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>ownership_ratios_by_state %>% \n  \n  # join to get state boundaries (lat/long)\n  left_join(states, by = c(\"state_name\" = \"region\")) %>% \n  \n  # setup ggplot canvas + US borders\n  ggplot(aes(long, lat, fill = corp_vs_lic, group = group)) +\n  \n  # add geometries\n  geom_polygon() +\n  ggplot2::borders(\"state\") +\n  \n  # formatting\n  ggthemes::theme_map() + # remove x/y for tidy map\n  theme(legend.position = c(.9, .05)) +\n  coord_map(projection = \"mercator\") + # scales map projection \n  scale_fill_gradient2(low = \"white\", high = \"#18BC9C\", ) + \n  labs(title = \"Ratio of Corporate vs. Licensed Starbucks in the US (Lower 48)\",\n       subtitle = \"Darker green equates to more corporate locations compared to licensed establishments.\",\n       fill = \"Ratio of\\nCorporate/\\nLicensed\") </code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.exploringdata.org/post/2020-09-02-how-to-clean-data-janitor-package_files/figure-html/unnamed-chunk-25-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>This represent the data in a way that helps us identify patterns – our manager will be pleased <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f44d.png\" alt=\"👍\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"wrap-up\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Wrap Up</h3>\n<p>I hope you enjoyed the first post in this new series.</p>\n<p>Leave a
