c532ac380.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/Qfmn6u4WRNg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn28@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-18-829_b095ce730636b18b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287180062},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","title":"A few tips for beginners on how to engage with the open source community","link":"http://gforge.se/?p=2210","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/G-forgeR/~3/5Zstpudmbig/\"> R – G-Forge</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<div id=\"attachment_2255\" style=\"width: 810px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://i2.wp.com/usercontent.one/wp/gforge.se/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Ryan-Roberts-sharing-flickr.jpg?ssl=1\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2255\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/usercontent.one/wp/gforge.se/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Ryan-Roberts-sharing-flickr.jpg?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2255\" srcset_temp=\"https://i2.wp.com/usercontent.one/wp/gforge.se/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Ryan-Roberts-sharing-flickr.jpg?w=450&#038;ssl=1 800w, https://usercontent.one/wp/gforge.se/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Ryan-Roberts-sharing-flickr-300x192.jpg 300w, https://usercontent.one/wp/gforge.se/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Ryan-Roberts-sharing-flickr-768x492.jpg 768w, https://usercontent.one/wp/gforge.se/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Ryan-Roberts-sharing-flickr-468x300.jpg 468w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p id=\"caption-attachment-2255\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Engaging the open source community can be challenging at start. The image is CC by <a href=\"https://flic.kr/p/dA7Vg\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Ryan Roberts</a>.</p>\n</div>\n<p>I have been writing code in multiple languages since 1994 and for almost a decade now I have been active within the open source community, primarily in R but I’ve also published some <a href=\"https://www.npmjs.com/settings/gforge/packages\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">JavaSript/TypeScript packages</a>. Publishing something that other people enjoy is a pure joy and the fact that some of my packages have download counts in the thousands is something that truly warms my heart. </p>\n<p>Throughout the years I have though noticed that there is newcomers to the open source community often struggle with how to get help, which is why I decided to write this post. I’ll start out with some basics and then a little more advanced topics such as how to write an issue or a pull request.<span id=\"more-2210\"></span></p>\n<h1>“Help me, I’m stuck!”</h1>\n<p>This happens to us all and the steps for solving your issue follow a simple path:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>Google the problem:\n<ul>\n<li>Find key words that are unique to your issue</li>\n<li>Copy-paste error codes/messages into the search field (if you get to few hits, remove sections of the error code, especially anything that is specific to your setting, e.g. filename in the message)</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li>Make sure you understand what is happening, the easiest way is usually to print variables and inspect their content (this is usually frowned upon, but we humans are lazy by nature). A slightly more advanced approach is to use a debugger, in R there I often use <code>debug()</code> or <code>browser</code>, you can read more about it <a href=\"https://rstats.wtf/debugging-r-code.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">here</a> (JavaScript the <code>debugger</code> can be quite useful).</li>\n<li>Ok, so none of those worked then we read the manual or if you are in R, start looking if there is a <i>vignette</i> that covers what you are trying to do. Usually we did look at the manual in the beginning but most likely we skimmed it quickly, jumped to conclusions and started writing code. Quite often the information is there in the manual but perhaps less accessible, writing a good manual is difficult and as a package maintainer I always welcome ideas for making my manuals more accessible.</li>\n<li>Now is the time to start looking for human help, ideally you start with writing up a question on <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Stack Overflow</a> (see section below)</li>\n<li>Get a <a href=\"https://github.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">GitHub</a> account and write an issue (see section below)</li>\n</ol>\n<p>Usually before I start engaging other people I try to have a look at the source code and see if I can understand it myself. This is often useful as I sometimes pick up neat tricks while solving the original problem, although it requires some skill and is perhaps not something that you should do as a beginner.</p>\n<h1>Who are these open source folks?</h1>\n<p>Understanding who is behind the open source community is key if you want to get help. First and foremost most of us are doing this for fun in our spare time and we don’t get paid. Some examples that I recently encountered that suggest that this isn’t obvious:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>I recently helped out with a JavaSript package, graphql-passport. In a follow-up <a href=\"https://github.com/jkettmann/graphql-passport/issues/34\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">issue</a> there was a related bug that I was asked to help out with and wrote that <i>I’ll try to have a look at this</i> which someone downvoted without even taking the time to comment.</li>\n<li>In an email from a researcher I got a question regarding my <a href=\"https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/forestplot/index.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">forestplot</a> package. I recommended they post on Stack Overflow first so that others could find the solution, and got this back <i>“It’s 6 hours I posted my problem in StackOverflow, and I have not got any response yet. It’s very urgent for me to get the solution.”</i></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Clearly none of these bothered to understand that I’m primarily an <i>orthopedic surgeon</i> and that I offer my code so that others may use it, enjoy it and hopefully get something useful out of it. If you are in a hurry, then you can always find people that know how to code that you can pay to help you out.</p>\n<h1>Writing a good Stack Overflow question</h1>\n<p>Writing a question should take time. A good question will give you an answer within minutes while a poor will perhaps never be answered. I have usually had success with the following structure:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>Make the title as precise as you can</li>\n<li>Start with the question, possibly with an intro, e.g. <em>“I’m trying to do a forestplot with multiple bands per row, how should I structure my input data?”</em></li>\n<li>Short background on language, packages you’re using and other relevant details.</li>\n<li>Add a <strong>reproducible code example</strong> – this is probably one of the most time consuming details but it is vital as the answer can build upon this code and make sure that the answer is the one that you are looking for</li>\n<li>Add a longer background if needed with more details – don’t be shy to use headlines that separate sections. It is sometimes difficult to understand what detail you have missed and here you can add all those things that could possibly be contributing to your problem. At this time the reader will be familiar with the problem and can easily skip anything that he/she deems irrelevant (don’t make this too large though as a huge question may be intimidating).</li>\n<li>Repeat your question, at the end you can feel free to repeat your question, possibly with some more details.</li>\n<li>Choose the relevant tags, e.g. R, forestplot if you have an R question about forest plots.</li>\n</ol>\n<p>While writing your question, <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Stack Overflow</a> suggests similar questions, it has happened to me some times that I write a question and the find that I missed it during the “Googling step”. Similarly, when I’ve been writing my <i>reproducible code example</i> I figured out the solution on my own as I have removed all the noise and made everything much clearer. Sometimes it can bee good idea to then answer your own question, you won’t get points for this but it is an easy way to become an open source contributor as others will find your answer and hopefully save precious hours of debugging.</p>\n<h1>Interacting with authors</h1>\n<p>The more popular a package is the more issues and questions arise for that package. Keep this in mind when you consider to contact authors as maintaining a popular package can be a lot of work. Make sure to be kind and solution oriented (despite being frustrated and annoyed that your code is failing). Also, once you post an issue/pull request make sure that you <strong>quickly answer</strong> any questions that the community has. If I post a question without getting an answer, my willingness to spend time solving the issue quickly diminishes unless it affects my own work.</p>\n<h2>Writing an issue</h2>\n<p>An issue can be of two types, (1) a bug in the software that needs correction, or (2) a feature that you want to be implemented. When you write your issue, usually on <a href=\"https://github.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">GitHub</a>, you should structure it similarly to the section on “Writing a good Stack Overflow question”; it should be clear what the issue is about and there should be some code example (ideally you should look at the already existing examples within the package and use that code). If you want to speed up the implementation you can suggest a solution and/or <a href=\"https://r-pkgs.org/tests.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">a test case</a>. Issues usually take longer time to get resolved than Stack Overflow questions and sometimes I write my issue first on Stack Overflow so that I am certain that I haven’t missed something in the manual.</p>\n<p>Before you write an issue it can be a good idea to see if the problem has already an existing issue. Note that some issues may still exist and have been closed (make sure to remove “<a href=\"https://docs.github.com/en/github/searching-for-information-on-github/searching-issues-and-pull-requests\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">is:open</a>” for GitHub searches) as the author may have decided that it isn’t an issue that is fixeable or where there already is a working solution. </p>\n<h2>Make a pull request</h2>\n<p>If you have found the source of the issue and your fairly certain that this is the cause of the problem, then do a pull request (also know as a PR). In addition to fixing the problem you should add <a href=\"https://r-pkgs.org/tests.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">a test case</a> (unless you are changing the manual), this makes it much easier for the author to understand the problem and trust the solution. Make sure to follow each author’s coding style, e.g. if someone uses CamelCase then you should also be using CamelCase. <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lint_(software)\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Linting</a> is not yet that common in languages such as R (e.g. <a href=\"https://github.com/jimhester/lintr\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">lintr</a>), but judging from developments in more popular languages, we can expect linting to become commonplace the coming decade. By making sure that you follow the style that the author has chosen, it usually becomes easier to read and understand code as a homogenous style allows you to focus more on the code intention than on argument spacing, brackets etc.</p>\n<p>In GitHub this is <a href=\"https://linuxhint.com/pull_request_github/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">fairly easy</a>, fork the repository with the package, do your changes and then click on pull request once you have uploaded the changes.  A pull request can be done alone or in combination with writing an issue. I often add an issue if it is a somewhat more complex issue that I am addressing.</p>\n<h2>E-mail</h2>\n<p>I sometimes receive e-mails from users asking questions. Usually I try to refer everyone to Stack Overflow as I believe that e-mailed questions scale poorly; <a href=\"https://github.com/gforge/htmlTable\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">htmlTable</a> has 160 000+ downloads/month and therefore it is reasonable that all support should be available through a Google-search. I often encourage people to e-mail me a link to a Stack Overflow question that has gone unanswered for a week and then I can have a look at it.</p>\n<h1>Contribute yourself!</h1>\n<p>At the core of the open source community is that everyone can contribute. Contribution can be simple</p>\n<h2>First steps</h2>\n<p>Try to take the time to look for questions on Stack Overflow that you can answer or if you know a package well, look into all the issues that people have with that package and see if your knowledge can help them. Many of the posted issues have already existing functionality that the users haven’t found.</p>\n<h2>Improve a package</h2>\n<p>Writing and maintaining a package is a lot of work, I would therefore suggest that you look for already existing solutions and look for what is missing something. The easiest starting point is to ask yourself what in the manual doesn’t make sense and suggest changes. There are always things that can be improved upon and as an author you often become myopic and fail to see everything from spelling mistakes to errors/ambiguities.</p>\n<p>Remember that the sky is the limit. Some of the best features in my packages come from suggestions that I have gotten from others. Sometimes getting the core functionality in place takes so much effort that the you forget about the finishing touches that can do miracles for your users.</p>\n<h2>Publishing your own R-packages</h2>\n<p>Publishing a package is a lot of work, especially if you are writing an R-package and are aiming for <a href=\"https://cran.r-project.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">CRAN</a>. I think CRAN is amazing but it has the most ambitious code quality routines that I have ever encountered. Even a published package will have to be updated once in a while as the requirements change. Despite this, it may be worthwhile; the checks are good for guaranteeing that your code has a certain quality and is well documented.</p>\n<h3>What should I publish?</h3>\n<p>As R is a programming language for statisticians I thought in the beginning that packages should address hardcore statistics, e.g. regressions. In truth, the statistics is a tiny part of the research process and everything from preparing datasets to cool new plots are good enough for publishing. My packages with the most impact have been tools that I was working on during my thesis. These were simple things like tables, plots etc that I found useful.</p>\n<h3>What to think of when writing a package?</h3>\n<p>We all have our regrets, some of mine are due to bad argument naming in my packages. Once you’ve published a package and it becomes a success, changing the argument names, function structure etc incur a pain for others and should be avoided if possible. Planning is therefore crucial, here is a simple list of things to think of when writing your first real package:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>Look at other popular packages and their names for functions and arguments and use that as a source of inspiration.</li>\n<li>Add as many tests as you can from the start, these will make maintenance much easier as you don’t have to worry so much about breaking stuff when you add new features.</li>\n<li>Use a <a href=\"https://github.com/jimhester/lintr\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">linter</a> for code consistency.</li>\n<li>Make good use of <code>...</code> and the <a href=\"http://adv-r.had.co.nz/S3.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">S3</a>-system, one of my regrets have been that I have not used <code>print</code>, <code>summary</code> and other functions for my own classes as much as I should have. These could have simplified things a great deal.</li>\n<li>Documentation is today written in <a href=\"https://roxygen2.r-lib.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">roxygen2</a>. Take your time to look through all the features.</li>\n</ol>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = Then let’s learn time series.</strong> In this short articles series, I highlight how you can get up to speed quickly on important aspects of time series analysis. Today we are focusing analyzing anomalies in time series data.</p>\n<h3 id=\"updates\">Updates</h3>\n<p>This article has been updated. <a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/09/02/five-minute-time-series-anomaly-detection.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">View the updated Time Series in 5-Minutes article at Business Science.</a></p>\n<h2 id=\"time-series-in-5-mintues-articles-in-this-series\">Time Series in 5-Mintues <br /><small>Articles in this Series</small></h2>\n<div class=\"pull-right hidden-xs\" style=\"width:50%; margin-left:20px;\">\n  <a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/09/02/five-minute-time-series-anomaly-detection.html#\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><br />\n  <img class=\"img-responsive\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.business-science.io/assets/2020-09-02-time-series-anomaly-detection/time_series_anomaly_detection_cover.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><br />\n  </a></p>\n<p class=\"date text-center\">Anomaly Detection – A fundamental tool in your arsenal</p>\n</div>\n<p>I just released <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">timetk</code> 2.0.0 (<a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/06/05/timetk-vesion-2-announcement.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">read the release announcement</a>). A ton of new functionality has been added. We’ll discuss some of the key pieces in this article series:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/08/19/five-minute-time-series-rolling-calculations.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Part 1, Data Wrangling and Rolling Calculations</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/06/08/five-minute-time-series-time-plot.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Part 2, The Time Plot</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/06/17/five-minute-time-series-part-2.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Part 3, Autocorrelation</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/08/26/five-minute-time-series-seasonality.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Part 4, Seasonality</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.business-science.io/code-tools/2020/09/02/five-minute-time-series-anomaly-detection.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Part 5, Anomalies and Anomaly Detection</a></li>\n<li>Part 6, Dealing with Missing Time Series Data</li>\n</ul>\n<p><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f449.png\" alt=\"👉\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> <a href=\"https://mailchi.mp/business-science/blog-registration\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>Register for our blog to get new articles as we release them.</strong></a></p>\n<h1 id=\"have-5-minutes-then-lets-learn-time-series-anomaly-detection\">Have 5-Minutes? <br /><small>Then let’s learn Time Series Anomaly Detection</small></h1>\n<p><strong>Anomaly detection</strong> is an important part of time series analysis:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>Detecting anomalies can signify special events</li>\n<li>Cleaning anomalies can improve forecast error</li>\n</ol>\n<p>In this short tutorial, we will cover the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">plot_anomaly_diagnostics()</code> and <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">tk_anomaly_diagnostics()</code> functions for visualizing and automatically detecting anomalies at scale.</p>\n<h2 id=\"time-series-course\">Advanced Time Series Course<br /><small>Become the times series domain expert in your organization.</small></h2>\n<p>Make sure you’re notified when my new <strong><em>Advanced Time Series Forecasting in R course</em></strong> comes out. Become the times series domain expert in your organization.</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f449.png\" alt=\"👉\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: Course.</strong></a></p>\n<div class=\"\" style=\"width:100%; \">\n  <a href=\"https://mailchi.mp/business-science/time-series-forecasting-course-coming-soon\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><br rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Signup for the Time Series Course waitlist</a>\n</p>\n<h1 id=\"lets-get-started\">Let’s Get Started</h1>\n<p>First setup the libraries we’ll use:</p>\n<figure class=\"highlight\">\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\" data-lang=\"r\"><span class=\"n\">library</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">tidyverse</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"n\">library</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">timetk</span><span class=\"p\">)</span></code></pre>\n</figure>\n<h1 id=\"data\">Data</h1>\n<p>This tutorial will use the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">walmart_sales_weekly</code> dataset:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Weekly</li>\n<li>Sales spikes at various events</li>\n</ul>\n<figure class=\"highlight\">\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\" data-lang=\"r\"><span class=\"n\">walmart_sales_weekly</span></code></pre>\n</figure>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.business-science.io/assets/2020-09-02-time-series-anomaly-detection/data_summary.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Data Summary\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<h1 id=\"automatic-anomaly-detection\">Automatic Anomaly Detection</h1>\n<p>To get the data on the anomalies, we use <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">tk_anomaly_diagnostics()</code>, the preprocessing function.</p>\n<p>The <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">tk_anomaly_diagnostics()</code> method for anomaly detection implements a 2-step process to detect outliers in time series.</p>\n<h4 id=\"step-1-detrend--remove-seasonality-using-stl-decomposition\">Step 1: Detrend & Remove Seasonality using STL Decomposition</h4>\n<p>The decomposition separates the “season” and “trend” components from the “observed” values leaving the “remainder” for anomaly detection.</p>\n<p>The user can control two parameters: frequency and trend.</p>\n<ol>\n<li><code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">.frequency</code>: Adjusts the “season” component that is removed from the “observed” values.</li>\n<li><code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">.trend</code>: Adjusts the trend window (t.window parameter from <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">stats::stl()</code> that is used.</li>\n</ol>\n<p>The user may supply both .frequency and .trend as time-based durations (e.g. “6 weeks”) or numeric values (e.g. 180) or “auto”, which predetermines the frequency and/or trend based on the scale of the time series using the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">tk_time_scale_template()</code>.</p>\n<h4 id=\"step-2-anomaly-detection\">Step 2: Anomaly Detection</h4>\n<p>Once “trend” and “season” (seasonality) is removed, anomaly detection is performed on the “remainder”. Anomalies are identified, and boundaries (recomposed_l1 and recomposed_l2) are determined.</p>\n<p>The Anomaly Detection Method uses an inner quartile range (IQR) of +/-25 the median.</p>\n<p>IQR Adjustment, alpha parameter</p>\n<p>With the default alpha = 0.05, the limits are established by expanding the 25/75 baseline by an IQR Factor of 3 (3X). The IQR Factor = 0.15 / alpha (hence 3X with alpha = 0.05):</p>\n<ul>\n<li>To increase the IQR Factor controlling the limits, decrease the alpha, which makes it more difficult to be an outlier.</li>\n<li>Increase alpha to make it easier to be an outlier.</li>\n<li>The IQR outlier detection method is used in <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">forecast::tsoutliers()</code>.</li>\n<li>A similar outlier detection method is used by Twitter’s AnomalyDetection package.</li>\n<li>Both Twitter and Forecast tsoutliers methods have been implemented in Business Science’s anomalize package.</li>\n</ul>\n<figure class=\"highlight\">\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\" data-lang=\"r\"><span class=\"n\">walmart_sales_weekly</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">%>%</span><span class=\"w\">\n  </span><span class=\"n\">group_by</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">Store</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">Dept</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">%>%</span><span class=\"w\">\n  </span><span class=\"n\">tk_anomaly_diagnostics</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">Date</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">Weekly_Sales</span><span class=\"p\">)</span></code></pre>\n</figure>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.business-science.io/assets/2020-09-02-time-series-anomaly-detection/automatic_anomaly_detection_summary.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Anomaly Detection\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<h1 id=\"anomaly-visualization\">Anomaly Visualization</h1>\n<p>Using the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">plot_anomaly_diagnostics()</code> function, we can interactively detect anomalies at scale.</p>\n<p>The <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">plot_anomaly_diagnostics()</code> is a visualtion wrapper for <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">tk_anomaly_diagnostics()</code> group-wise anomaly detection, implementing the 2-step process from above.</p>\n<figure class=\"highlight\">\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\" data-lang=\"r\"><span class=\"n\">walmart_sales_weekly</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">%>%</span><span class=\"w\">\n  </span><span class=\"n\">group_by</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">Store</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">Dept</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">%>%</span><span class=\"w\">\n  </span><span class=\"n\">plot_anomaly_diagnostics</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">Date</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">Weekly_Sales</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">.facet_ncol</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"m\">2</span><span class=\"p\">)</span></code></pre>\n</figure>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.business-science.io/assets/2020-09-02-time-series-anomaly-detection/anomaly_diagnostics.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Anomaly Diagnostics\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<h2 id=\"time-series-course\">Advanced Time Series Course<br /><small>Become the times series domain expert in your organization.</small></h2>\n<p>Make sure you’re notified when my new Today we have Texas (2) taking on Florida (3) for the right to compete in the State-Off championships! Don’t forget to update your version of <code>SwimmeR</code> to 0.4.1, because we’ll be using some newly released functions. We’ll also take a look at how to plot swimming times while maintaining their format.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>library(SwimmeR)\nlibrary(dplyr)\nlibrary(stringr)\nlibrary(flextable)\nlibrary(ggplot2)</code></pre>\n<p>Since I like to use the same style <code>flextable</code> to display a lot of results, rather than retyping the associated <code>flextable</code> calls let’s just define a function now, which we can reuse every time.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>flextable_style <- function(x) {\n  x %>%\n    flextable() %>%\n    bold(part = \"header\") %>% # bolds header\n    bg(bg = \"#D3D3D3\", part = \"header\") %>% # puts gray background behind the header row\n    autofit()\n}</code></pre>\n<p>\n</p>\n<hr />\n<div id=\"getting-results\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Getting Results</h3>\n<p>As I mentioned in the <code>SwimmeR 0.4.1</code> <a href=\"https://pilgrim.netlify.app/post/new-version-of-swimmer-and-the-next-round-of-the-state-off-tournament/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">post</a> last week these posts are no longer going to focus on using <code>SwimmeR</code> to read in results. That’s been covered extensively in previous State-Off posts. Instead I’ve already read in the results for each state and I’m hosting them on github.</p>\n<p>We’ll just pull results for Texas and Florida, and then stick them together with <code>bind_rows</code>.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Texas_Link <-\n  \"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/gpilgrim2670/Pilgrim_Data/master/TX_States_2020.csv\"\nTexas_Results <- read.csv(url(Texas_Link)) %>% \n  mutate(State = \"TX\",\n         Grade = as.character(Grade))</code></pre>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Florida_Link <-\n  \"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/gpilgrim2670/Pilgrim_Data/master/FL_States_2020.csv\"\nFlorida_Results <- read.csv(url(Florida_Link)) %>% \n  mutate(State = \"FL\")</code></pre>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Results <- Texas_Results %>%\n  bind_rows(Florida_Results) %>%\n  mutate(Gender = case_when(\n    str_detect(Event, \"Girls\") == TRUE ~ \"Girls\",\n    str_detect(Event, \"Boys\") == TRUE ~ \"Boys\"\n  ))</code></pre>\n<p>\n</p>\n<hr />\n</div>\n<div id=\"scoring-the-meet\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Scoring the Meet</h3>\n<p>Here’s one of the new functions for <code>SwimmeR 0.4.1</code>. If you followed along with previous State-Off posts then you remember we developed the <code>results_score</code> function together during the first round. Now we get to use it in all of its released glory. <code>results_score</code> takes several arguments, <code>results</code>, which are the results to score, <code>events</code>, which are the events of interest, <code>meet_type</code>, which is either <code>\"timed_finals\"</code> or <code>\"prelims_finals\"</code>. The last three, <code>lanes</code>, <code>scoring_heats</code>, and <code>point_values</code> are fairly self-explanatory, as the number of lanes, scoring heats, and the point values for each place respectively.</p>\n<p>For the State_Off we’ve been using a <code>\"timed_finals\"</code> meet type, with 8 lanes, 2 scoring heats and point values per NFHS.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Results_Final <- results_score(\n  results = Results,\n  events = unique(Results$Event),\n  meet_type = \"timed_finals\",\n  lanes = 8,\n  scoring_heats = 2,\n  point_values = c(20, 17, 16, 15, 14, 13, 12, 11, 9, 7, 6, 5, 4, 3, 2, 1)\n)\n\nScores <- Results_Final %>%\n  group_by(State, Gender) %>%\n  summarise(Score = sum(Points))\n\nScores %>%\n  arrange(Gender, desc(Score)) %>%\n  ungroup() %>%\n  flextable_style()</code></pre>\n<div class=\"tabwid\">\n<table style='border-collapse:collapse;'>\n<thead>\n<tr style=\"overflow-wrap:break-word;\">\n<td style=\"width:47px;background-clip: id=\"swimmers-of-the-meet\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Swimmers of the Meet</h3>\n<p>Swimmer of the Meet criteria is the same as it’s been for the entire State-Off. First we’ll look for athletes who won two events, thereby scoring a the maximum possible forty points. We’ll also grab the All-American cuts to use as a tiebreaker, in case multiple athletes win two events. It’s possible this week we’ll have our first multiple Swimmer of the Meet winner – the suspense!</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Cuts_Link <-\n  \"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/gpilgrim2670/Pilgrim_Data/master/State_Cuts.csv\"\nCuts <- read.csv(url(Cuts_Link))\n\nCuts <- Cuts %>% # clean up Cuts\n  filter(Stroke %!in% c(\"MR\", \"FR\", \"11 Dives\")) %>% # %!in% is now included in SwimmeR\n  rename(Gender = Sex) %>%\n  mutate(\n    Event = case_when((Distance == 200 & #match events\n                         Stroke == &#39;Free&#39;) ~ \"200 Yard Freestyle\",\n                      (Distance == 200 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;IM&#39;) ~ \"200 Yard IM\",\n                      (Distance == 50 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;Free&#39;) ~ \"50 Yard Freestyle\",\n                      (Distance == 100 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;Fly&#39;) ~ \"100 Yard Butterfly\",\n                      (Distance == 100 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;Free&#39;) ~ \"100 Yard Freestyle\",\n                      (Distance == 500 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;Free&#39;) ~ \"500 Yard Freestyle\",\n                      (Distance == 100 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;Back&#39;) ~ \"100 Yard Backstroke\",\n                      (Distance == 100 &\n                         Stroke == &#39;Breast&#39;) ~ \"100 Yard Breaststroke\",\n                      TRUE ~ paste(Distance, \"Yard\", Stroke, sep = \" \")\n    ),\n    \n    Event = case_when(\n      Gender == \"M\" ~ paste(\"Boys\", Event, sep = \" \"),\n      Gender == \"F\" ~ paste(\"Girls\", Event, sep = \" \")\n    )\n  )\n\nInd_Swimming_Results <- Results_Final %>%\n  filter(str_detect(Event, \"Diving|Relay\") == FALSE) %>% # join Ind_Swimming_Results and Cuts\n  left_join(Cuts %>% filter((Gender == \"M\" &\n                               Year == 2020) |\n                              (Gender == \"F\" &\n                                 Year == 2019)) %>%\n              select(AAC_Cut, AA_Cut, Event),\n            by = &#39;Event&#39;)\n\nSwimmer_Of_Meet <- Ind_Swimming_Results %>%\n  mutate(\n    AA_Diff = (Finals_Time_sec - sec_format(AA_Cut)) / sec_format(AA_Cut),\n    Name = str_to_title(Name)\n  ) %>%\n  group_by(Name) %>%\n  filter(n() == 2) %>% # get swimmers that competed in two events\n  summarise(\n    Avg_Place = sum(Place) / 2,\n    AA_Diff_Avg = round(mean(AA_Diff, na.rm = TRUE), 3),\n    Gender = unique(Gender),\n    State = unique(State)\n  ) %>%\n  arrange(Avg_Place, AA_Diff_Avg) %>%\n  group_split(Gender) # split out a dataframe for boys (1) and girls (2)</code></pre>\n<p>\n</p>\n<hr />\n</div>\n<div id=\"boys\" class=\"section While she hasn’t written in to answer <a href=\"https://pilgrim.netlify.app/post/high-school-swimming-state-off-tournament-texas-2-vs-ohio-7/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">my questions about swimming outdoors in cold weather</a> she did tell <a href=\"https://swimswam.com/us-national-teamer-lillie-nordmann-defers-the-start-of-her-stanford-career/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">SwimSwam</a> that she’s postponing her collegiate career because of the ongoing pandemic. Also, it’s warmer in Texas than it is in Palo Alto (or nearby San Jose). Just saying. Actually I’m not just saying – I have data on the temperatures in <a href=\"https://w2.weather.gov/climate/xmacis.php?wfo=mtr\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">San Jose</a> and <a href=\"https://w2.weather.gov/climate/xmacis.php?wfo=hgx\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Houston</a>, from the National Weather Service. Let me show you what I’m talking about, in both Fahrenheit and Celsius (the State-Off has a surprisingly international audience).</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>San_Jose_Link <- \"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/gpilgrim2670/Pilgrim_Data/master/San_Jose_Temps.csv\"\nSan_Jose <- read.csv(url(San_Jose_Link)) %>% \n  mutate(City = \"San Jose\")\nHouston_Link <- \"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/gpilgrim2670/Pilgrim_Data/master/Houston_Temps.csv\"\nHouston <- read.csv(url(Houston_Link)) %>% \n  mutate(City = \"Houston\")\n\nTemps <- bind_rows(San_Jose, Houston)\n\nnames(Temps) <- c(\"Month\", \"Mean_Max\", \"Mean_Min\", \"Mean_Avg\", \"City\")\n\nTemps$Month <- factor(Temps$Month, ordered = TRUE, \n                                levels = unique(Temps$Month))\n\nTemps %>%\n  ggplot() +\n  geom_line(aes(\n    x = Month,\n    y = Mean_Avg,\n    group = City,\n    color = City\n  )) +\n  scale_y_continuous(sec.axis = sec_axis( ~ (. - 32) * (5/9), name = \"Monthly Mean Average Temperature (C)\")) +\n  theme_bw() +\n  theme(axis.text.x = element_text(angle = 45, hjust = 1)) +\n  labs(x = \"Month\", y = \"Monthly Mean Average Temperature (F)\")</code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/pilgrim.netlify.com/post/2020-09-02-high-school-swimming-state-off-tournament-texas-2-vs-florida-3_files/figure-html/weather-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Those are the monthly average temperatures. <code>SwimmeR 0.4.1</code> is that results now include a column called <code>DQ</code>. When <code>DQ == 1</code> a swimmer or relay has been disqualified. Recording DQs is of course important to the overall mission of <code>SwimmeR</code> (making swimming results available in usable formats etc. etc.), but it’s also of personal interest to me as an official.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Results %>%\n  group_by(State) %>%\n  summarise(DQs = sum(DQ, na.rm = TRUE) / n()) %>%\n  flextable_style()</code></pre>\n<div class=\"tabwid\">\n<table 1.00);background-color:transparent;\">3</span></p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n</div>\n<p>Not surprisingly most DQs are seen in relays. False starts can and do happen as teams push for every advantage. The line between a fast start and a false start is exceedingly fine. No one is trying to false start, it just happens sometimes. Breaststroke on the other hand is a disciple where intentional cheating can and sometimes does prosper. Recall <a href=\"https://sports.yahoo.com/blogs/olympics-fourth-place-medal/cheater-swimming-gold-medalist-admits-taking-illegal-kicks-162450535--oly.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Cameron van der Burgh</a> admitting to taking extra dolphin kicks after winning gold in Rio, or <a href=\"https://www.espn.com/olympics/summer04/swimming/news/story?id=1859947\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Kosuke Kitajima</a> having “no response” to Aaron Peirsol’s accusation (backed up by video) that he took an illegal dolphin kick en route to gold in Beijing. Breaststrokers also seem to struggle with the modern “brush” turn, and ring up a lot of one-hand touches. The Texas results (<a href=\"https://www.uiltexas.org/tournament-results/swimming-diving/2020/5a/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">5A</a>, <a href=\"https://www.uiltexas.org/tournament-results/swimming-diving/2020/6a/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">6A</a>) actually say what infraction was committed, and it’s the usual mix of kicking violations and one hand touches. Florida doesn’t share reasons for DQs, but just from my own experience – kicking and one hand touches. Must be in the water. Or not, because butterflyers don’t seem to suffer from the same issues. Could be the the butterfly recovery is more difficult to stop short, or to do in an unbalanced fashion such that only one hand makes contact with the wall? I don’t know, it’s an genuine mystery, but it is nice that my personal observations from officiating are borne out in the data.</p>\n<hr />\n</div>\n<div id=\"plotting-times-with-swimmer\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Plotting Times With SwimmeR</h3>\n<p>We might be interested in seeing a distribution of times for a particular event. There’s a problem though. Swimming times are traditionally reported as minutes:seconds.hundredths. In <code>R</code> that means they’re stored as character strings, which can be confirmed by calling <code>str(Results$Finals_Time)</code>. We’d like to be able to look at times from fastest to slowest though – that is we’d like them to have an order. One option would be to convert the character strings in <code>Results$Finals_Time</code> to an ordered factor, but that would be awful. We’d likely need to manually define the ordering – no fun at all. <code>SwimmeR</code> instead offers a pair of functions to make this easier. The function <code>sec_format</code> will take a time written as minutes:seconds.hundredths, like the ones in <code>Results$Finals_Time</code> and covert it to a numeric value – the total duration in seconds. Ordering numerics is easy, <code>R</code> handles it automatically. While that’s very nice, we as swimming aficionados would still like to look at times in the standard swimming format. This is where <code>mmss_format</code> comes in – it simply coverts times back to the standard minutes:seconds.hundredths for our viewing pleasure. Below is an example of converting times to numeric with <code>sec_format</code> for plotting, and then converting the axis labels to swimming format with <code>mmss_format</code> for viewing.</p>\n<pre class=\"r\"><code>Results %>%\n  filter(Gender == &#39;Girls&#39;,\n         str_detect(Event, \"Relay\") == FALSE) %>% \n  left_join(Cuts %>% filter(Year == 2019, Gender == \"F\"), by = c(\"Event\")) %>% # add in All-American cuts\n  filter(Event == \"Girls 200 Yard Freestyle\") %>% # pick event\n  mutate(Finals_Time = sec_format(Finals_Time)) %>% # change time format to seconds\n  ggplot() +\n  geom_histogram(aes(x = Finals_Time, fill = State), binwidth = 0.5) +\ngeom_vline(aes(xintercept = sec_format(AA_Cut))) + # line for All-American cut\n  geom_vline(aes(xintercept = sec_format(AAC_Cut))) + # line for All-American consideration cut\n  scale_y_continuous(breaks = seq(0, 10, 2)) +\n  scale_x_continuous(\n    breaks = seq(100, 150, 5),\n    labels = scales::trans_format(\"identity\", mmss_format) # labels in swimming format\n  ) +\n    geom_label(\n    label=\"All-American\",\n    x = 107,\n    y = 7,\n    color = \"black\"\n  ) +\n      geom_label(\n    label=\"All-American Cons.\",\n    x = 110.5,\n    y = 10,\n    color = \"black\"\n  ) +\n  theme_bw() +\n  labs(y = \"No. of Swimmers\",\n       x = \"Finals Time\")</code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/pilgrim.netlify.com/post/2020-09-02-high-school-swimming-state-off-tournament-texas-2-vs-florida-3_files/figure-html/spread-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Here we see classic “bell” curves for each state, with a few very fast or very slow (comparatively speaking) swimmers, and a bunch of athletes clumped right in the middle.\n</p>\n<hr />\n</div>\n<div id=\"in-closing\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>In Closing</h3>\n<p>Texas wins the overall, despite a winning effort from the Florida girls. Next week at <a href=\"https://pilgrim.netlify.app/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Swimming + Data Science</a> we’ll have number one seed California (1) vs. number five Pennsylvania (5). We’ll also dig further into new <code>SwimmeR 0.4.1</code> functionality. Hope to see you then!</p>\n</div>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = d.createElement(t);\n 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Train and analyze many models for #TidyTuesday crop yields","link":"https://juliasilge.com/blog/crop-yields/","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://juliasilge.com/blog/crop-yields/\"> rstats | Julia Silge</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>Lately I’ve been publishing<br />\n<a href=\"https://juliasilge.com/category/tidymodels/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">screencasts</a> demonstrating how to use the<br />\n<a href=\"https://www.tidymodels.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">tidymodels</a> framework, from just getting started to tuning more complex models. Today’s screencast explores how to fluently apply tidy data principles to the task of building many models using with this week’s<br />\n<a href=\"https://github.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>#TidyTuesday</code> dataset</a> on crop yields. <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f33d.png\" alt=\"🌽\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /></p>\n<p><!--html_preserve--></p>\n<div style=\"position: relative; padding-bottom: 56.25%; height: 0; overflow: hidden;\">\n  <iframe src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/rhhuNGjj3cU\" style=\"position: absolute; top: 0; left: 0; width: 100%; height: 100%; border:0;\" allowfullscreen title=\"YouTube Video\"></iframe>\n</div>\n<p><!--/html_preserve--><br />\n</br></p>\n<p>Here is the code I used in the video, for those who prefer reading instead of or in addition to video.</p>\n<h2 id=\"explore-the-data\">Explore the data</h2>\n<p>Our modeling goal is to estimate how<br />\n<a href=\"https://github.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday/blob/master/data/2020/2020-09-01/readme.md\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">crops yields are changing around the world using this week’s #TidyTuesday dataset</a>. We can build many models for the country-crop combinations we are interested in.</p>\n<p>First, let’s read in two of the datasets for this week.</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">library(tidyverse)\n\nkey_crop_yields <- read_csv(\"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday/master/data/2020/2020-09-01/key_crop_yields.csv\")\nland_use <- read_csv(\"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday/master/data/2020/2020-09-01/land_use_vs_yield_change_in_cereal_production.csv\")\n</code></pre>\n<p>I’m going to use the <code>land_use</code> dataset only to find the top population countries. Let’s create a vector of their names.</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">top_countries <- land_use %>%\n  janitor::clean_names() %>%\n  filter(!is.na(code), entity != \"World\") %>%\n  group_by(entity) %>%\n  filter(year == max(year)) %>%\n  ungroup() %>%\n  slice_max(total_population_gapminder, n = 30) %>%\n  pull(entity)\n\ntop_countries\n</code></pre>\n<pre><code>##  [1] \"China\"                        \"India\"                       \n##  [3] \"United States\"                \"Indonesia\"                   \n##  [5] \"Pakistan\"                     \"Brazil\"                      \n##  [7] \"Nigeria\"                      \"Bangladesh\"                  \n##  [9] \"Russia\"                       \"Mexico\"                      \n## [11] \"Japan\"                        \"Ethiopia\"                    \n## [13] \"Philippines\"                  \"Egypt\"                       \n## [15] \"Vietnam\"                      \"Democratic Republic of Congo\"\n## [17] \"Germany\"                      \"Turkey\"                      \n## [19] \"Iran\"                         \"Thailand\"                    \n## [21] \"United Kingdom\"               \"France\"                      \n## [23] \"Italy\"                        \"South Africa\"                \n## [25] \"Tanzania\"                     \"Myanmar\"                     \n## [27] \"Kenya\"                        \"South Korea\"                 \n## [29] \"Colombia\"                     \"Spain\"\n</code></pre>\n<p>Now let’s create a tidy version of the crop yields data, for the countries and crops I am interested in.</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">tidy_yields <- key_crop_yields %>%\n  janitor::clean_names() %>%\n  pivot_longer(wheat_tonnes_per_hectare:bananas_tonnes_per_hectare,\n    names_to = \"crop\", values_to = \"yield\"\n  ) %>%\n  mutate(crop = str_remove(crop, \"_tonnes_per_hectare\")) %>%\n  filter(\n    crop %in% c(\"wheat\", \"rice\", \"maize\", \"barley\"),\n    entity %in% top_countries,\n    !is.na(yield)\n  )\n\ntidy_yields\n</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 6,032 x 5\n##    entity     code   year crop   yield\n##    <chr>      <chr> <dbl> <chr>  <dbl>\n##  1 Bangladesh BGD    1961 wheat  0.574\n##  2 Bangladesh BGD    1961 rice   1.70 \n##  3 Bangladesh BGD    1961 maize  0.799\n##  4 Bangladesh BGD    1961 barley 0.577\n##  5 Bangladesh BGD    1962 wheat  0.675\n##  6 Bangladesh BGD    1962 rice   1.53 \n##  7 Bangladesh BGD    1962 maize  0.738\n##  8 Bangladesh BGD    1962 barley 0.544\n##  9 Bangladesh BGD    1963 wheat  0.607\n## 10 Bangladesh BGD    1963 rice   1.77 \n## # … with 6,022 more rows\n</code></pre>\n<p>This data structure is just right for plotting <strong>crop yield over time</strong>!</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">tidy_yields %>%\n  ggplot(aes(year, yield, color = crop)) +\n  geom_line(alpha = 0.7, size = 1.5) +\n  geom_point() +\n  facet_wrap(~entity, ncol = 5) +\n  scale_x_continuous(guide = guide_axis(angle = 90)) +\n  labs(x = NULL, y = \"yield (tons per hectare)\")\n</code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/juliasilge.com/blog/crop-yields/index_files/figure-html/unnamed-chunk-5-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Notice that not all countries produce all crops, but that overall crop yields are <em>increasing</em>.</p>\n<h2 id=\"many-models\">Many models</h2>\n<p>Now let’s fit a linear model to each country-crop combination.</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">library(tidymodels)\n\ntidy_lm <- tidy_yields %>%\n  nest(yields = c(year, yield)) %>%\n  mutate(model = map(yields, ~ lm(yield ~ year, data = .x)))\n\ntidy_lm\n</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 111 x 5\n##    entity     code  crop   yields            model \n##    <chr>      <chr> <chr>  <list>            <list>\n##  1 Bangladesh BGD   wheat  <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  2 Bangladesh BGD   rice   <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  3 Bangladesh BGD   maize  <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  4 Bangladesh BGD   barley <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  5 Brazil     BRA   wheat  <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  6 Brazil     BRA   rice   <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  7 Brazil     BRA   maize  <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  8 Brazil     BRA   barley <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n##  9 China      CHN   wheat  <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n## 10 China      CHN   rice   <tibble [58 × 2]> <lm>  \n## # … with 101 more rows\n</code></pre>\n<p>Next, let’s <code>tidy()</code> those models to get out the coefficients, and adjust the p-values for multiple comparisons while we’re at it.</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">slopes <- tidy_lm %>%\n  mutate(coefs = map(model, tidy)) %>%\n  unnest(coefs) %>%\n  filter(term == \"year\") %>%\n  mutate(p.value = p.adjust(p.value))\n\nslopes\n</code></pre>\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 111 x 10\n##    entity  code  crop  yields  model term  estimate std.error statistic  p.value\n##    <chr>   <chr> <chr> <list>  <lis> <chr>    <dbl>     <dbl>     <dbl>    <dbl>\n##  1 Bangla… BGD   wheat <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0389   0.00253      15.4  5.11e-20\n##  2 Bangla… BGD   rice  <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0600   0.00231      26.0  6.05e-31\n##  3 Bangla… BGD   maize <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.122    0.0107       11.3  1.82e-14\n##  4 Bangla… BGD   barl… <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.00505  0.000596      8.47 4.34e-10\n##  5 Brazil  BRA   wheat <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0366   0.00222      16.5  2.55e-21\n##  6 Brazil  BRA   rice  <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0755   0.00490      15.4  4.96e-20\n##  7 Brazil  BRA   maize <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0709   0.00395      18.0  4.37e-23\n##  8 Brazil  BRA   barl… <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0466   0.00319      14.6  5.05e-19\n##  9 China   CHN   wheat <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0880   0.00141      62.6  1.72e-51\n## 10 China   CHN   rice  <tibbl… <lm>  year   0.0843   0.00289      29.2  1.47e-33\n## # … with 101 more rows\n</code></pre>\n<h2 id=\"explore-results\">Explore results</h2>\n<p>Now we can visualize the results of this modeling, which is estimating how crop yields are changing around the world.</p>\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">library(ggrepel)\nslopes %>%\n  ggplot(aes(estimate, p.value, label = entity)) +\n  geom_vline(\n    xintercept = 0, lty = 2,\n    size = 1.5, alpha = 0.7, color = \"gray50\"\n  ) +\n  geom_point(aes(color = crop), alpha = 0.8, size = 2.5, show.legend = FALSE) +\n  scale_y_log10() +\n  facet_wrap(~crop) +\n  geom_text_repel(size = 3, family = \"IBMPlexSans\") +\n  theme_light(base_family = \"IBMPlexSans\") +\n  theme(strip.text = element_text(family = \"IBMPlexSans-Bold\", size = 12)) +\n  labs(x = \"increase in tons per hectare per year\")\n</code></pre>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/juliasilge.com/blog/crop-yields/index_files/figure-html/unnamed-chunk-8-1.png?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<ul>\n<li>\n<p>On the x-axis is the slope of these models. Notice that most countries are on the positive side, with increasing crop yields. The further to the right a country is, the larger the increase in crop yield over this time period. Corn yields have increased the most.</p>\n</li>\n<li>\n<p>On the y-axis is the p-value, a measure of how surprising the effect we see is under the assumption of no relationship (no change with time). Countries lower in the plots have smaller p-values; we are more certain those are real relationships.</p>\n</li>\n</ul>\n<p>We can extend this to check out how well these models fit the data with <code>glance()</code>. This approach for using statistical models to estimate changes in many subgroups at once has been so helpful to me in many situations!</p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>I’ve added a worked <code>R</code> example of the non-convexity, with respect to model parameters, of square loss of a sigmoid-derived prediction <a href=\"https://github.com/WinVector/Examples/blob/main/WhyNotSquareError/Square_Error_Example.md\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://github.com/WinVector/Examples/blob/main/WhyNotSquareError/Square_Error_Example.md\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://winvector.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/unnamed-chunk-6-1.png?w=450&#038;h=428&#038;fit=599%2C428\" alt=\"Unnamed chunk 6 1\" title=\"unnamed-chunk-6-1.png\" border=\"0\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>This is finishing an example for our <code>Python</code> note <a href=\"https://github.com/WinVector/Examples/blob/main/WhyNotSquareError/why_deviance.ipynb\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">“Why not Square Error for Classification?”</a>.  Reading <a href=\"https://github.com/WinVector/Examples/blob/main/WhyNotSquareError/why_deviance.ipynb\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">that note</a> will give a usable context and background for this diagram.</p>\n<p>The undesirable property is: such a graph says that a parameter value of <code>b = -1</code> and <code>b = -0.25</code> have similar losses, but parameters values in-between are worse. This might seem paradoxical, but it is an artifiact of the loss-function – not an actual property of the data or model. The <a href=\"https://github.com/WinVector/Examples/blob/main/WhyNotSquareError/why_deviance.ipynb\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">same note</a> shows the deviance loss has the desirable convex property: interpolations of good parameter values are also good.</p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 01 Sep 2020 10:52:18 +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"https://winvector.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/unnamed-chunk-6-1.png?w=450&h=428&fit=599%2C428","linkMd5":"a155e7da1172acc6c3cba59242c9fd4a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn23@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-08-924_2ec310110de7698e.webp","destWidth":450,"destHeight":322,"sourceBytes":9877,"destBytes":6078,"author":"jmount","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/92a7340d55e6770e6659e961a10909e4?s=96&d=identicon&r=G","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://winvector.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/unnamed-chunk-6-1.png?w=450&h=428&fit=599%2C428":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn23@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-08-924_2ec310110de7698e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/yD-K2wALd8A":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn92@2020_6/2020/09/05/06-27-16-202_fbc0c3630ffa5744.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287180038},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Deploying flexdashboard on Github Pages","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/RBloggers/~3/Om7uKkq3dgU/","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://ramikrispin.github.io/2020/09/deploying-flexdashboard-on-github-pages/\"> Rami Krispin</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>One of Github’s coolest features is <a href=\"https://pages.github.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Github Pages</a>, which enables you to create and deploy websites under github.com domain for free. The most common usage of Github Pages is the deployment of project documentation. The R community is widely using it to deploy different <a href=\"https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R Markdown</a> formats such as package documentation with <a href=\"https://pkgdown.r-lib.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">pkgdown</a>, blogs with <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">blogdown</a>, books with <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">bookdown</a>, etc. Recently, as I was looking for a way to share Covid19 data, I had this eureka moment realizing that it should be straightforward to deploy <a href=\"https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com/flexdashboard/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">flexdashboard</a> or any other format of R Markdown (none Shiny) on Github Pages. After a short googling, I come across this <a href=\"https://github.com/pbatey/flexdashboard-example\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">example</a> by Phil Batey. Since then, I started to utlized this feature in some of my packages (visualizing Covid19 datasets). Here are some use cases of such dashboards:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>The <a href=\"https://ramikrispin.github.io/coronavirus_dashboard/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">coronavirus dashboard</a>, supporting the <a href=\"https://github.com/RamiKrispin/coronavirus\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">coronavirus</a> package</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://github.com/RamiKrispin/italy_dash\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Covid19 Italy</a> dashboard, supporting the <a href=\"https://github.com/RamiKrispin/covid19italy\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">covid19italy</a> package</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://ramikrispin.github.io/uswildfire/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">US Wildfire</a> dashboard, tracking the ongoing wildfire in the US</li>\n</ul>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-03-deploying-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/corona_dash.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>This post provides a short tutorial for deploying flexdashboard on Github Pages.</p>\n<div id=\"flexdashboard-on-github-pages\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Flexdashboard on Github Pages</h3>\n<p>The flexdashboard package provides a customized format for building interactive dashboards. It is a simplistic, useful, and fast method for developing a static dashboard that does not require a big data or back-end server (although you can use flexdashboard with Shiny to create a dynamic dashboard with back-end server support). To deploy flexdashboard on Github Pages, you will need:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Github repository</li>\n<li>flexdashboard and rmarkdown packages</li>\n<li>A flexdashboard file (i.e., <code>Rmd</code>)</li>\n<li><code>_site.yml</code> file</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<div id=\"simple-example\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Simple example</h3>\n<p>The following example demonstrates the deployment process of flexdashboard on Github Pages. We will start by creating a new repository for the deployment (can also deploy on an existing repository as well) using the Github web interface:</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/new_repo.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Note that the repository must be public to expose it on a Github page. We will set the name, keep all the checkboxes unchecked, and create the repository.</p>\n<p>The next step is to create a local folder and sync it with the <code>origin</code> on Github. There are few methods for doing it, such as local file manager (e.g., Finder on Mac, File Explorer on Windows, etc.), terminal (or command line), RStudio, etc. For simplicity, I will use here RStudio by creating new project by following those steps:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Clicking the <code>Project:</code> drop-down button on the top right (see the blue rectangle on the first screenshot)</li>\n<li>Select the <code>New Project…</code> option</li>\n<li>Select the first option, <code>New Directory</code>, on the <code>New Project</code> window (screenshot below)</li>\n</ul>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/new_project.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<ul>\n<li>Set the folder path and name, leave the checkboxes unchecked, and click the <code>create project</code> button</li>\n</ul>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/new_project_folder.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Before we sync the new folder with the <code>origin</code> on Github, let’s create the dashboard. We will start by creating the <code>_site.yml</code> file. The <code>_site.yml</code> is a setting file that enables us to configure the R Markdown file’s rendering options. Creating this file can be done by clicking on the top left menu <code>File</code> -> <code>New File</code> -> <code>Text File</code>, which will pop-up a new file on the source pane, and we will save it as <code>_site.yml</code>. As typically, Github pages is rendering the website from the <code>docs</code> folder, we will set the R Markdown HTML output to the docs folder. Paste the following code and save it:</p>\n<pre class=\"bash\"><code>name: \"flexdashboard-example\"\noutput_dir: docs</code></pre>\n<p>More details about the <code>_site.yml</code> file available <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/rmarkdown-site.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>.</p>\n<p>Next, we will use the built-in flexdashboard template by selecting on Rstudio on the top left options <code>File</code> -> <code>New File</code> -> <code>R Markdown</code>. The <strong>New R Markdown</strong> window should pop-up, select the <code>From Template</code> option on the left, and then choose the <code>Flex Dashboard</code> template. Note that you probably won’t have this template available on this template menu if the <strong>flexdashboard</strong> package is not installed.</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/markdown%20temp.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Once creating the template, a new file will pop-up on the source pane named <code>Untitled1</code> (or <code>Untitiledn</code> if more n-1 un-named files are already opened). To run the dashboard as a website, we will name the file as <code>index.Rmd</code> (saving it on RStudio as <code>index</code> will automatically name it as <code>index.Rmd</code>), which will render it into <code>index.html</code> file. By default, the dashboard template comes with a layout of two empty columns (where the second column splitted into two raws). For making this example more appealing, I will drop three interactive plots from the <strong>Plotly</strong> package <a href=\"https://plotly.com/r/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">documentation</a>.</p>\n<p>Now we are ready to render the dashboard! Click on the <code>Knit</code> option (under the file name tab on the source pane), and this is the output you should expect (just without the plots which I added for the example):</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/render%20dash.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>You can noticed on the screenshot above that the dashboard file name (on the dashboard top right) is <code>index.html</code>. In addition, the <code>HTML</code> output was rendered automatically into the <code>docs</code> folder (see the folder on the buttom right of the screenshot) as we set on the <code>_site.yml</code> file before.</p>\n<p>The last step of the deployment is to sync the local repo with the <code>origin</code> and set the website. To set the <code>git</code> you can use the repository details from Github to linked it with the local folder:</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/setting%20repo.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>We will init a git work space on the local folder we created for the project, and commit the folder content by using the origin address from the red box above:</p>\n<pre class=\"bash\"><code>git init\ngit add *\ngit commit -m \"init commit\"\ngit remote add origin git@github.com:RamiKrispin/flexdashboard_example.git\ngit push -u origin master</code></pre>\n<p>Now, if all went well, you should see the content of the local folder on the Github repository. The last step would be to set the Github Page. Go to the repository <code>settings</code> -> <code>options</code> -> <code>Github Pages</code> and set the page source by selecting the <code>master</code> branch and the <code>docs</code> folder, and save the changes. Once saved, you should get the deployed Github Page URL on top:</p>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/github%20pages.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>And… it deployed!</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://ramikrispin.github.io/flexdashboard_example/\" class=\"uri\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://ramikrispin.github.io/flexdashboard_example/</a></p>\n<p><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/ramikrispin.github.io/post/2020-09-02-deploy-flexdashboard-on-github-pages_files/dashboard.png?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Note that the page URL is a combination of <code>https://Your_Github_User_Name.github.io/Repository_Name/</code></p>\n</div>\n<div id=\"resources\" class=\"section level3\">\n<h3>Resources</h3>\n<p>Here are some useful resources for deploying flexdashboard on Github Pages:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Deployment code – <a href=\"https://github.com/RamiKrispin/flexdashboard_example\" class=\"uri\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://github.com/RamiKrispin/flexdashboard_example</a></li>\n<li>Flexdashboard – <a href=\"https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com/flexdashboard/\" class=\"uri\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com/flexdashboard/</a></li>\n<li>R Markdown: The Definitive Guide – <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/\" class=\"uri\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/</a>, and in particular <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/rmarkdown-site.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">chapter 10.5</a> for setting the <code>_site.yml</code> file</li>\n<li>Github Pages – <a href=\"https://pages.github.com/\" class=\"uri\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">https://pages.github.com/</a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var frm,\n   xvar_names = qc(x1, x2, x3),\n   truthVar = \"yC\", truthTarget = TRUE,\n   title = \"Example ROC list plot\")\n</pre>\n<p><a
