alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 04 Sep 2020 12:07:15 +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"https://winvector.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/untitledimage.png?w=450&h=386&fit=599%2C386","linkMd5":"5ff2c46e310befcff9380f865dd72427","destWidth":450,"destHeight":290,"sourceBytes":34551,"destBytes":8838,"author":"jmount","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"https://0.gravatar.com/avatar/92a7340d55e6770e6659e961a10909e4?s=96&d=identicon&r=G","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://winvector.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/untitledimage.png?w=450&h=386&fit=599%2C386":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/2D_cURO0tH8":null},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287179927},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","title":"Correcting for confounded variables with GLMs","link":"http://www.seascapemodels.org/rstats/2020/09/04/correcting-with-glms.html","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"http://www.seascapemodels.org/rstats/2020/09/04/correcting-with-glms.html\"> Bluecology blog</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<h1 id=\"correcting-for-confounded-variables-with-glms\">Correcting for confounded variables with GLMs</h1>\n<p>General (and generalized) linear models can be useful for analyzing<br />\nfield data, where sampling is often distributed unevenly across different<br />\nenvironmental gradients or treatment groups. They help us correct for<br />\nconfounded gradients and discover effects that are hidden in plots of<br />\nraw data.</p>\n<p>For instance, we used GLMs in a meta-analysis of the rates species are shifting their ranges under climate change. We used the GLMs to correct for <a href=\"https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/gcb.13184\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">differences in ways different studies had measured species ranges</a>, so we could then study the unique effects of ecological variables.</p>\n<p>You can also think of these GLMs with multiple covariates as ‘statistically’ (rather than experimentally) controlling for the effects of each variable when looking at the effects of the other variables.</p>\n<p>In this post I’ll demonstrate this application for statistical controls  with a simple example.</p>\n<h2 id=\"simulate-data\">Simulate data</h2>\n<p>We’ll start by simulating some data. Let’s say the data represent fish<br />\nabundance at sites. Fish vary in the area of fish habitat available at<br />\nthat site. There are also two bioregions we’ve sampled. We’ll assume the<br />\nfish only occur on one bioregion, even though habitat is available in<br />\nboth bioregions.</p>\n<p>We’ll assume data are poisson distributed, to account for the fact that<br />\nfish numbers can’t be <0, and that they should be integers.</p>\n<p>To simulate the data we will specify an intercept and an habitat effect<br />\n<code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">b_habitat</code>. We’ll assume two bioregions, so we only need to specify one<br />\nbioregion effect (which is the mean difference from bioregion B and A)</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>nsites <- 88\n\n#Effects\nintercept <- 0\nb_habitat <- 1\nb_region <- -20\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Then we need to make up the covariate data.</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>set.seed(50)\nregion <- c(rep(0, nsites/2), rep(1, nsites/2))\n\nhabitat_area <- rnorm(nsites, 1.5, 0.5)\nhabitat_area <- habitat_area*(region + 1)\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>We made it so habitat area was on average twice as big in the second<br />\nregion.</p>\n<p>Now assemble a ‘design matrix’ (<code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">X</code>) and matrix of effects (ie our ).<br />\nThese help us then make predictions of mean abundance with a simple<br />\nmatrix multiplication:</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>coefs_true <- matrix(c(intercept, b_habitat, b_region))\nX <- matrix(cbind(1, habitat_area, region), ncol = 3)\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Now make the ‘true’ means for our simulation and the fish abundance<br />\ndata:</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>y_mean <- exp(X %*% coefs_true)\ny <- rpois(nsites, y_mean)\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>We took an exponent to ensure positive values.</p>\n<p>If this confuses you so far, you can read more about <a href=\"http://www.seascapemodels.org/rstats/2018/01/19/intro-to-glms.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">linear models</a> and then <a href=\"http://www.seascapemodels.org/rstats/2018/10/16/understanding-the-glm-link.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">generalized linear models</a> on my other blogs.</p>\n<h2 id=\"plot-simulations\">Plot simulations</h2>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>plot(habitat_area, y)\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-5-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>So not strong evidence of any effect of habitat area on abundance. What<br />\nabout if we colour points by bioregion?</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>plot(habitat_area, y, col = region + 1)\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-6-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>So it appears the red region (the region with <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">1</code> in the region<br />\nvariable) has not habitat relationship, whereas the black region (region<br />\nwith <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">0</code> in region variable) has a positive relationship. This occurs<br />\nbecause we set the region intercept to -20 (and a poisson with mean<br />\nexp(-20) will predict basically always zero).</p>\n<h2 id=\"naive-glm\">Naive GLM</h2>\n<p>Now let’s fit a GLM assuming we don’t know about bioregion</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>m1 <- glm(y ~ habitat_area, family = \"poisson\")\nsummary(m1)\n\n##\n## Call:\n## glm(formula = y ~ habitat_area, family = \"poisson\")\n##\n## Deviance Residuals:\n##    Min      1Q  Median      3Q     Max  \n## -3.071  -1.783  -1.281   0.229   7.194  \n##\n## Coefficients:\n##              Estimate Std. Error z value Pr(>|z|)    \n## (Intercept)   1.91396    0.15321  12.493  < 2e-16 ***\n## habitat_area -0.53476    0.08272  -6.464 1.02e-10 ***\n## ---\n## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1\n##\n## (Dispersion parameter for poisson family taken to be 1)\n##\n##     Null deviance: 401.78  on 87  degrees of freedom\n## Residual deviance: 354.11  on 86  degrees of freedom\n## AIC: 501.23\n##\n## Number of Fisher Scoring iterations: 7\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Habitat is significant (p < 0.001), but the estimate is in the wrong<br />\ndirection! It is saying there are fewer fish with more habitat. This is<br />\nbecause the second region has on average twice as much habitat, but our<br />\nfish doesn’t ever occur there.</p>\n<p>To see this, we can do a plot with the <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">visreg</code> package:</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>library(visreg)\nvisreg(m1, scale = \"response\")\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-8-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>So predicting an effect of habitat area that decreases with habitat<br />\narea.</p>\n<h2 id=\"region-adjusted-glm\">Region adjusted GLM</h2>\n<p>Ok, so now try including region:</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>m2 <- glm(y ~ habitat_area + region, family = \"poisson\")\nsummary(m2)\n\n##\n## Call:\n## glm(formula = y ~ habitat_area + region, family = \"poisson\")\n##\n## Deviance Residuals:\n##      Min        1Q    Median        3Q       Max  \n## -2.43472  -0.26179  -0.00006  -0.00002   2.46518  \n##\n## Coefficients:\n##               Estimate Std. Error z value Pr(>|z|)    \n## (Intercept)     0.2340     0.2294   1.020    0.308    \n## habitat_area    0.8851     0.1319   6.713 1.91e-11 ***\n## region        -23.0326  2100.7940  -0.011    0.991    \n## ---\n## Signif. codes:  0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1\n##\n## (Dispersion parameter for poisson family taken to be 1)\n##\n##     Null deviance: 401.777  on 87  degrees of freedom\n## Residual deviance:  54.311  on 85  degrees of freedom\n## AIC: 203.43\n##\n## Number of Fisher Scoring iterations: 18\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Habitat area is significant and positive now. Note also the effect size<br />\n(0.885) is within the error bounds (SE = 0.132) for the true effect size<br />\n(=1). Let’s also confirm our better model (most parsimonious) with the<br />\nAIC (<a href=\"http://www.seascapemodels.org/rstats/2018/04/13/how-to-use-the-AIC.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">you can read more about what the AIC is on this blog</a>).</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>AIC(m1, m2)\n\n##    df      AIC\n## m1  2 501.2268\n## m2  3 203.4282\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>So there’s basically no evidence for the model without bioregion<br />\ncompared to the model with that effect.</p>\n<p>But notice that the region effect is approximately correct (-23), but<br />\nthe standard error is huge and, consequently, the region effect is<br />\nnon-signficant (p = 0.99). This happens because the abundance is always<br />\nzero in region 2, so the poisson model can’t get a good estimate of the<br />\nmean abundance. Put another way, the poisson must have mean >0, but<br />\nit can’t distinguish between a mean of 0.000000001 versus say 0.0001<br />\n(they both predict basically all zeros), so the SE are very broad.</p>\n<p>We see this if we do a plot, and habitat area appears insignificant:</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>visreg(m2, xvar = \"habitat_area\")\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-11-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Now replot the predictions, asking for habitat area just in the first<br />\nbioregion (labelled <code class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">0</code> in the data):</p>\n<div class=\"language-plaintext highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code>visreg(m2, xvar = \"habitat_area\",\n       cond = list(region = 0), scale = \"response\")\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><img src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-12-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>Now we see the trend.</p>\n<p>So our GLM has corrected for the absence of fish in bioregion 2 and<br />\ncorrectly identified the positive relationship between abundance and<br />\nfish biomass. The GLM with the region effect also gets the magnitude of<br />\nthe trend approximately correct (within the margin of error from the<br />\ntrue value).</p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n s.defer = true;\n//          s.src = '//cdn.viglink.com/api/vglnk.js'; \n\t\t\ts.src = 'https://www.r-bloggers.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/vglnk.js';\n        var r = d.getElementsByTagName(t)[0];\n            r.parentNode.insertBefore(s, r);\n    }(document, 'script'));\n</script>\n\t\t\n<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><div style=\"border: 1px solid; width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 03 Sep 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-5-1.png?w=578","linkMd5":"d4ea548e31b3e5f918ec56167a0f56f7","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn47@2020_2/2020/09/05/06-27-09-966_470e987d4693dc24.webp","destWidth":578,"destHeight":412,"sourceBytes":5444,"destBytes":5444,"author":"Bluecology blog","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-5-1.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn47@2020_2/2020/09/05/06-27-09-966_470e987d4693dc24.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-6-1.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn84@2020_2/2020/09/05/06-27-11-251_5f25a81ae1984b8f.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-8-1.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn80@2020_2/2020/09/05/06-27-11-246_225fb25b71c30799.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-11-1.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn100@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-14-045_220229b08bc56074.webp","https://i0.wp.com/www.seascapemodels.org/images/blogs-2020/correcting-with-glms_files/figure-markdown_strict/unnamed-chunk-12-1.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn53@2020_4/2020/09/05/06-27-17-235_e0b5eed4868eb560.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/9nK7gKAcgE4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn87@2020_5/2020/09/05/06-27-11-219_678ece9052e515e7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287179982},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","title":"Using dplyr::filter when the condition is a string","link":"http://statisticaloddsandends.wordpress.com/?p=3723","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://statisticaloddsandends.wordpress.com/2020/09/03/using-dplyrfilter-when-the-condition-is-a-string/\"> R – Statistical Odds &#38; Ends</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>This took me a while to figure out and so I thought I would post this as future reference. Let’s say I have the <code>mtcars</code> data and I want to filter for just the rows with <code>cyl == 6</code>. I would do something like this:</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nlibrary(tidyverse)\ndata(mtcars)\nmtcars %>% filter(cyl == 6)\n\n#                 mpg cyl  disp  hp drat    wt  qsec vs am gear carb\n# Mazda RX4      21.0   6 160.0 110 3.90 2.620 16.46  0  1    4    4\n# Mazda RX4 Wag  21.0   6 160.0 110 3.90 2.875 17.02  0  1    4    4\n# Hornet 4 Drive 21.4   6 258.0 110 3.08 3.215 19.44  1  0    3    1\n# Valiant        18.1   6 225.0 105 2.76 3.460 20.22  1  0    3    1\n# Merc 280       19.2   6 167.6 123 3.92 3.440 18.30  1  0    4    4\n# Merc 280C      17.8   6 167.6 123 3.92 3.440 18.90  1  0    4    4\n# Ferrari Dino   19.7   6 145.0 175 3.62 2.770 15.50  0  1    5    6\n</pre>\n<p><em><strong>What if I had the filter condition as a string instead?</strong></em> The code below doesn’t work:</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nfilter_string <- \"cyl == 6\"\nmtcars %>% filter(filter_string)\n\n# Error: Problem with `filter()` input `..1`.\n# x Input `..1` must be a logical vector, not a character.\n#   Input `..1` is `filter_string`.\n# Run `rlang::last_error()` to see where the error occurred.\n</pre>\n<p>This is one possible solution:</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nmtcars %>% filter(!! rlang::parse_expr(filter_string))\n\n#                 mpg cyl  disp  hp drat    wt  qsec vs am gear carb\n# Mazda RX4      21.0   6 160.0 110 3.90 2.620 16.46  0  1    4    4\n# Mazda RX4 Wag  21.0   6 160.0 110 3.90 2.875 17.02  0  1    4    4\n# Hornet 4 Drive 21.4   6 258.0 110 3.08 3.215 19.44  1  0    3    1\n# Valiant        18.1   6 225.0 105 2.76 3.460 20.22  1  0    3    1\n# Merc 280       19.2   6 167.6 123 3.92 3.440 18.30  1  0    4    4\n# Merc 280C      17.8   6 167.6 123 3.92 3.440 18.90  1  0    4    4\n# Ferrari Dino   19.7   6 145.0 175 3.62 2.770 15.50  0  1    5    6\n</pre>\n<p>This can be useful if you are trying to running several different filters automatically. In the following contrived example, I want to compute the mean MPG for two different slices of the data:</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nfilters <- c(\"carb == 4\", \"am == 1\")\nfor (filter in filters) {\n  print(paste0(\"Mean mpg for \",\n               filter,\n               \": \",\n               mtcars %>% filter(!! rlang::parse_expr(filter)) %>%\n                 summarize(mean_mpg = mean(mpg)) %>%\n                 pull()))\n\n# [1] \"Mean mpg for carb == 4: 15.79\"\n# [1] \"Mean mpg for am == 1: 24.3923076923077\"\n}\n\n</pre>\n<p>You can read about <code>parse_expr</code> <a href=\"https://adv-r.hadley.nz/expressions.html#parsing\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">here</a> and about <code>!!</code> <a href=\"https://adv-r.hadley.nz/quasiquotation.html#unquoting-one-argument\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>. I don’t fully understand tidy evaluation at this point, but the code above should work in a wide variety of situations.</p>\n<p>(Disclaimer: There was a reference I came across for the <code>!!</code> + <code>rlang::parse_expr</code> trick that I can’t find now. If anyone knows where it is please let me know and I’ll acknowledge it here in the references.)</p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n '//cdn.viglink.com/api/vglnk.js'; \n\t\t\ts.src = 'https://www.r-bloggers.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/vglnk.js';\n        var r = d.getElementsByTagName(t)[0];\n            r.parentNode.insertBefore(s, r);\n    }(document, 'script'));\n</script>\n\t\t\n<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Avoid apply() function in large datasets","link":"https://predictivehacks.com/?p=2398","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://predictivehacks.com/avoid-apply-function-in-large-datasets/\"> R – Predictive Hacks</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>When we are dealing with large datasets and there is a need to calculate some values like the <code>row/column min/max/rank/mean</code> etc we should avoid the <code>apply</code> function because it takes a lot of time. Instead, we can use the <a href=\"https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/matrixStats/matrixStats.pdf\" class=\"rank-math-link\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">matrixStats </a>package and its corresponding functions. Let’s provide some comparisons.</p>\n<h2>Example of Minimum value per Row</h2>\n<p>Assume that we want to get the minimum value of each row from a <code>500 x 500</code>  matrix. Let’s compare the performance of the <code>apply</code> function from the <code>base</code> package versus the <code>rowMins</code> function from the <code>matrixStats</code> package.</p>\n</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nlibrary(matrixStats)\nlibrary(microbenchmark)\nlibrary(ggplot2)\n\nx <- matrix( rnorm(5000 * 5000), ncol = 5000 )\n\ntm <- microbenchmark(apply(x,1,min),\n                     rowMins(x).\n                     times = 100L\n                    )\n\ntm\n \n</pre>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>Unit: milliseconds\n             expr      min         lq       mean    median        uq       max neval\n apply(x, 1, min) 981.6283 1034.98050 1078.04485 1065.4163 1107.9962 1327.9284   100\n       rowMins(x)  42.1838   43.80065   46.55752   45.2255   47.6249   81.3097   100</code></pre>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n<p>As we can see from the output above, <strong>the <code>apply</code> function was 23 times slower than the <code>rowMins</code></strong>. Below we represent the violin plot</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nautoplot(tm)\n \n</pre>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-large-file=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/04/image6-1.png?w=578\" src=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/04/image6-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-63\" srcset_temp=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/04/image6-1.png 544w, https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/04/image6-1.png?w=150 150w, https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/04/image6-1.png?w=300 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 544px) 100vw, 544px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption><em>D. melanica</em> from the Olympic mountains in WA (left) and the Sierra Nevada mountains in CA (right).</figcaption></figure>\n</div>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\" />\n<h3>Exact Test and Plotting Script – exactTest_edgeR.r</h3>\n<p>The following <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R </a>script will be used to normalize raw gene counts produced by a quantification program such as <a href=\"https://ccb.jhu.edu/software/hisat2/manual.shtml\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Hisat2 </a>or <a href=\"https://ccb.jhu.edu/software/tophat/manual.shtml\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Tophat2</a>. Then, we will perform exact tests for a specified genotype data set and plot both intermediate and final results.</p>\n<p>We will use several different plotting functions to generate informative plots of our input data sets and exact test results with <a href=\"https://www.bioconductor.org/packages/release/bioc/vignettes/edgeR/inst/doc/edgeRUsersGuide.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">edgeR</a>.</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/graphics/versions/3.6.2/topics/barplot?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">barplot </a></strong>– Bar plots of sequence read library sizes</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/limma/versions/3.28.14/topics/plotMDS?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>plotMDS</strong> </a>– Multi-dimensional scaling (MDS) scatterplot of distances between the samples</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/stats/versions/3.6.2/topics/heatmap?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>heatmap</strong> </a>– Heatmaps of moderated log counts per million (logcpm)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/edgeR/versions/3.14.0/topics/plotBCV?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>plotBCV</strong> </a>– Genewise biological coefficient of variation (BCV) plots against gene abundance</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/limma/versions/3.28.14/topics/plotMD?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>plotMD</strong> </a>– Mean difference (MD) plots of all log fold change (logFC) against average count size</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/edgeR/versions/3.14.0/topics/plotSmear?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><strong>plotSmear</strong> </a>– MA plots of log-intensity ratios (M-values) versus log-intensity averages (A-values)</li>\n</ul>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\" />\n<h4>Script Inputs</h4>\n<p>A csv formatted file of raw gene counts is expected as an input to the script. Below is an example raw gene count table where columns are samples, rows are gene IDs, and raw counts are represented by “N#” for simplicity here.</p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><a href=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img data-attachment-id=\"1360\" data-permalink=\"https://morphoscape.wordpress.com/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign/\" data-orig-file=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png\" data-orig-size=\"1444,93\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=720\" src=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-1360\" srcset_temp=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=578 1024w, https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=150 150w, https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=300 300w, https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png?w=768 768w, https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/daphnia_exampleexperimentaldesign.png 1444w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></figure>\n<p>Additionally, the column numbers for the range of samples you wish to perform exact tests on need to be specified. So for example, the column numbers 1 and 6 should be input to perform an exact test on the E05 <em>Daphnia</em> genotype for the example raw gene count table above. This selects all replicates of the UV treatment and VIS control for the E05 genotype.</p>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\" />\n<h4>R Script</h4>\n<p>First, we should add some comments to the top of the R script describing how to run the script. We should also add a comment with a reminder of the purpose of the script.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#!/usr/bin/env Rscript\n#Usage: Rscript exactTest_edgeR.r rawGeneCountsFile startColumn endColumn\n#Usage Ex: Rscript exactTest_edgeR.r daphnia_rawGeneCounts_htseq.csv 1 6\n#R script to perform exact test DE analysis of raw gene counts using edgeR</code></pre>\n<p>Before proceeding with plotting and exact tests we will need to import the edgeR library.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Load the edgeR library\nlibrary(\"edgeR\")</code></pre>\n<p>Next, we retrieve the input path for our raw gene counts and the column range for the exact test.  The number of input arguments to the script are verified to help ensure that the required data is input to the script.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Retrieve inputs to the script\nargs = commandArgs(trailingOnly=TRUE)\n#Test if there is one input argument\nif (length(args)!=3) {\n  stop(\"One file name and a range of columns must be supplied.n\", call.=FALSE)\n}</code></pre>\n<p>Now we can import our data with the input path to our raw gene counts and the column range for the sample we want to test.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Import gene count data\ncountsTable <- read.csv(file=args[1], row.names=\"gene\")[ ,args[2]:args[3]]\nhead(countsTable)</code></pre>\n<p>The samples that represent our treatment and control need to be specified as a grouping factor to perform the exact tests with edgeR. In our example input data set the samples are ordered as:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>Treatment replicate 1</li>\n<li>Treatment replicate 2</li>\n<li>Treatment replicate 3</li>\n<li>Control replicate 1</li>\n<li>Control replicate 2</li>\n<li>Control replicate 3</li>\n</ol>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Add grouping factor\ngroup <- factor(c(rep(\"treat\",3),rep(\"ctrl\",3)))\n#Create DGE list object\nlist <- DGEList(counts=countsTable,group=group)</code></pre>\n<p>This is a good point to generate some interesting plots of our input data set before we begin preparing the raw gene counts for the exact test.</p>\n<p>First, we will plot the library sizes of our sequencing reads before normalization using the <strong>barplot</strong> function. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotBarsBefore</strong> jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Plot the library sizes before normalization\njpeg(\"plotBarsBefore.jpg\")\nbarplot(list$samples$lib.size*1e-6, names=1:6, ylab=\"Library size (millions)\")\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>Next, we will use the <strong>plotMDS</strong> function to display the relative similarities of the samples and view batch and treatment effects before normalization. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotMDSBefore</strong> jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Draw a MDS plot to show the relative similarities of the samples\njpeg(\"plotMDSBefore.jpg\")\nplotMDS(list, col=rep(1:3, each=3))\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>It can also be useful to view the moderated log-counts-per-million before normalization using the <strong>cpm</strong> function results with <strong>heatmap</strong>. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotHeatMapBefore</strong> jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Draw a heatmap of individual RNA-seq samples\njpeg(\"plotHeatMapBefore.jpg\")\nlogcpm <- cpm(list, log=TRUE)\nheatmap(logcpm)\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>There is no purpose in analyzing genes that are not expressed in either experimental condition (treatment or control), so raw gene counts are first filtered by expression levels. The normalized gene counts are output to the <strong>stats_normalizedCounts</strong> csv file.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Filter raw gene counts by expression levels\nkeep <- filterByExpr(list)\ntable(keep)\nlist <- list[keep, , keep.lib.sizes=FALSE]\n#Calculate normalized factors\nlist <- calcNormFactors(list)\n#Write normalized counts to file\nnormList <- cpm(list, normalized.lib.sizes=TRUE)\nwrite.table(normList, file=\"stats_normalizedCounts.csv\", sep=\",\", row.names=TRUE)\n#View normalization factors\nlist$samples\ndim(list)</code></pre>\n<p>Now that we have normalized gene counts for our samples we should generate the same set of previous plots for comparison.</p>\n<p>First, we plot the library sizes of our sequencing reads after normalization using the <strong>barplot</strong> function. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotBarsAfter</strong> jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Plot the library sizes after normalization\njpeg(\"plotBarsAfter.jpg\")\nbarplot(list$samples$lib.size*1e-6, names=1:6, ylab=\"Library size (millions)\")\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>Next, we will use the <strong>plotMDS</strong> function to display the relative similarities of the samples and view batch and treatment effects after normalization. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotMDSAfter</strong> jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Draw a MDS plot to show the relative similarities of the samples\njpeg(\"plotMDSAfter.jpg\")\nplotMDS(list, col=rep(1:3, each=3))\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>It can also be useful to view the moderated log counts per million (logcpm) after normalization using the <strong>cpm</strong> function results with <strong>heatmap</strong>. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotHeatMapAfter</strong> jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Draw a heatmap of individual RNA-seq samples\njpeg(\"plotHeatMapAfter.jpg\")\nlogcpm <- cpm(list, log=TRUE)\nheatmap(logcpm)\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>With the normalized gene counts we can also produce a matrix of pseudo-counts to estimate the common and tagwise dispersions. This allows us to use the <strong>plotBCV</strong> function to generate a genewise biological coefficient of variation (BCV) plot of dispersion estimates. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotBCVResults </strong>jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Produce a matrix of pseudo-counts\nlist <- estimateDisp(list)\nlist$common.dispersion\n#View dispersion estimates and biological coefficient of variation\njpeg(\"plotBCV.jpg\")\nplotBCV(list)\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>Finally, we are ready to perform exact tests with edgeR using the <strong><a href=\"https://www.rdocumentation.org/packages/edgeR/versions/3.14.0/topics/exactTest?tap_a=5644-dce66f&tap_s=10907-287229\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">exactTest</a></strong> function. The resulting table of differentially expressed (DE) genes are written to the <strong>stats_exactTest</strong> csv file to your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Perform an exact test for treat vs ctrl\ntested <- exactTest(list, pair=c(\"ctrl\", \"treat\"))\ntopTags(tested)\n#Create results table of DE genes\nresultsTbl <- topTags(tested, n=nrow(tested$table))$table\n#Output resulting table\nwrite.table(resultsTbl, file=\"stats_exactTest.csv\", sep=\",\", row.names=TRUE)</code></pre>\n<p>Using the resulting DE genes from the exact test we can view the counts per million for the top genes of each sample.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Look at the counts per million in individual samples for the top genes\no <- order(tested$table$PValue)\ncpm(list)[o[1:10],]\n#View the total number of differentially expressed genes at 5% FDR\nsummary(decideTests(tested))</code></pre>\n<p>We can also generate a mean difference (MD) plot of the log fold change (logFC) against the log counts per million (logcpm) using the <strong>plotMD</strong> function. DE genes are highlighted and the blue lines indicate 2-fold changes. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotMDResults </strong>jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Make a MD plot of logFC against logcpm\njpeg(\"plotMDResults.jpg\")\nplotMD(tested)\nabline(h=c(-1, 1), col=\"blue\")\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<p>As a final step, we will produce a MA plot of the libraries of count data using the <strong>plotSmear</strong> function. There are smearing points with very low counts, particularly those counts that are zero for one of the columns. The resulting plot is saved to the <strong>plotMAResults </strong>jpg file in your working directory.</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>#Make a MA plot of the libraries of count data\njpeg(\"plotMAResults.jpg\")\nplotSmear(tested)\ndev.off()</code></pre>\n<div class=\"wp-block-file aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/exacttest_edger.r.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">exacttest_edger.r</a><a href=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/exacttest_edger.r.pdf\" class=\"wp-block-file__button\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Download</a></div>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\" />\n<h3>Example Outputs – exactTest_edgeR.r</h3>\n<p>The following plots are example outputs from the above R script.</p>\n<h4>plotBarsAfter.jpg</h4>\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\">\n<figure class=\"aligncenter size-large\"><a href=\"https://morphoscape.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/barplot_e05.png\" The page functions as an easy to navigate,  one-stop-shop for available books on the R programming language.  </p>\n<p data-rte-preserve-empty=\"true\" class=\"\">\n<h2>WHY IS IT AWESOME?</h2>\n<p class=\"\">It’s awesome because it’s so easy to browse for new content on our beloved R.  Oscar jumpstarted the website with over 100 books and since the launch, there has been an ongoing stream of contributions from the open-source community. </p>\n<p class=\"\">Best of all, most of the books are either free or very low cost.  </p>\n<p data-rte-preserve-empty=\"true\" class=\"\">\n<h2>HOW TO GET STARTED?</h2>\n<p class=\"\">It’s very simple, <a href=\"https://www.bigbookofr.com/index.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"> go to the website</a> and start browsing for new R content!  If you have a favorite book that you don’t see listed, please contribute your content by submitting a pull request or issue into the <a href=\"https://github.com/oscarbaruffa/BigBookofR\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">GitHub repo</a>.</p>\n<p data-rte-preserve-empty=\"true\" class=\"\">\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"https://images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/58eef8846a4963e429687a4d/1599186207125-H2SRSC3564LKBD983OOZ/ke17ZwdGBToddI8pDm48kJK4Mm1kch8SFO9ZNkN1NT97gQa3H78H3Y0txjaiv_0fDoOvxcdMmMKkDsyUqMSsMWxHk725yiiHCCLfrh8O1z5QHyNOqBUUEtDDsRWrJLTmN9YSRtfoTLg6dUq-6F17A0FFZK5fArcnK1IqGweyunyWChwIwkIJ_P7MaZif-uMs/The%20Big%20Book%20of%20R.png?format=1500w","linkMd5":"4aaa0a0e57892d47becd87f919c47cf8","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn68@2020_1/2020/09/05/06-27-11-509_989e1330c67bed1d.webp","destWidth":1080,"destHeight":1080,"sourceBytes":95539,"destBytes":36774,"author":"Laura Ellis","enclosureType":"image/png","enclosureUrl":"https://images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/58eef8846a4963e429687a4d/1599186207125-H2SRSC3564LKBD983OOZ/ke17ZwdGBToddI8pDm48kJK4Mm1kch8SFO9ZNkN1NT97gQa3H78H3Y0txjaiv_0fDoOvxcdMmMKkDsyUqMSsMWxHk725yiiHCCLfrh8O1z5QHyNOqBUUEtDDsRWrJLTmN9YSRtfoTLg6dUq-6F17A0FFZK5fArcnK1IqGweyunyWChwIwkIJ_P7MaZif-uMs/The%20Big%20Book%20of%20R.png?format=1500w","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i0.wp.com/images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/58eef8846a4963e429687a4d/1599184267057-QTE1OG6LY4RG00XRAFCQ/ke17ZwdGBToddI8pDm48kHUr0Bf5cnoVftFwCAEKSwdZw-zPPgdn4jUwVcJE1ZvWEtT5uBSRWt4vQZAgTJucoTqqXjS3CfNDSuuf31e0tVEB0tZRCKGPzR1qCKWBAt5IrdwBFrQgH06KEwsS7c8q76QvevUbj177dmcMs1F0H-0/Rstats+GIF-downsized_large.gif?w=578&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn45@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-20-360_0bd7b721938fea28.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/VYUZwonxa_k":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn8@2020_6/2020/09/05/06-27-16-426_ce5642e3fe3e2de5.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287179962},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","title":"Recession Forecasting with a Neural Net in R","link":"http://franklinparker.com/?p=358","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://franklinparker.com/2020/09/04/recession-forecasting-with-a-neural-net-in-r/\"> R – Franklin J. Parker</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>I spend quite a bit of time at work trying to understand where we are in the business cycle. That analysis informs our capital market expectations, and, by extension, our asset allocation and portfolio risk controls.</p>\n<p>For years now I have used a trusty old linear regression model, held together with bailing wire, duct tape, and love. It’s a good model. It predicted the 2020 recession and the 2008 recessions (yes, out of sample).</p>\n<p>Even so, a couple of years ago I began experimenting with a neural net to (a) increase the amount of data I could shove at it and (b) possibly get a better model. Of course, I wouldn’t retire my old model just yet, I’d just run them along side each other.</p>\n<p>So, here is the framework for forecasting a recession using a neural net in R.</p>\n<h2>What <em>are </em>We Trying to Predict, Anyway?</h2>\n<p>The first question we need to answer is what we want our output to be. Typically, markets see recessions coming 3 to 6 months before they officially hit. If I’m going to add value to a portfolio, I need to be rebalancing out of risk assets <em>before </em>everyone else. So, for my purposes, I’d like to have 9 to 12 months of lead time to get ahead of a recession.</p>\n<p>Ideally, then, our model would look like this:</p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"364\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-6/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png\" data-orig-size=\"1172,619\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=696\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-364\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=578 1024w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=300 300w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=768 768w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png 1172w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>Except, instead of getting 1s concurrently with a recession, we would get them 12-months in advance. This, then, will be our output: USREC data with a 12-month lead.</p>\n<p>The next challenge is finding the data points which reliably predict recessionary environments. Now let’s pause here for a second and acknowledge some danger…</p>\n<p>We could data mine this thing and swap indicators in and out until we have an over-fitted model that doesn’t work going forward. Personally, I’m not a fan of that. It may be a bird-dog style analysis, pointing the way to potential information, but to me that is too dangerous. I could construct any story to “justify” random inputs to a model.</p>\n<p>I would rather begin with indicators that (1) have been at least acknowledged and studied by others, and (2) I can build a convincing theory for how it would influence/indicate the business cycle <em>a priori</em>. Then I’ll plug them in and see if the model is effective.</p>\n<p>I’d rather have an imperfect hammer than a brittle one that breaks on first impact.</p>\n<h2>Inputs</h2>\n<p>Tor the sake of this demonstration, I’ve settled on three inputs. Each of these has academic research behind it and has worked <em>ex post</em>. What’s more, I can build strong justifications for why each of these should both indicate and influence the economy as a whole. Of course there are others, but this is just a demonstration after all!</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Headline Unemployment</li>\n<li>Effective Federal Funds Rate</li>\n<li>The Yield Curve</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Now, we won’t be using the raw figure of each of these. We are going to convert them into “signal” form. The idea is to boil-down the essence of the signal so the neural net has to do as little work as possible. That is, in my opinion, the most effective way to increase your accuracy without increasing model fragility.</p>\n<h2>The Code – Get and Wrangle Data</h2>\n<p>We are going to need five libraries for this one:</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\nlibrary(quantmod)\nlibrary(tidyverse)\nlibrary(nnet)\nlibrary(DALEX)\nlibrary(breakDown)\n</pre>\n<p>Note that DALEX and breakDown are more about visualization and understanding of the model later. </p>\n<p>Next, let’s load our raw data. By the way, you can get these symbols from a search on the <a href=\"https://fred.stlouisfed.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">St. Louis Fed website</a>. The symbol is immediately after the title. If you haven’t been to the FRED database… well, then we can’t be friends anymore and I can’t invite you to my birthday party. </p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"370\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-1-2/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png\" data-orig-size=\"518,500\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image-1\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png?w=578\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-370\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png 518w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png?w=300 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 518px) 100vw, 518px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Get data\ngetSymbols( c('USREC', 'UNRATE', 'T10Y3M', 'DFF'), src = 'FRED')\n\n# Note the differing periods of data\nUSREC %>% head() # Monthly data\n           USREC\n1854-12-01     1\n1855-01-01     0\n1855-02-01     0\n1855-03-01     0\n1855-04-01     0\n1855-05-01     0\n\nUNRATE %>% head() # Monthly data\n           UNRATE\n1948-01-01    3.4\n1948-02-01    3.8\n1948-03-01    4.0\n1948-04-01    3.9\n1948-05-01    3.5\n1948-06-01    3.6\n\nDFF %>% head() # Daily data\n            DFF\n1954-07-01 1.13\n1954-07-02 1.25\n1954-07-03 1.25\n1954-07-04 1.25\n1954-07-05 0.88\n1954-07-06 0.25\n\nT10Y3M %>% head() # Daily data (sort of)\n           T10Y3M\n1982-01-04   2.32\n1982-01-05   2.24\n1982-01-06   2.43\n1982-01-07   2.46\n1982-01-08   2.50\n1982-01-11   2.32\n</pre>\n<p>Our data is not aligned, so we have a touch of work to do to wrangle it, then signal-fy it. Though the other data starts much earlier, the yield curve data doesn’t start until 1982, so we’ll cut everything before then.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Wrangle Data, Convert to Signals\n# Get all data into monthly format, starting in 1982\nrecessions <- USREC\nunemployment <- UNRATE\nfedfunds <- to.monthly(DFF)$DFF.Open\nyieldcurve <- to.monthly(T10Y3M)$T10Y3M.Open\n</pre>\n<p>Again, I won’t cover all of the theory behind the signalification of our data, but just know that this isn’t me making random stuff up. <em>(You should never trust anyone who says that, by the way.)</em></p>\n<p>The other important component of these signals is how they are shifting through time. Because the nnet package doesn’t look backward at the data, it only takes in this moment, we need to add inputs that look backward. I’m doing that by lagging each signal by 3 months and 6 months.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Unemployment signal: 12-month slow moving average minus\n# the headline unemployment rate.\nsignal_unemployment <- SMA(unemployment, n = 12) - unemployment \nsignal_unemployment_lag3 <- lag(signal_unemployment, n = 3)\nsignal_unemployment_lag6 <- lag(signal_unemployment, n = 6)\n\n# FedFunds signal: effective federal funds rate minus the \n# maximum fedfunds rate over the past 12 months\nsignal_fedfunds <- fedfunds - rollmax(fedfunds, k = 12, align = 'right')\nsignal_fedfunds_lag3 <- lag(signal_fedfunds, n = 3)\nsignal_fedfunds_lag6 <- lag(signal_fedfunds, n = 6)\n\n# Yield curve signal: 10-year US treasury yield minus the 3-month\n# US treasury yield.\nsignal_yieldcurve <- yieldcurve\nsignal_yieldcurve_lag3 <- lag(signal_yieldcurve, n = 3)\nsignal_yieldcurve_lag6 <- lag(signal_yieldcurve, n = 6)\n</pre>\n<p>Recall that we want a 12-month lead time on our recession indicator, so we are going to adjust USREC data by 12-months for the purposes of training our model.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Since we want to know if a recession is starting in the next 12 months, \n# need to lag USREC by NEGATIVE 12 months = lead(12)\nsignal_recessions <- recessions %>% \n  coredata() %>% # Extract core data\n  lead(12) %>% # Move it 12 months earlier\n  as.xts(., order.by = index(recessions)) # Reorder as an xts, with Recessions adjusted\n</pre>\n<p>If you remember from neural net training 101, a neural net has hyperparameters, i.e. how big the net is and how we manage weight decay. This means we can’t simply <em>train </em>a neural net. We have to <em>tune </em>it also.</p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"380\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-7/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg\" data-orig-size=\"651,383\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg?w=578\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-380\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg 651w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg?w=300 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 651px) 100vw, 651px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>Which brings us to the really sucky part: we need to divide our data into a train set, tune set, and out-of-sample test set. This means tedious “window” work. Ugh.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Seperate data into train, test, and out-of-sample\n# Training data will be through Dec 1998\n# Testing data will be Jan 1999 through Dec 2012\n# Out of sample will be Jan 2013 through July 2020\nstart_train <- as.Date('1982-08-01')\nend_train <- as.Date('1998-12-31')\nstart_test <- as.Date('1999-01-01')\nend_test <- as.Date('2012-12-31')\nstart_oos <- as.Date('2013-01-01')\nend_oos <- as.Date('2020-07-31')\ntrain_data <- data.frame( 'unemployment' = window( signal_unemployment,\n                                                   start = start_train,\n                                                   end = end_train ),\n                          \n                          'unemployment_lag3' = window( signal_unemployment_lag3,\n                                                        start = start_train,\n                                                        end = end_train ),\n                          \n                          'unemployment_lag6' = window( signal_unemployment_lag6,\n                                                        start = start_train,\n                                                        end = end_train ),\n                          \n                          'fedfunds' = window( signal_fedfunds,\n                                               start = start_train,\n                                               end = end_train),\n                          \n                          'fedfunds_lag3' = window( signal_fedfunds_lag3,\n                                                    start = start_train,\n                                                    end = end_train),\n                          \n                          'fedfunds_lag6' = window( signal_fedfunds_lag6,\n                                                    start = start_train,\n                                                    end = end_train),\n                          \n                          'yieldcurve' = window( signal_yieldcurve,\n                                                 start = start_train,\n                                                 end = end_train),\n                          \n                          'yieldcurve_lag3' = window( signal_yieldcurve_lag3,\n                                                      start = start_train,\n                                                      end = end_train),\n                          \n                          'yieldcurve_lag6' = window( signal_yieldcurve_lag6,\n                                                      start = start_train,\n                                                      end = end_train),\n                          \n                          'recessions' = window( signal_recessions,\n                                                 start = start_train,\n                                                 end = end_train) )\ntest_data <- data.frame( 'unemployment' = window( signal_unemployment,\n                                                  start = start_test,\n                                                  end = end_test ),\n                         \n                         'unemployment_lag3' = window( signal_unemployment_lag3,\n                                                       start = start_test,\n                                                       end = end_test ),\n                         \n                         'unemployment_lag6' = window( signal_unemployment_lag6,\n                                                       start = start_test,\n                                                       end = end_test ),\n                         \n                         'fedfunds' = window( signal_fedfunds,\n                                              start = start_test,\n                                              end = end_test),\n                         \n                         'fedfunds_lag3' = window( signal_fedfunds_lag3,\n                                                   start = start_test,\n                                                   end = end_test),\n                         \n                         'fedfunds_lag6' = window( signal_fedfunds_lag6,\n                                                   start = start_test,\n                                                   end = end_test),\n                         \n                         'yieldcurve' = window( signal_yieldcurve,\n                                                start = start_test,\n                                                end = end_test),\n                         \n                         'yieldcurve_lag3' = window( signal_yieldcurve_lag3,\n                                                     start = start_test,\n                                                     end = end_test),\n                         \n                         'yieldcurve_lag6' = window( signal_yieldcurve_lag6,\n                                                     start = start_test,\n                                                     end = end_test),\n                         \n                         'recessions' = window( signal_recessions,\n                                                start = start_test,\n                                                end = end_test) )\noos_data <- data.frame( 'unemployment' = window( signal_unemployment,\n                                                 start = start_oos,\n                                                 end = end_oos ),\n                        \n                        'unemployment_lag3' = window( signal_unemployment_lag3,\n                                                      start = start_oos,\n                                                      end = end_oos ),\n                        \n                        'unemployment_lag6' = window( signal_unemployment_lag6,\n                                                      start = start_oos,\n                                                      end = end_oos ),\n                        \n                        'fedfunds' = window( signal_fedfunds,\n                                             start = start_oos,\n                                             end = end_oos),\n                        \n                        'fedfunds_lag3' = window( signal_fedfunds_lag3,\n                                                  start = start_oos,\n                                                  end = end_oos),\n                        \n                        'fedfunds_lag6' = window( signal_fedfunds_lag6,\n                                                  start = start_oos,\n                                                  end = end_oos),\n                        \n                        'yieldcurve' = window( signal_yieldcurve,\n                                               start = start_oos,\n                                               end = end_oos),\n                        \n                        'yieldcurve_lag3' = window( signal_yieldcurve_lag3,\n                                                    start = start_oos,\n                                                    end = end_oos),\n                        \n                        'yieldcurve_lag6' = window( signal_yieldcurve_lag6,\n                                                    start = start_oos,\n                                                    end = end_oos),\n                        \n                        'recessions' = window( signal_recessions,\n                                               start = start_oos,\n                                               end = end_oos) )\ncolnames(train_data) <- colnames(test_data) <- colnames(oos_data) <- c( 'unemployment',\n                                                                        'unemployment_lag3',\n                                                                        'unemployment_lag6',\n                                                                        'fedfunds',\n                                                                        'fedfunds_lag3',\n                                                                        'fedfunds_lag6',\n                                                                        'yieldcurve',\n                                                                        'yieldcurve_lag3',\n                                                                        'yieldcurve_lag6',\n                                                                        'recessions' )\n</pre>\n<p><em>Comment below if you have a more efficient way of doing this!</em></p>\n<h2>The Code – Train & Tune Our Neural Net</h2>\n<p>From here we need to make some educated, but mostly guess-like, decisions. </p>\n<p>We must specify a minimum and maximum neural net size. I remember reading once that a neural network hidden layer should be no less than about 35% the number of inputs, and no larger than 1.5 times the total number of inputs. I’m open to critique here, but that’s what I’ll specify for the minimum and maximum.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Hyperparameters for tuning\n# Minimum net size\nsize_min <- (ncol(train_data) * 0.35) %>% round(0)\n# Maximum net size\nsize_max <- (ncol(train_data) * 1.5) %>% round(0)\nsize <- seq(size_min, size_max, 1)\n</pre>\n<p>The next hyperparameter is weight decay. As far as I know, there is really no rule of thumb for this, other than it should be 0 < <em>w</em> < 1. For illustration, I’m going to sequence it between 0.1 and 1.5. </p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Weight decay settings, start at seq(2, 50, 2) then up it.\n# This figure will get divided by 100 in the coming code.\nw_decay <- seq(10, 150, 2)\n</pre>\n<p>Yay! Now the fun part begins! </p>\n<p>We want the neural network that delivers the best possible <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brier_score\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Brier score</a>, and we want to output the optimal model and hyperparameters for inspection and use later. Normally I wouldn’t log the Brier scores as they go, but I want to see how net size and various weight decays effect our model, just for fun.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\nbest_brier <- 1 # Prime variable\nnet_size_optimal <- 5 # Prime variable\nw_decay_optimal <- 0.2 # Prime variable\n# Log evolution of RMSE, rows are net size, columns are weight decay\nbrier_evolution <- 0\ni <-  1\nj <-  1\nfor(n in size){\n  for(w in w_decay){\n    \n    # Train model\n    model_fit <- nnet( as.matrix(train_data$recessions) ~\n                         unemployment + unemployment_lag3 + unemployment_lag6 +\n                         fedfunds + fedfunds_lag3 + fedfunds_lag6 +\n                         yieldcurve + yieldcurve_lag3 + yieldcurve_lag6,\n                       data = as.matrix(train_data),\n                       maxit = 500,\n                       size = n,\n                       decay = w/100,\n                       linout = 1 )\n    \n    # Test model (for hyperparameter tuning), return Brier\n    model_predict <- predict( model_fit, newdata = as.matrix(test_data) )\n    brier <- mean( (model_predict - test_data$recessions)^2 )\n    \n    # If this is the best model, store the hyperparams and the model\n    if( brier < best_brier ){\n      net_size_optimal <- n\n      w_decay_optimal <- w/100\n      best_brier <- brier\n      model <- model_fit\n    }\n    brier_evolution[i] <- brier\n    i <- i + 1\n  }\n}\n</pre>\n<p>And BOOM! We’ve got ourselves an artificial neural network!</p>\n<h2>The Code – Results</h2>\n<p>First, let’s see how our hyperparameters affected our Brier score. </p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Drop Brier score into a matrix\nbrier_evolution <- matrix( brier_evolution, nrow = length(w_decay), ncol = length(size), byrow = FALSE )\nrownames(brier_evolution) <- w_decay/100\ncolnames(brier_evolution) <- size\n# View Brier score results\npersp( x = w_decay/100,\n       y = size,\n       z = brier_evolution,\n       ticktype = 'detailed',\n       xlab = 'Weight Decay',\n       ylab = 'Net Size',\n       zlab = 'Brier Score',\n       shade = 0.5,\n       main = 'Brier Score, Effect of Net Size and Weight Decay')\n</pre>\n<p>Yielding</p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"388\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-3-2/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png\" data-orig-size=\"822,791\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image-3\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=696\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-388\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png 822w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=300 300w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=768 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 822px) 100vw, 822px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>It is so interesting to me how weight decay is, by far, the most influential hyperparameter. Size matters, but not nearly as much. Also, we should note there is not too much difference between the worst model we tested and the best, as the Brier score drops from 0.15 to 0.10. </p>\n<p>Just to put Brier scores into perspective: a score of 0 is perfect prescience, and a score of 0.50 is no better than chance. <a href=\"https://jpm.pm-research.com/content/45/5/125/tab-pdf-disaabled\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Tetlock claims</a> that we need a Brier score of 0.30 or better to generate portfolio alpha (this is a firm-wide metric, see plot from his paper below), but it looks like 0.20 and below is really where you want to be.</p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image\"><img src=\"https://i2.wp.com/jpm.pm-research.com/content/iijpormgmt/45/5/125/F4.large.jpg?w=578&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Accelerating Learning in Active Management: The Alpha-Brier Process | The  Journal of Portfolio Management\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>At 0.10, we are on track! Of course, this is in-sample. How did we do out-of-sample?</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Use model Out of Sample, plot results\nmodel_predict_oos <- predict( model, newdata = as.matrix(oos_data) )\ndata.frame( 'Date' = index( as.xts(oos_data)), \n            'Probability' = coredata(model_predict_oos) ) %>% \n  ggplot(., aes(x = Date, y = Probability) )+\n  geom_line(size = 2, col = 'dodgerblue4')+\n  labs(title = 'Probability of Recession in the Next 12 Months')+\n  xlab('')+\n  ylab('')+\n  theme_light()+\n  theme( axis.text = element_text(size = 12),\n         plot.title = element_text(size = 18))\n</pre>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"391\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-4-2/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png\" data-orig-size=\"897,559\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image-4\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=696\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-391\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png 897w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=300 300w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=768 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 897px) 100vw, 897px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>Which looks pretty good! Here we see that the traditional metrics produced a pretty reliable indication that 2020 would see a recession. <em>My trusty-rusty old linear regression model predicted the same, by the way!</em></p>\n<p>I am very interested to know our out-of-sample Brier score.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n> brier_oos <- mean( (model_predict_oos - oos_data$recessions)^2, na.rm = T )\n> brier_oos\n[1] 0.04061196\n</pre>\n<p>0.04 is about as close to prescience as you can get. Honestly, it scares me a little because it is a bit <em>too </em>good. It is also suspicious to me that the model produced <em>negative </em>probabilities of a recession for 2014 through 2016. Before putting this into production I’d really like to know why that is.</p>\n<h2>What’s Driving These Results</h2>\n<p>Using the DALEX and breakDown packages, we can examine what is driving the results.</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\n# Try to understand what drives the model using DALEX package\nmodel_explanation <- explain( model, data = oos_data, predict_function = predict, y = oos_data$recessions )\nbd <- break_down( model_explanation, \n                  oos_data[ length(oos_data), 1:(ncol(oos_data)-1) ], \n                  keep_distributions = T )\nplot(bd)\n</pre>\n<p>Giving us</p>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"396\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-5-2/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png\" data-orig-size=\"891,562\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image-5\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=696\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-396\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png 891w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=300 300w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=768 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 891px) 100vw, 891px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>This illustrates the largest factors effecting the outcome <em>in the most recent data point</em>. It looks like the model has a standard bias of 0.081 (the intercept), and the most recent 3-month lagged yield curve data is what moved the needle most. All-in, the yield curve is the strongest signal in this data point (which doesn’t surprise me at all, it is the most well-known and widely studied because it has accurately predicted every recession since nineteensixtysomething).</p>\n<p>We can also see how changes in any of the data points affect the output of the model</p>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; gutter: false; title: ; notranslate\">\nprofile <- model_profile( model_explanation,\n                          variables = c( 'unemployment',\n                                         'unemployment_lag3',\n                                         'unemployment_lag6',\n                                         'fedfunds',\n                                         'fedfunds_lag3',\n                                         'fedfunds_lag6',\n                                         'yieldcurve',\n                                         'yieldcurve_lag3',\n                                         'yieldcurve_lag6' ) )\nplot(profile, geom = 'aggregates')\n</pre>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-image size-large\"><img data-attachment-id=\"399\" data-permalink=\"https://franklinparker.com/image-6-2/\" data-orig-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png\" data-orig-size=\"904,778\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{\"aperture\":\"0\",\"credit\":\"\",\"camera\":\"\",\"caption\":\"\",\"created_timestamp\":\"0\",\"copyright\":\"\",\"focal_length\":\"0\",\"iso\":\"0\",\"shutter_speed\":\"0\",\"title\":\"\",\"orientation\":\"0\"}\" data-image-title=\"image-6\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=300\" data-large-file=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=696\" src=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=578\" alt=\"\" class=\"wp-image-399\" srcset_temp=\"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png 904w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=150 150w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=300 300w, https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=768 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 904px) 100vw, 904px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></figure>\n<p>Again, the yield curve appears to be the most influential variable as changes there (<em>x</em>-axis) dramatically affect the output (<em>y</em>-axis). That said, unemployment is also a significant factor, except when lagged by 6 months. Changes there seem to have no effect.</p>\n<p>In the end, I’d say this is a productive model. It appears robust across the various inputs, and it has an excellent out-of-sample Brier score for predicting recessions (though 2020 is our only data point for that). I am concerned that the Brier score is <em>too </em>good and we have created a fragile model that may not work with future inputs, and I am concerned about the negative probabilities produced in 2013 through 2016 (this may be a function of the fragility inherent in the model–those years carried unprecedented inputs).</p>\n<p>Ultimately, there is enough here for me to dig and refine further, but I wouldn’t put this exact model into production without addressing my concerns.</p>\n<p><em>As always, if you have critiques of anything here, please share them so that I can correct errors and learn!</em> </p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = d.createElement(t);\n            s.type = 'text/javascript';\n            s.async = true;\n\t\t\t// s.defer = true;\n//          s.src = '//cdn.viglink.com/api/vglnk.js'; \n\t\t\ts.src = 'https://www.r-bloggers.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/vglnk.js';\n        var r = d.getElementsByTagName(t)[0];\n            r.parentNode.insertBefore(s, r);\n    }(document, 'script'));\n</script>\n\t\t\n<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 13px;\">\n<div style=\"text-align: center;\">To <strong>leave a comment</strong> for the author, please follow the link and comment on their blog: <strong><a href=\"https://franklinparker.com/2020/09/04/recession-forecasting-with-a-neural-net-in-r/\"> R – Franklin J.
