Parker","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn51@2020_1/2020/09/05/06-27-10-140_5328c285545a0e52.webp","https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-1.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn47@2020_2/2020/09/05/06-27-12-445_12ffa2cb4a5098b2.webp","https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image.jpeg?w=578":null,"https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-3.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn67@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-13-231_2b3cd73434704a5e.webp","https://i2.wp.com/jpm.pm-research.com/content/iijpormgmt/45/5/125/F4.large.jpg?w=578&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn92@2020_3/2020/09/05/06-27-17-941_3b71d29d7ea8493a.webp","https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-4.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn49@2020_1/2020/09/05/06-27-17-238_acd6c656d75204f4.webp","https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-5.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn30@2020_1/2020/09/05/06-27-12-167_154955b77e040440.webp","https://franklinparker447404942.files.wordpress.com/2020/09/image-6.png?w=578":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn9@2020_2/2020/09/05/06-27-11-477_089d37662a98a977.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/dUtPQOczdXc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn39@2020_4/2020/09/05/06-27-12-444_83fe77402d476d75.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287179960},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:19","title":"How to Backtest your Crypto Trading Strategies in R","link":"https://predictivehacks.com/?p=2439","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://predictivehacks.com/how-to-backtest-your-crypto-trading-strategies-in-r/\"> R – Predictive Hacks</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<h2>Few words about Trading Strategies</h2>\n<p>One of the biggest challenges is to predict the Market. Many people have developed their own trading strategies, some of them are advanced based on Machine Learning and Artificial Intelligence algorithms like LSTM, xgBoost, Random Forest etc, some others are based on  Statistical models like <a href=\"https://predictivehacks.com/arima-r/\" class=\"rank-math-link\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">ARIMA</a>, and some others are based on <a href=\"https://www.investopedia.com/terms/t/technicalanalysis.asp\" class=\"rank-math-link\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">technical analysis</a>. </p>\n<p>Whatever Trading Strategy we are about to apply we need to backtest it, meaning to simulate it and finally to evaluate it. Today, we will provide an example of how you can easily backtest your own trading strategy in R. </p>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n<h2>Define the Trading Strategy</h2>\n<p>For illustrative purposes, we defined an arbitrary Trading Strategy which does not make much sense, but it is good to work on it as an example. Let’s define the rules:</p>\n<p>When the <strong>close price</strong> of the Cryptocurrency is <strong>X consecutive</strong> days in the same direction (i.e. 7 consecutive days “up” or 7 consecutive days “down”) then we<strong> Open a Position</strong> as follows:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>When the close price is X consecutive days down, then the next day we buy (long) the cryptocurrency at the “open price”</li>\n<li>When the close price is X consecutive days up, then the next day we sell (short) the cryptocurrency at the “open price”. We assume that short selling is allowed with <a href=\"https://capital.com/cfd-trading-explained\" class=\"rank-math-link\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">CFD </a></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Once we have Opened our positions we use the following alerts:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>TP: Take profit when your P/L is above X%</li>\n<li>SL: Stop loss when you P/L is below -Y%</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Every position is closed at the open price. Of course, we can extend this assumption by considering hourly or even per minute data. Every trade is <strong>1 unit</strong> but we can change this to a multiplier or to a fraction. Notice that the assumption of the 1-unit does not affect our outcome, since we communicate the ROI which is the (P/L) as a percentage.</p>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n<h2>R Code for to backtest the Trading Strategy</h2>\n<p>You can have a look at how we can get the <a href=\"https://predictivehacks.com/how-to-get-cryptocurrency-prices-in-r/\" class=\"rank-math-link\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Cryptocurrency prices in R</a> and <a href=\"https://predictivehacks.com/count-the-consecutive-events-in-r/\" class=\"rank-math-link\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">how to count the consecutive events in R</a>. Below we build a function which takes as parameters:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>symbol</strong>: The cryptocurrency symbol. For example, <code>BTC</code> is for the Bitcoin.</li>\n<li><strong>consecutive</strong>: The consecutive count of the signs of the closing prices.</li>\n<li><strong>SL</strong>: The percentage that we stop loss.</li>\n<li><strong>TP</strong>: The percentage that we take profit.</li>\n<li><strong>start_date</strong>: The date that we want to start the backtesting strategy.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Notice that the open positions that have not met the alert criteria of SL and TP and still “Active” and we return them with an “Active” status and as “Profit” we return their current “Profit”.  </p>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nlibrary(tidyverse)\nlibrary(crypto)\n\n\nback_testing<-function(symbol=\"BTC\", consecutive=7, SL=0.1, TP=0.1, start_date = \"20180101\") {\n  \n \n  \n  df<-crypto_history(coin = symbol, start_date = start_date)\n  \n\n  \n  df<-df%>%mutate(Sign = ifelse(close>lag(close),\"up\", \"down\"))%>%\n    mutate(Streak=sequence(rle(Sign)$lengths))\n  \n  \n  df<-df%>%select(symbol, date, open, high, low, close, Sign, Streak)%>%na.omit()%>%\n    mutate(Signal = case_when(lag(Sign)==\"up\" &#38; lag(Streak)%%consecutive==0~'short',\n                              lag(Sign)==\"down\" &#38; lag(Streak)%%consecutive==0~'long',\n                              TRUE~\"\"), Dummy=TRUE\n    )\n  \n  \n  Trades<-df%>%filter(Signal!=\"\")%>%select(Open_Position_Date=date, Open_Position_Price=open, Dummy, Signal)\n  Trades\n  \n  \n  Portfolios<-Trades%>%inner_join(df%>%select(-Signal), by=\"Dummy\")%>%filter(date>Open_Position_Date)%>%select(-Dummy)%>%mutate(Pct_Change=open/Open_Position_Price-1)%>%\n    mutate(Alert = case_when(Signal=='long'&#38; Pct_Change>TP~'TP',\n                             Signal=='long'&#38; Pct_Change< -SL~'SL',\n                             Signal=='short'&#38; Pct_Change>TP~'SL',\n                             Signal=='short'&#38; Pct_Change< -SL~'TP'\n    )\n    )%>%group_by(Open_Position_Date)%>%mutate(Status=ifelse(sum(!is.na(Alert))>0, 'Closed', 'Active'))\n  \n  \n  Active<-Portfolios%>%filter(Status=='Active')%>%group_by(Open_Position_Date)%>%arrange(date)%>%slice(n())%>%\n    mutate(Profit=case_when(Signal=='short'~Open_Position_Price-open, \n                            Signal=='long'~open-Open_Position_Price))%>%\n    select(symbol, Status, Signal, date, Open_Position_Date, Open_Position_Price, open, Profit)\n  \n  Closed<-Portfolios%>%filter(Status=='Closed')%>%na.omit()%>%group_by(Open_Position_Date)%>%arrange(date)%>%slice(1)%>%\n    mutate(Profit=case_when(Signal=='short'~Open_Position_Price-open, \n                            Signal=='long'~open-Open_Position_Price))%>%\n    select(symbol, Status, Signal, date, Open_Position_Date, Open_Position_Price, open, Profit)\n  \n  final<-bind_rows(Closed,Active)%>%ungroup()%>%arrange(date)%>%mutate(ROI=Profit/Open_Position_Price, Running_ROI=cumsum(Profit)/cumsum(Open_Position_Price))\n  \n \n  return(final)\n  \n  \n}\n</pre>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n<h2>Results of the Backtest</h2>\n<p>Let’s assume that we want to backtest the trading strategy that we described earlier with the following parameters:</p>\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>symbol=\"BTC\", consecutive=5, SL=0.1, TP=0.5, start_date = \"20180101\"</code></pre>\n<pre class=\"brush: r; title: ; notranslate\">\nttt<-back_testing(symbol=\"BTC\", consecutive=5, SL=0.1, TP=0.15, start_date = \"20180101\")\n\n</pre>\n<figure class=\"wp-block-table\">\n<table 6</td>\n<td>-840</td>\n<td>-15.70%</td>\n<td>-1.06%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>5/12/2019</td>\n<td>5/5/2019</td>\n<td>5831</td>\n<td>7204</td>\n<td>-1372</td>\n<td>-23.50%</td>\n<td>-2.59%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>5/27/2019</td>\n<td>5/12/2019</td>\n<td>7204</td>\n<td>8674</td>\n<td>-1471</td>\n<td>-20.40%</td>\n<td>-3.98%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>6/5/2019</td>\n<td>5/28/2019</td>\n<td>8803</td>\n<td>7704</td>\n<td>1098</td>\n<td>12.50%</td>\n<td>-2.55%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>6/23/2019</td>\n<td>6/17/2019</td>\n<td>8989</td>\n<td>10697</td>\n<td>-1708</td>\n<td>-19.00%</td>\n<td>-3.89%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>6/27/2019</td>\n<td>6/24/2019</td>\n<td>10854</td>\n<td>13017</td>\n<td>-2163</td>\n<td>-19.90%</td>\n<td>-5.32%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>8/30/2019</td>\n<td>8/4/2019</td>\n<td>10822</td>\n<td>9515</td>\n<td>1307</td>\n<td>12.10%</td>\n<td>-3.90%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>9/25/2019</td>\n<td>9/4/2019</td>\n<td>10621</td>\n<td>8603</td>\n<td>2018</td>\n<td>19.00%</td>\n<td>-2.20%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>long</td>\n<td>9/25/2019</td>\n<td>9/18/2019</td>\n<td>10248</td>\n<td>8603</td>\n<td>-1644</td>\n<td>-16.00%</td>\n<td>-3.12%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>long</td>\n<td>1/15/2020</td>\n<td>11/23/2019</td>\n<td>7296</td>\n<td>8825</td>\n<td>1529</td>\n<td>21.00%</td>\n<td>-2.03%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>long</td>\n<td>1/15/2020</td>\n<td>12/5/2019</td>\n<td>7253</td>\n<td>8825</td>\n<td>1572</td>\n<td>21.70%</td>\n<td>-1.00%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>1/31/2020</td>\n<td>1/8/2020</td>\n<td>8162</td>\n<td>9508</td>\n<td>-1346</td>\n<td>-16.50%</td>\n<td>-1.72%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>2/27/2020</td>\n<td>2/10/2020</td>\n<td>10116</td>\n<td>8825</td>\n<td>1290</td>\n<td>12.80%</td>\n<td>-0.93%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>long</td>\n<td>3/13/2020</td>\n<td>2/29/2020</td>\n<td>8671</td>\n<td>5018</td>\n<td>-3653</td>\n<td>-42.10%</td>\n<td>-2.77%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>short</td>\n<td>5/2/2020</td>\n<td>4/27/2020</td>\n<td>7679</td>\n<td>8869</td>\n<td>-1190</td>\n<td>-15.50%</td>\n<td>-3.25%</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BTC</td>\n<td>Closed</td>\n<td>long</td>\n<td>7/28/2020</td>\n<td>6/28/2020</td>\n<td>9048</td>\n<td>11017</td>\n<td>1969</td>\n<td>21.80%</td>\n<td>-2.18%</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n</figure>\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n<h2>Discussion</h2>\n<p>As we can see the Trading Strategy ended with<strong> -2.18% P/L</strong> without taking into account the transaction fees. You can see also that in some periods was profitable (2018) and in some other periods was not (2019), that is why is very important to backtest a trading strategy for many different periods. </p>\n<p>Notice that with this function we can backtest hundred of backtest strategies with some small tweaks. Instead of this simple trading strategy could be an advanced machine learning model. Once we define the trading signals, then the backtest is the same.</p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = d.createElement(t);\n            s.type = 'text/javascript';\n            s.async = true;\n\t\t\t// s.defer = true;\n//          s.src = '//cdn.viglink.com/api/vglnk.js'; \n\t\t\ts.src = 'https://www.r-bloggers.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/vglnk.js';\n        var r = d.getElementsByTagName(t)[0];\n            r.parentNode.insertBefore(s, r);\n    }(document, 'script'));\n</script>\n\t\t\n<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 13px;\">\n<div style=\"text-align: rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p><p>The post <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/how-to-backtest-your-crypto-trading-strategies-in-r/\" target=\"_blank\">How to Backtest your Crypto Trading Strategies in R</a> first appeared on <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" target=\"_blank\">R-bloggers</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/SkUV_hJpQYc\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 04 Sep 2020 10:29:17 +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"a53c6e16ccf7f1e09d04b3a2531d3712","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"George Pipis","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/SkUV_hJpQYc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn13@2020_5/2020/09/05/06-27-12-152_1db1b6cba39f562b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287179948},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"State of R packages in your library","link":"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/\"> Posts on R-hub blog</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>Ever wondered where packages in general and their code in particular go when you run something like <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/utils/install.packages.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>install.packages()</code></a>? This post is for you!</p>\n<h2 id=\"where-do-installed-packages-live\">Where do installed packages live?</h2>\n<p>Packages are installed</p>\n<ul>\n<li>\n<p>at the path you give as <code>lib</code> argument to <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/utils/install.packages.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>install.packages()</code></a> (or to any <code>remotes::install_</code> function, that will pass them on to <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/utils/install.packages.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>install.packages()</code></a>);</p>\n</li>\n<li>\n<p>most often since you won’t give any, at the first path returned by <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/libPaths.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>.libPaths()</code></a> that exists and for which the user has the right permissions. There are <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/a/31707983/5489251\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">several ways to change the paths returned, should you want to do so</a>.<sup id=\"fnref:1\"><a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/#fn:1\" class=\"footnote-ref\" role=\"doc-noteref\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">1</a></sup></p>\n</li>\n</ul>\n<p>Now at library loading, the important argument is called <code>lib.loc</code>, not <code>lib</code>.</p>\n<blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n<p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">Random <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hashtag/RStats?src=hash&ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">#RStats</a> \"This drives me mad\":</p>\n<p>install.packages( lib = )</p>\n<p>library( lib.loc = )</p>\n<p>— Colin Fay <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f918.png\" alt=\"🤘\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> (@_ColinFay) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/_ColinFay/status/1275366793423523842?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">June 23, 2020</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><script async src=\"https://platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script></p>\n<p>Furthermore, tweaking this argument is best avoided: e.g. if you only use a package via <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/ns-dblcolon.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>::</code></a>, but do not import it via the namespace, then when <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/ns-dblcolon.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>::</code></a> runs R will not search in <code>mylib</code>.</p>\n<p>Now how do you know where any of your installed packages was installed? You can use <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/find.package.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>find.package()</code></a> and <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/find.package.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>path.package()</code></a>!</p>\n<p>To check whether a package is installed, it is better to use <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/find.package.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>find.package()</code></a> than <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/utils/installed.packages.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>installed.packages()</code></a> because the latter, as its docs state, can be slow on some systems. In both cases, it does not mean the package is usable, for that you’d need to use <code>library()</code> or <code>require()</code>.</p>\n<h2 id=\"what-files-are-stored-locally\">What files are stored locally?</h2>\n<p>The <a href=\"https://r-pkgs.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R packages book by Hadley Wickham and Jenny Bryan</a> has <a href=\"https://r-pkgs.org/package-structure-state.html#binary-package\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">a very neat chapter called “Package structure and state”, including an explanation of the binary state</a>. It says <em>“There are no .R files in the R/ directory – instead there are three files that store the parsed functions in an efficient file format. This is basically the result of loading all the R code and then saving the functions with save(). (In the process, this adds a little extra metadata to make things as fast as possible).\"</em></p>\n<p>The installed packages in the library do not contain the original R files, see <a href=\"https://github.com/tidyverse/ggplot2/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">ggplot2 source code</a> and ggplot2 on my disk</p>\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class='chroma'><code class='language-r' data-lang='r'><span class='k'>fs</span>::<span class='nf'><a href='http://fs.r-lib.org/reference/dir_tree.html' rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">dir_tree</a></span>(\n  <span class='nf'><a href='https://rdrr.io/r/base/file.path.html' rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">file.path</a></span>(\n    <span class='nf'><a href='https://rdrr.io/r/base/find.package.html' rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">find.package</a></span>(<span class='s'>\"ggplot2\"</span>),\n    <span class='s'>\"R\"</span>\n    )\n  )\n\n├── ggplot2\n├── ggplot2.rdb\n└── ggplot2.rdx\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n<p>Under the R folder, there are three files that don’t even have the dot R extension!</p>\n<h2 id=\"how-is-code-stored\">How is code stored?</h2>\n<p>Now, regarding the code, let’s mention two important things happening to it.</p>\n<h3 id=\"byte-compilation\">Byte compilation</h3>\n<p>Since <a href=\"https://cran.r-project.org/doc/manuals/r-release/NEWS.3.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R 3.5</a>, the code is byte-compiled by default which means it is also stored in a format easier for a machine to deal with. You can learn more about byte compilation in the <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/csgillespie/efficientR/programming.html#the-byte-compiler\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Efficient R Programming book by Colin Gillespie and Robin Lovelace</a>, and in <a href=\"https://blog.revolutionanalytics.com/2017/08/take-advantage-compiler.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">a talk by R Core Member Tomas Kalibera</a>.</p>\n<h3 id=\"original-formatting-and-comments\">Original formatting and comments?</h3>\n<p>Also, by default, note that the source code is stripped of all empty lines and comments because they are useless for code execution and take up space.<sup id=\"fnref:2\"><a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/#fn:2\" class=\"footnote-ref\" role=\"doc-noteref\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">2</a></sup></p>\n<p>It is similar to CSS, JS, HTML being minified in web development to make websites load faster. Now sometimes you might want to keep code with its comments: as an user for being able to read it locally with all its comments, as a developer for debugging or profiling (to have line numbers in parsed code refer to actual line numbers you can look up in your scripts).</p>\n<p>As an user installing packages, you need to look into the <code>keep.source.pkgs</code> option in <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/base/options.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>options()</code></a> that influences the behavior of package installation, or for a specific package you’d write <a href=\"https://rdrr.io/r/utils/install.packages.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>install.packages(\"rhub\", INSTALL_opts = \"--with-keep.source\", type = \"source\")</code></a>.<sup id=\"fnref:3\"><a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/#fn:3\" class=\"footnote-ref\" role=\"doc-noteref\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">3</a></sup> If you use Windows or Mac and don’t write <code>type = \"source\"</code>, binaries might be use in which case the <code>keep.source.pkgs</code> option is ignored.</p>\n<p>As a developer working interactively on a package (with e.g. <a href=\"https://devtools.r-lib.org//reference/load_all.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>devtools::load_all()</code></a>), you need to make sure the source is kept as is when loading the package, and when loading it (lucky you, the relevant <code>keep.source</code> option is <code>TRUE</code> by default in interactive sessions <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f389.png\" alt=\"🎉\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" />).</p>\n<p>As a developer you might also encounter the case where <code>R CMD check</code> will tell you about another switch, in an environment variable. It is a switch related to package installation, since <code>R CMD check</code> will install your package for checking it . <a href=\"https://github.com/wch/r-source/blob/f27cbf1a52a31cd9b9676340394946a22041a4ae/src/library/tools/R/check.R#L5248-L5253\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">See the lines below from the R source mirror</a>:</p>\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"chroma\"><code class=\"language-r\" data-lang=\"r\">                        <span class=\"nf\">wrapLog</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"s\">\"Information on the location(s)\"</span><span class=\"p\">,</span>\n                                <span class=\"s\">\"of code generating the\"</span><span class=\"p\">,</span>\n                                <span class=\"nf\">paste0</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"nf\">sQuote</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"s\">\"Note\"</span><span class=\"p\">),</span> <span class=\"s\">\"s\"</span><span class=\"p\">),</span>\n                                <span class=\"s\">\"can be obtained by re-running with\"</span><span class=\"p\">,</span>\n                                <span class=\"s\">\"environment variable R_KEEP_PKG_SOURCE\"</span><span class=\"p\">,</span>\n                                <span class=\"s\">\"set to &#39;yes&#39;.\\n\"</span><span class=\"p\">)</span>\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n<p>Also note that there is also a way for package maintainers to <a href=\"https://stat.ethz.ch/pipermail/r-devel/2011-April/060410.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">force the installation of their package to keep the source</a>. Here are <a href=\"https://github.com/search?q=keepsource+user%3Acran+filename%3ADESCRIPTION&type=Code&ref=advsearch&l=&l=\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">packages that do that</a>. A potential use case might be to try and hire people <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/international\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">like the web development team at The Guardian seems to do if you view the source of its website</a>.</p>\n<p>As a summary: for keeping the source when loading code, in particular for a package with <a href=\"https://devtools.r-lib.org//reference/load_all.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>devtools::load_all()</code></a>, there is the <code>keep.source</code> option. For keeping the source of a package at installation you need to use the <code>keep.source.pkgs</code> option (<code>R CMD install --with-keep.source</code>) or the <code>R_KEEP_PKG_SOURCE</code> environment variable or to be installing a package that forces the source keeping.</p>\n<h2 id=\"conclusion\">Conclusion</h2>\n<p>In this post we summarized where packages live once installed, in what format, and how their code is processed at installation. An important aspect was the original code formatting and commenting being removed by default, unless one changes some options for installing packages. Do you use any of options related to keeping source in your R usage and development? How do you <a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2019/05/14/read-the-source/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">read source code</a>?</p>\n<section class=\"footnotes\" role=\"doc-endnotes\">\n<hr>\n<ol>\n<li id=\"fn:1\" role=\"doc-endnote\">\n<p>If your wish is to isolate packages you are installing for a given project, you might find a better workflow by using Docker or <a href=\"https://rstudio.github.io/renv/index.html\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">the <code>renv</code> package</a>. <a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/#fnref:1\" viewing source code you might get a better default experience by <a href=\"https://github.com/jimhester/lookup#default-printing\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">loading <code>lookup</code></a> in your <a href=\"https://rstats.wtf/r-startup.html#rprofile\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">.Rprofile</a> <strong>if you don’t use Windows, because if you use Windows and do this you won’t be able to update the loaded packages with compiled code</strong>. <a href=\"https://blog.r-hub.io/2020/09/03/keep.source/#fnref:3\" class=\"footnote-backref\" role=\"doc-backlink\" rel=\"nofollow\" />︎</a></p>\n</li>\n</ol>\n</section>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = d.createElement(t);\n 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":1736,"bgimg":"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f918.png","linkMd5":"e124f0bc23fddaa88fcd6801cfded734","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn35@2020_5/2020/09/05/06-27-09-480_59c69b8f6caa42ec.webp","destWidth":72,"destHeight":72,"sourceBytes":603,"destBytes":1212,"author":"Posts on R-hub blog","enclosureType":"","enclosureUrl":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f918.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn35@2020_5/2020/09/05/06-27-09-480_59c69b8f6caa42ec.webp","https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/1f389.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn52@2020_1/2020/09/05/06-27-19-085_41448c516a847944.webp","https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/13.0.0/72x72/21a9.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn25@2020_5/2020/09/05/06-27-14-739_25686225908fe44d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/QbJQkEQGubU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn12@2020_6/2020/09/05/06-27-18-253_6cbb363b950f4c46.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599287180047},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"R treasures: modifyList()","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/RBloggers/~3/3n7gZyzSaEk/","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://petermeissner.de/blog/2020/09/03/utils-treasures-moifyList/\"> petermeissner</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<p>Sometimes, more precisely quite often, the standard libraries hold treasures that we are not aware of.<br />\nMaybe they have obscure names, have been reinvented and shadowed by the newest cutting edge convenience package or one simply does not expect those treasures to be there so they are hidden in plain sight.</p>\n<p>The <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">modifyList()</code> function is one of those treasures. Its part of the {utils} packages so it ships with every R version ready to use without further dependencies.</p>\n<p>The function offers a way to merge two lists into one similar to <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">c()</code>, the concatenate function.<br />\nBut other than with using <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">c()</code> items with the same keys will be updated instead of simply added.<br />\nThus <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">modifyList()</code> presents the answer to the question:</p>\n<p><strong>In R, how to update items of a list with the values of another list?</strong></p>\n<p>Let’s first create two lists that will serve as example.</p>\n<div class=\"language-r highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code><span class=\"n\">list_a</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\"><-</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"nf\">list</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">key_1</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"m\">1</span><span class=\"o\">:</span><span class=\"m\">3</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"n\">list_a</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_1</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] 1 2 3</span><span class=\"w\">\n\n\n</span><span class=\"n\">list_b</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\"><-</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"nf\">list</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">key_1</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"m\">7</span><span class=\"o\">:</span><span class=\"m\">9</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">key_2</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"s2\">\"my_string\"</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"n\">list_b</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_1</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] 7 8 9</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## </span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_2</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] \"my_string\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Now we can first look at what happens when using <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">c()</code> to combine the two lists.<br />\nTrue to its name, the concatenate function, combines the two lists such that every<br />\nitem is part of the newly created list.</p>\n<div class=\"language-r highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code><span class=\"nf\">c</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">list_a</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">list_b</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_1</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] 1 2 3</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## </span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_1</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] 7 8 9</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## </span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_2</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] \"my_string\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Now let’s use <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">modifyList()</code> on our example data. Instead of three items we now only get two.<br />\nwhile the second item of <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">list_b</code> has simply been added to the new list, the first item<br />\nof <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">list_a</code> has been updated (modified) with the value of the item in <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">list_b</code>.</p>\n<div class=\"language-r highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code><span class=\"n\">modifyList</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">list_a</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">list_b</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_1</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] 7 8 9</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## </span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $key_2</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] \"my_string\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><strong>Caveats</strong></p>\n<p>There are some caveats to note however. First, let us have a look at the function<br />\ndocumentation:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>A modified version of x, with the modifications determined as follows (here, list elements are identified by their names). Elements in val which are missing from x are added to x. For elements that are common to both but are not both lists themselves, the component in x is replaced (or possibly deleted, depending on the value of keep.null) by the one in val. For common elements that are in both lists, x[[name]] is replaced by modifyList(x[[name]], val[[name]])</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The documentation basically describes the behavior we have already observed in our<br />\nexample but it also mentions the <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">keep.null</code> parameter that per default is set<br />\nto <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">FALSE</code>. This parameter emulates the behavior we are used to when deleting items<br />\nfrom a list by assigning <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">NULL</code> to that item, e.g.: <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">list['key_1'] <- NULL</code>.<br />\nIf we want to update a list with another list that has embedded <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">NULL</code> values<br />\nthat we want to keep, the deletion of those items might come unexpected<br />\n(on the other hand the value of a none existent item will always be NULL anyways, e.g.:<br />\n<code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">list()$a</code>, <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">list()[['a']]</code>).</p>\n<p>The second caveat is that <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">modifyList()</code> will not update unnamed items of a list.<br />\nThis makes sense if you think of <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">modifyList()</code> as unnamed having no name to match<br />\nvalues upon. Again, its mostly just a good idea to know, that’s that the way the<br />\nfunction works.</p>\n<p><strong>Use Cases</strong></p>\n<p>Now what is this useful for? I myself often use <code class=\"highlighter-rouge\">modifyList()</code> when I want to<br />\npass around whole sets of information which</p>\n<p>(1) which have a default set of values and<br />\n(2) might get extended in the future.</p>\n<p>That way I can always add options while older versions of options will still<br />\nwork by extending the default options and later on updating the default values<br />\nwith those values I specifiacally want to differ from the defaults.</p>\n<div class=\"language-r highlighter-rouge\">\n<div class=\"highlight\">\n<pre class=\"highlight\"><code><span class=\"n\">options_default</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\"><-</span><span class=\"w\"> \n    </span><span class=\"nf\">list</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"w\">\n        </span><span class=\"n\">plots</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"kc\">TRUE</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\">\n        </span><span class=\"n\">font_face</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"s2\">\"Comic Sans\"</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\">\n        </span><span class=\"n\">author</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"s2\">\"No One In Particular\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n    </span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n\n\n</span><span class=\"n\">options_patch</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\"><-</span><span class=\"w\"> \n    </span><span class=\"nf\">list</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"w\">\n        </span><span class=\"n\">author</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\">=</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"s2\">\"Me MySelf And I\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n    </span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n    \n</span><span class=\"n\">options_to_use</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"o\"><-</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">modifyList</span><span class=\"p\">(</span><span class=\"n\">options_default</span><span class=\"p\">,</span><span class=\"w\"> </span><span class=\"n\">options_patch</span><span class=\"p\">)</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"n\">options_to_use</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $plots</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] TRUE</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## </span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $font_face</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] \"Comic Sans\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## </span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## $author</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span><span class=\"c1\">## [1] \"Me MySelf And I\"</span><span class=\"w\">\n</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n</div>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = d.createElement(t);\n            s.type = 'text/javascript';\n            s.async = true;\n\t\t\t// s.defer = true;\n//          s.src = '//cdn.viglink.com/api/vglnk.js'; \n\t\t\ts.src = 'https://www.r-bloggers.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/vglnk.js';\n        var r = d.getElementsByTagName(t)[0];\n            r.parentNode.insertBefore(s, r);\n    }(document, 'script'));\n</script>\n\t\t\n<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; target=\"_blank\">R-bloggers</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/RBloggers/~4/3n7gZyzSaEk\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 02 Sep 2020 23:00:00 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"Data Visualization in R with ggplot2: A Beginner Tutorial","link":"https://www.dataquest.io/?p=25651","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://www.dataquest.io/blog/data-visualization-in-r-with-ggplot2-a-beginner-tutorial/\"> R tutorial – Dataquest</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\">R-bloggers</a>].  (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"5\" data-line-start=\"4\">A famous general is thought to have said, “A good sketch is better than a long speech.” That advice may have come from the battlefield, but it’s applicable in lots of other areas — including data science. “Sketching” out our data by visualizing it using ggplot2 in R is more impactful than simply describing the trends we find.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper tve_image_caption\" data-css=\"tve-u-1744f259320\"><span class=\"tve_image_frame\"><a href=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch.jpeg?resize=450%2C1253&#038;ssl=1?utm_source=rbloggers&utm_medium=referral&utm_campaign=affiliate\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><img class=\"tve_image wp-image-25658\" alt=\"ggplot2-sketch\" data-id=\"25658\" width=\"450\" data-init-width=\"450\" data-init-height=\"1253\" title=\"ggplot2-sketch\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch.jpeg?resize=450%2C1253&#038;ssl=1\" data-width=\"450\" data-height=\"441\" data-link-wrap=\"true\" style=\"\" data-css=\"tve-u-1744f2599ec\" srcset_temp=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch.jpeg?resize=450%2C1253&#038;ssl=1 1880w, https://www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch-300x200.jpeg 300w, https://www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch-1024x682.jpeg 1024w, https://www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch-768x512.jpeg 768w, https://www.dataquest.io/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ggplot2-sketch-1536x1024.jpeg 1536w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 661px) 100vw, 661px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></span></p>\n<p class=\"wp-caption-text thrv-inline-text\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><em>Sketching out the design for a house communicates much more clearly than trying to describe it with words. The same thing is often true for data — and that’s where data visualization with ggplot2 comes in!</em></p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element tve-froala fr-box fr-basic\">\n<p data-line-end=\"7\" data-line-start=\"6\">This is why we <em>visualize</em> data. We visualize data because <strong>it’s easier to learn from something that we can see rather than read</strong>. And thankfully for data analysts and data scientists who use R, there’s a <a href=\"https://www.tidyverse.org/packages/\" class=\"tve-froala\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">tidyverse package</a> called <a href=\"https://ggplot2.tidyverse.org/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">ggplot2</a> that makes data visualization a snap!</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"7\" data-line-start=\"6\">In this blog post, we’ll learn how to take some data and produce a visualization using R. To work through it, it’s best if you already have an understanding of <a href=\"https://www.dataquest.io/course/introduction-to-data-analysis-in-r/?utm_source=rbloggers&utm_medium=referral&utm_campaign=affiliate\" class=\"tve-froala\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">R programming syntax</a>, but you don’t need to be an expert or have any prior experience working with ggplot2.</p>\n<h2 class=\"\" data-line-end=\"11\" data-line-start=\"10\">Introducing the Data</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"13\" data-line-start=\"12\">The <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/index.htm\" class=\"tve-froala fr-basic\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">National Center for Health Statistics</a> has been tracking United States mortality trends since 1900. They’ve compiled <a href=\"https://data.cdc.gov/NCHS/NCHS-Death-rates-and-life-expectancy-at-birth/w9j2-ggv5\" class=\"tve-froala fr-basic\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">data</a> on life expectancy and death rate of United States citizens.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"15\" data-line-start=\"14\">We would like to know how life expectancy has been changing through time. With advances in medicine and technology, we would expect that life expectancy would be increasing, but we won’t know for sure until we have a look!</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"17\" data-line-start=\"16\">If you’d like to reproduce the graphs we’ll create in this blog post, download the data set <a href=\"https://data.world/dataquest/life-expectancy-data\" class=\"tve-froala fr-basic\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">here</a> and follow along! </p>\n<p data-line-end=\"17\" data-line-start=\"16\">(Not sure how you can work with R on your personal computer? Check out <a href=\"https://www.dataquest.io/blog/tutorial-getting-started-with-r-and-rstudio/?utm_source=rbloggers&utm_medium=referral&utm_campaign=affiliate\" class=\"tve-froala\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">how to get started with RStudio</a>!)</p>\n<h2 class=\"\" data-line-end=\"19\" data-line-start=\"18\">What’s in a Graph?</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"21\" data-line-start=\"20\">Before we dive into the post, some context is needed. There are many types of visualizations out there, but most of them will boil down to the following:</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"23\" data-line-start=\"22\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/complete_line_graph.svg\" alt=\"line chart\"></p>\n<p data-line-end=\"25\" data-line-start=\"24\">We can break down this plot into its fundamental building blocks:</p>\n<ol class=\"\">\n<li data-line-end=\"28\" data-line-start=\"26\">The data used to create the plot:</li>\n</ol>\n<p data-line-end=\"29\" data-line-start=\"28\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/data.svg\" alt=\"line chart\"></p>\n<ol start=\"2\" class=\"\">\n<li data-line-end=\"32\" data-line-start=\"30\">The axes of the plot:</li>\n</ol>\n<p data-line-end=\"33\" data-line-start=\"32\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/coord_sys.svg\" alt=\"line chart\"></p>\n<ol start=\"3\" class=\"\">\n<li data-line-end=\"36\" data-line-start=\"34\">The geometric shapes used to visualize the data. In this case, a line:</li>\n</ol>\n<p data-line-end=\"37\" data-line-start=\"36\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/geoms.svg\" alt=\"line chart\"></p>\n<ol start=\"4\" class=\"\">\n<li data-line-end=\"40\" data-line-start=\"38\">The labels or annotations that will help a reader understand the plot:</li>\n</ol>\n<p data-line-end=\"41\" data-line-start=\"40\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/annotations.svg\" alt=\"line chart\"></p>\n<p data-line-end=\"43\" data-line-start=\"42\">Breaking down a plot into <strong>layers</strong> is important because it is how the <a href=\"https://ggplot2.tidyverse.org/index.html\" class=\"tve-froala\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"><code>ggplot2</code> package</a> understands and builds a plot. The <code>ggplot2</code> package is one of the packages in the <code>tidyverse</code>, and it is responsible for visualization. As you continue reading through the post, keep these layers in mind.</p>\n<h2 class=\"\" data-line-end=\"45\" data-line-start=\"44\">Importing the Data</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"47\" data-line-start=\"46\">In order to start on the visualization, we need to get the data into our workspace. We’ll bring in the <code>tidyverse</code> packages and use the <code>read_csv()</code> function to import the data. We have our data named as <code>life_expec.csv</code>, so you’ll need to rename it according to how you name the file.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">library<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>tidyverse<span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span>\nlife_expec <span class=\"token operator\"><-</span> read_csv<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span><span class=\"token string\">\"life_expec.csv\"</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"54\" data-line-start=\"53\">Let’s see what data we’re working with:</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre><code>colnames(life_expec)\n[1] \"Year\"    \"Race\"        “Sex\"         \"Avg_Life_Expec\"    \"Age_Adj_Death_Rate\"\n</code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"61\" data-line-start=\"60\">We can see that time is encoded in terms of years via the <code>Year</code> column. There are two columns that allow us to distinguish between different race and sex categories. Finally, the last two columns correspond to life expectancy and death rate.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"63\" data-line-start=\"62\">Let’s have a quick look at the data to see how it looks like for one particular year:</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">life_expec <span class=\"token percent-operator operator\">%>%</span>\n  filter<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>Year <span class=\"token operator\">==</span> <span class=\"token number\">2000</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"70\" data-line-start=\"69\">For the year 2000, there are nine data points:</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre><code>## # A tibble: 9 x 5\n##    Year Race      Sex        Avg_Life_Expec Age_Adj_Death_Rate\n##   <dbl> <chr>     <chr>               <dbl>              <dbl>\n## 1  2000 All Races Both Sexes           76.8               869\n## 2  2000 All Races Female               79.7               731.\n## 3  2000 All Races Male                 74.3              1054.\n## 4  2000 Black     Both Sexes           71.8              1121.\n## 5  2000 Black     Female               75.1               928.\n## 6  2000 Black     Male                 68.2              1404.\n## 7  2000 White     Both Sexes           77.3               850.\n## 8  2000 White     Female               79.9               715.\n## 9  2000 White     Male                 74.7              1029.\n</dbl></dbl></chr></chr></dbl></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"85\" data-line-start=\"84\">One year has nine different rows, each one corresponding to a different demographic division. For this visualization, we’ll focus on the United States overall, so we’ll need to filter the data down accordingly:</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">life_expec <span class=\"token operator\"><-</span> life_expec <span class=\"token percent-operator operator\">%>%</span>\n  filter<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>Race <span class=\"token operator\">==</span> <span class=\"token string\">\"All Races\"</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">,</span> Sex <span class=\"token operator\">==</span> <span class=\"token string\">\"Both Sexes\"</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"92\" data-line-start=\"91\">The data is in a good place, so we can pipe it into a <code>ggplot()</code> function to begin creating a graph. We use the <code>ggplot()</code> function to indicate that we want to create a plot.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">life_expec <span class=\"token percent-operator operator\">%>%</span>\n  ggplot<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"99\" data-line-start=\"98\">This code produces a blank graph (as we see below). But it now “knows” to use the <code>life_expec</code> data, even though we don’t see it charted yet.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"101\" data-line-start=\"100\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/ggplot_1.svg\" alt=\"ggplot_1\"></p>\n<h2 data-line-end=\"103\" data-line-start=\"102\" class=\"\">Building the Axes</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"105\" data-line-start=\"104\">Now that we’ve prepared the data, we can start building our visualization. The next layer that we need to establish are the axes. We are interested in looking at how life expectancy changes with time, so this indicates what our two axes are: <code>Year</code> and <code>Avg_Life_Expec</code>.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"107\" data-line-start=\"106\">In order to specify the axes, we need to use the <code>aes()</code> function. <code>aes</code> is short for “aesthetic”, and it is where we tell <code>ggplot</code> what <em>columns</em> we want to use for different parts of the plot. We are trying to look at life expectancy through time, so this means that <code>Year</code> will go to the <code>x-axis</code> and <code>Avg_Life_Expec</code> will go to the y-axis.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">life_expec <span class=\"token percent-operator operator\">%>%</span>\n  ggplot<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>aes<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>x <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> Year<span class=\"token punctuation\">,</span> y <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> Avg_Life_Expec<span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"114\" data-line-start=\"113\">With the addition of the <code>aes()</code> function, the graph now knows what columns to attribute to the axes:</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"116\" data-line-start=\"115\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/ggplot_2.svg\" alt=\"plot_example\"></p>\n<p data-line-end=\"118\" data-line-start=\"117\">But notice that there’s still nothing on the plot! We still need to tell <code>ggplot()</code> what kind of shapes to use to visualize the relationships between <code>Year</code> and <code>Avg_Life_Expec</code>.</p>\n<h2 data-line-end=\"120\" data-line-start=\"119\" class=\"\">Specifying Geoms</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"122\" data-line-start=\"121\">Typically when we think of visualizations, we normally think about the type of graph since it’s really the shapes that we see that tell us most of the information. While the <code>ggplot2</code> package gives us a lot of flexibility in terms of choosing a shape to draw the data, it’s worth taking some time to consider which one is <em>best</em> for our question.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"124\" data-line-start=\"123\">We are trying to visualize how life expectancy has changed through time. This means that there should be a way for us to compare the past directly with the future. In other words, we want a shape that helps show a relationship between two consecutive years. For this, a line graph is great.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"126\" data-line-start=\"125\">To create a line graph with <code>ggplot()</code>, we use the <code>geom_line()</code> function. A <code>geom</code> is the name for the specific shape that we want to use to visualize the data. All of the functions that are used to draw these shapes have <code>geom</code> in front of them. <code>geom_line()</code> creates a line graph, <code>geom_point()</code> creates a scatter plot, and so on.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">life_expec <span class=\"token percent-operator operator\">%>%</span>\n  ggplot<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>aes<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>x <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> Year<span class=\"token punctuation\">,</span> y <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> Avg_Life_Expec<span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span> <span class=\"token operator\">+</span>\n  geom_line<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element\">\n<p data-line-end=\"134\" data-line-start=\"133\">Notice how after the use of the <code>ggplot()</code> function, we start to add more layers to it using a <code>+</code> sign. This is important to note because we use <code>%>%</code> to tell <code>ggplot()</code> what data to function. <em>After</em> using <code>ggplot()</code>, we use <code>+</code> to add more layers to the plot.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"136\" data-line-start=\"135\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/ggplot_4.svg\" alt=\"plot_example\"></p>\n<p data-line-end=\"138\" data-line-start=\"137\">This graph is exactly what we were looking for! Having a look at the general trend, life expectancy has grown over time.</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"140\" data-line-start=\"139\">We could stop the plot here if we were just looking at the data quickly, but this is rarely the case. More common is that you’ll be creating a visualization for a report or for others on your team. In this case, the plot is not complete: if we were to give it to a teammate with no context, they wouldn’t understand the plot. Ideally, all of your plots should be able to explain themselves through the annotations and titles.</p>\n<h2 data-line-end=\"142\" data-line-start=\"141\" class=\"\">Adding a Title and Axis Labels</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"144\" data-line-start=\"143\">Currently the graph keeps the column names as the labels for both of the axes. This is sufficient for <code>Year</code>, but we’ll want to change up the y-axis. In order to change the axis labels for a plot, we can use the <code>labs()</code> function and add it as a layer onto the plot. <code>labs()</code> can change both the axis labels as well as the title, so we’ll incorporate that here.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_custom_html_shortcode\">\n<pre class=\" language-r\"><code class=\" language-r\">life_expec <span class=\"token percent-operator operator\">%>%</span> <span class=\"token comment\" spellcheck=\"true\"># data layer</span>\n  ggplot<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>aes<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>x <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> Year<span class=\"token punctuation\">,</span> y <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> Avg_Life_Expec<span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span> <span class=\"token operator\">+</span> <span class=\"token comment\" spellcheck=\"true\"># axes layer</span>\n  geom_line<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span> <span class=\"token operator\">+</span> <span class=\"token comment\" spellcheck=\"true\"># geom layer</span>\n  labs<span class=\"token punctuation\">(</span>  <span class=\"token comment\" spellcheck=\"true\"># annotations layer</span>\n    title <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> <span class=\"token string\">\"United States Life Expectancy: 100 Years of Change\"</span><span class=\"token punctuation\">,</span>\n    y <span class=\"token operator\">=</span> <span class=\"token string\">\"Average Life Expectancy (Years)\"</span>\n  <span class=\"token punctuation\">)</span></code></pre>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_text_element tve-froala fr-box fr-basic\">\n<p data-line-end=\"156\" data-line-start=\"155\">Our final polished graph is:</p>\n<p data-line-end=\"158\" data-line-start=\"157\" style=\"text-align: center;\"><img src=\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/dq-content/273/ggplot_5.svg\" alt=\"plot_example\"></p>\n<h2 data-line-end=\"160\" data-line-start=\"159\" class=\"\">Conclusion: ggplot2 is Powerful!</h2>\n<p data-line-end=\"162\" data-line-start=\"161\">In only a few lines of code, we produced a great visualization that tells us everything we need to know about life expectancy for the general population in the United States. Visualization is an essential skill for all data analysts, and R makes it easy to pick up. </p>\n<p data-line-end=\"162\" data-line-start=\"161\">Check out our <a href=\"https://www.dataquest.io/path/data-analyst-r/?utm_source=rbloggers&utm_medium=referral&utm_campaign=affiliate\" class=\"tve-froala\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Data Analyst in R</a> path if you’re interested in learning more! The Data Analyst in R path includes a course on <a href=\"https://www.dataquest.io/course/r-data-viz/?utm_source=rbloggers&utm_medium=referral&utm_campaign=affiliate\" class=\"tve-froala\" style=\"outline: none;\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">data visualization in R</a> using <code>ggplot2</code>, where you’ll learn how to:</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv-styled_list\" tcb-no-delete tcb-no-save\" data-css=\"tve-u-1744f222aee\">Use histograms to understand data distributions.</span></li>\n<li class=\"thrv-styled-list-item\" data-css=\"tve-u-1744f22630e\">\n<div class=\"tcb-styled-list-icon\">\n<div class=\"thrv_wrapper thrv_icon tve_no_drag tcb-no-delete tcb-no-clone tcb-no-save tcb-icon-inherit-style tcb-local-vars-root\" data-css=\"tve-u-1744f235ac2\" -09-05 14:26:20","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 14:26:20","title":"3 Fun Shiny Apps for Your Long Labor Day Weekend","link":"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/","description":"<p class=\"syndicated-attribution\"><!-- \n<div style=\"min-height: 30px;\">\n[social4i size=\"small\" align=\"align-left\"]\n</div>\n-->\n\n<div style=\"border: 1px solid; background: none repeat scroll 0 0 #EDEDED; margin: 1px; font-size: 12px;\">\n[This article was first published on  <strong><a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/\"> RStudio Blog</a></strong>, and kindly contributed to <a (You can report issue about the content on this page <a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/contact-us/\">here</a>)\n<hr>Want to share your content on R-bloggers?<a href=\"https://www.r-bloggers.com/add-your-blog/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> click here</a> if you have a blog, or <a href=\"http://r-posts.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\"> here</a> if you don't.\n</div></p>\n<div style=\"padding: 35px 0 0 0;\">\n<p><img srcset_temp=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/bbq_huaf2e4fb4772dcd9f315c84bab7b5240e_185940_800x0_resize_q75_box.jpg, https://i1.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/bbq.jpg?w=450&#038;ssl=1 2x\" \n   src=\"https://i1.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/bbq.jpg?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" \n      alt=\"Photo of cookout\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></p>\n</div>\n<p>Photo by <a href=\"https://unsplash.com/@vincent_keiman_nl?utm_source=unsplash&utm_medium=referral&utm_content=creditCopyText\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Vincent Keiman</a> on <a href=\"https://unsplash.com/s/photos/barbeque-horizontal?utm_source=unsplash&utm_medium=referral&utm_content=creditCopyText\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Unsplash</a></p>\n<p>Many of us in the United States have a long Labor Day weekend coming up, which, contrary to its name, we usually celebrate by not doing labor. That fact leaves us facing a conundrum on the Friday before the weekend: we don’t want to start a new project that might require a lot of research or thinking, yet we really feel like we should do something before we knock off for the week.</p>\n<p>In this spirit, I’ve selected three submissions from the recent Second Annual Shiny Contest whose content is <em>NOT</em> directly related to data science or work. I feature them only to illustrate their creativity and possibly, to suggest light-hearted ways to enjoy your time off. If you’d like to make your own choices, please check out all the entries in the <a href=\"https://rpubs.com/minebocek/shiny-contest-2020-submissions\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">full listing of the 2nd Annual RStudio Shiny contest submissions</a>.</p>\n<p>So without further ado, here are our three recommendations for ways to relax with Shiny on your labor-day weekend. If you want to try out the apps for yourself, just click on the images and a live version of the app will open in a new window.</p>\n<h2 id=\"learn-to-paint-with-bob-ross\">Learn To Paint With Bob Ross</h2>\n<p><a href=\"https://karamanis.shinyapps.io/bob_ross/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"></p>\n<div style=\"padding: 35px 0 0 0;\">\n<p><img srcset_temp=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/bob-ross-app_huf2899d6f70360f0ff36bef024ea46923_35879_389x0_resize_q75_box.jpg, https://i2.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/bob-ross-app.jpg?w=389&#038;ssl=1 2x\" \n   src=\"https://i2.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/bob-ross-app.jpg?w=389&#038;ssl=1\" \n      alt=\"Bob Ross App\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></p>\n</div>\n<p></a></p>\n<p>With all the ennui of our current business and political worlds, perhaps you need a soothing and calm voice to help you learn to paint. I’m referring, of course, to Bob Ross’ <em>Joy of Painting</em> videos that appeared on the Public Broadcasting System from 1983 to 1994 and are <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/user/BobRossInc\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">now available on YouTube</a>.</p>\n<p>The Shiny app submitted by Georgios Karamanis is minimalist in appearance, but that’s part of its charm. It reads in a database of the Bob Ross episodes created by Walt Hickey of FiveThirtyEight.com and combines the painting elements being taught into a simple, hand-drawn painting. This visual synthesis allows the user to visually browse through the paintings created in each episode and decide which painting techniques they wish to learn. You then take the season and episode number from the app and it’s off to YouTube you go to be soothed, reassured, and taught that all you really need to start painting is a nice wet wash of platinum white.</p>\n<p>You can read more about this app on <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/bob-ross-painting-by-the-elements-2020-shiny-contest-submission/56922\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">its submission page at community.rstudio.com</a>, and you can learn about Bob Ross from <a href=\"http://bobross.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">his web site</a> and <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bob_Ross\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">wikipedia page</a>.</p>\n<h2 id=\"play-some-hangman\">Play Some Hangman</h2>\n<p><a href=\"https://smirnovayu.shinyapps.io/hangman_en/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"></p>\n<div style=\"padding: 35px 0 0 0;\">\n<p><img srcset_temp=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/hangman_huf2899d6f70360f0ff36bef024ea46923_52285_393x0_resize_q75_box.jpg, https://i2.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/hangman.jpg?w=393&#038;ssl=1 2x\" \n   src=\"https://i2.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/hangman.jpg?w=393&#038;ssl=1\" \n      alt=\"Hangman App\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></p>\n</div>\n<p></a></p>\n<p>I think pretty much everyone has played Hangman at one time or another. The goal is pretty simple: guess the letters that make up the target word in as few guesses as possible. Each wrong guess adds another element to the hangman figure. If you guess wrong 10 times, you’re hanged.</p>\n<p>Ten tries is quite a few, so I’m sure most readers of this blog will find themselves winning most games. Should you find yourself bored with so much winning in English, try the unadvertised <a href=\"https://smirnovayu.shinyapps.io/hangman_ru/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Russian version</a>.</p>\n<p>You can learn more about the author and the app in its <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/hangman-2020-shiny-contest-submission/54937\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">submission description</a>.</p>\n<h2 id=\"adopt-a-cat\">Adopt a Cat</h2>\n<p><a href=\"https://nsilbiger.shinyapps.io/AdoptDontShop/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"></p>\n<div style=\"padding: 35px 0 0 0;\">\n<p><img srcset_temp=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/adopt-dont-shop_huf2899d6f70360f0ff36bef024ea46923_98789_587x0_resize_q75_box.jpg, https://i2.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/adopt-dont-shop.jpg?w=450&#038;ssl=1 2x\" \n   src=\"https://i2.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/adopt-dont-shop.jpg?w=450&#038;ssl=1\" \n      alt=\"Adopt Don&#39;t Shop App\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></p>\n</div>\n<p></a></p>\n<p>You know that the internet is made of cats, right? Maybe it’s time to put away your computer and adopt one. If you live in the Los Angeles, California area, Nyssa Silbiger and Margaret Siple have you covered: you can browse the collection of the Los Angeles Kitten Rescue cats available for adoption with the Adopt Don’t Shop app.</p>\n<p>If you’re in a data sciency mood, you can examine the distribution of species and names in their appropriate tabs, but most people will want to go straight to the <em>Kitten Tinder</em> page and browse the kitties. Best of all, should you fall in love with one (and you probably will), hitting the “I want to adopt!” button will take you straight to that cat’s adoption page.</p>\n<p>Check out the <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/adopt-dont-shop-2020-shiny-contest-submission/59166\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">submission entry</a> for details on the app and its code but don’t feel you have to. Browsing adoptable cats is a perfectly acceptable Labor Day activity.</p>\n<h2 id=\"can-t-decide-practice-making-decisions\">Can’t Decide? Practice Making Decisions</h2>\n<p><a href=\"https://sparktuga.shinyapps.io/ShinyDecisions/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\"></p>\n<div style=\"padding: 35px 0 0 0;\">\n<p><img srcset_temp=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/shiny-decisions_hue9110a680068f294b26603f05e5aeb7f_671545_433x0_resize_box_2.png, https://i0.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/shiny-decisions.png?w=433&#038;ssl=1 2x\" \n   src=\"https://i0.wp.com/blog.rstudio.com/2020/09/04/3-fun-shiny-apps-for-your-long-labor-day-weekend/shiny-decisions.png?w=433&#038;ssl=1\" \n      alt=\"Shiny Decisions App\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\"/></p>\n</div>\n<p></a></p>\n<p>I know I said I’d only highlight 3 apps, but I’m adding a 4th because it’s one of those apps that captivates users. I’m speaking of one of the winners of the Shiny Contest, <a href=\"https://sparktuga.shinyapps.io/ShinyDecisions/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Shiny Decisions</a>. This app is about making the best decisions in bad situations while you try to save the world. And while I’m sure everyone remembers <em>War Games</em> computer Joshua claiming “The only winning move is not to play,” you’ll want to play this one anyway. Read the <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/shiny-decisions-card-swiping-game-2020-shiny-contest-submission/58723\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">submission entry</a> for more details or just click through on the image above to play the game immediately.</p>\n<h3 id=\"for-more-information\">For More Information</h3>\n<p>We thank all of the 183 developers who submitted the 220 apps in the 2nd Annual Shiny Contest for their hard work. We’ll be highlighting more topical selections from the Second Annual Shiny Contest applications during September 2020. In the meantime, you can <a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/07/13/winners-of-the-2nd-shiny-contest/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">read about the featured winners of that contest</a> in our July blog post.</p>\n\n<script type=\"text/javascript\">\n    var vglnk = {key: '949efb41171ac6ec1bf7f206d57e90b8'};\n    (function(d, t) {\n        var s = d.createElement(t);\n            s.type =
